THE RED PAGE.
FREDERICK LOCKER
IT is convenient to have Frederick Lockers
verses the “Golden Treasury Series”
(London Lyrics; Macmillan; 2s. o>d. ),
although in new, modern type the lines
seem to lose something of their old-fashioned
charm. One has never seen Locker look as well
as in W. D. Adams’s Songs of Society, a Pickering
book where type and page exactly met his idio-
syncrasy.
Locker (one may shed the intrusive Lampson,
as it is shed in Austin Dobson’s preface : he
is a writer who needs a new name from the
fairies) was born at Greenwich Hospital in 1821.
His father was Civil Commissioner of the Hos-
Jital: his grandfather, a naval officer, had been
deutenant-Governor. Locker gives this as his
earliest recollection:
I recollect a nurse call’d Ann,
Who carried me about the grass,
And one fine day a fine young man
Came out and kiss’d the pretty lass.,
She did not make the least objection !
Thinks I, “ Aha !
When I can talk I ’ll tell Mamma.”
And that’s my earliest recollection.
He did not attend a University, and started
business life in a “colonial broker’s” office in
Mincing Lane. Prom twenty to thirty he was
clerk at Somerset House and under the Admiralty:
then, marrying Lady Charlotte Bruce, he began
happy years of literary leisure till his death, a
decade ago. He tells the story of his wedding
engagement in My Confidences (1896):
This is how it came about. We had seated ourselves on
a bench, and neither spoke. I took her hand. “ This is
the prettiest hand in all the world,” said I. “ I happen
to know of one that’s quite as pretty,” said she. Another
silence. Perhaps I was incredulous, but when she put the
other pretty hand into mine I knew that we both were
very happy.
His wife’s family influence and affluence made
Locker’s life-path easy. A married ‘ ‘ man about
town,” he joined the city’s society of wits, and
celebrated its fashionable resorts in affectionate
verses. Lady Charlotte died in 1872, and at the
age of 53 he was married a second time : to Miss
Hannah Lampson, whose name, eleven years
later, he took in addition to his own.
The first book of London Lyrics was published
in 1857 —a handful of twenty-six pieces that
through years and editions, changes and losses,
grew to the sixty-four finally approved. Off-
shoots, outcasts and others were collected in a
volume privately’ printed, the London Rhymes of
1882. A small collection of prose essays and frag-
ments appeared as Patchwork in 1879. In his
later years Locker was an enthusiastic collector of
old books, old prints, old china ; his library at
Rowfant, Sussex, became famous. Three months
ago, to the scandal of literary England, the collec-
tion was sold privately to an American bookseller
and transported to the United States.
His long and fortunate life in the literary world
saved Frederick Locker for a space from the obli-
vion that has befallen Mortimer Collins and
Thomas Haynes Bayly : each with a vogue once
equal to his, and a merit equal at least, if dissimi-
lar. Londoners must regard Locker with peculiar
affection, but his claim on literature is slight. The
work is to be cherished rather for personal atmo-
sphere than for intrinsic value : it brings a message
from an elder generation, better-tempered, better-
bred. Its virtue is somewhat valetudinarian : the
verses will walk with you, if you come to them in
their own mood ; but they do not run to meet you.
They are cheerful, yet not gay; melancholy,
yet not sad ; satirical, yet not cynical. This is the
mood that Locker sought for ? Maybe •or was it
the mode he could manage ? Did he fix, was he
fixed by, his own limitations ? One thinks the
verse is more than usually the man. If it is less
bright than Bsuyly’S, that is because Locker was
more tender than Bayly, It is less easy than
Praed’s, but Locker did not have Praed’s practice
at Eton. One may even dream of naval discipline
in it: the epithets are conned as Locker’s grandfer
might have conned a ship, with nicest eye and
touch. Well, it is agreeable, fine ; and in its
elder age the mellow sentiment may delight us in
our own.
A good portrait is added to the volume: one
needs Du Maurier’s sketch as well. At p. 30 des
is misprinted for de.
O :
Some editors reject copy politely, and some...
don’t. Besant has just been quoted as relating
that one editor of his acquaintance used to signify
that he did n’t want a manuscript by scrawl-
ing across the front page the cabalistic letters
“ U.8.D.”
“ I would not give a farthing for a book unless I were at
liberty to blot it with niy criticisms.”—Burns.
A. E. Gunn gives this graphic description'of a
mangrove swamp in the real North (quoted by-
Searcy in In Northern Sects) .■
The place seemed to be the very heart of the huge
solitude in which we were situated. Overhead there was
a dark, closely-knitted canopy of leaves. Only here and
there a patch of ineffably blue skv, that appeared to be
immeasurably distant, gleamed through rifts in the
firmament of foliage. Through the apertures the sun
shot vertical shafts of golden light that counterfeited
gilded pillars, except where their masses were broken by
contact with the trunks and limbs of the trees.
But the lights that stole through only made the gloom
more ghostly and unreal by the contrast. It was like a
weird, uncanny underworld, a vast, shapeless vault,
whose roof was support ed by gnarled and knotted trunks,
carved with fantastic devices by the processes of Nature.
Slender flying buttresses vaulted away from the trunks in
long series of elliptical arches. The whole scheme of de-
sign of the jungle might'have been that of an unimagin-
able mediaeval cathedral, conceived in a nightmare and
executed in a delirium. It seemed to be peopled by un-
seen, silent, thinking, feeling beiim-s, capable of action ;
and the twisted and contorted boughs and branches,
stretching out hideous, mud-strained arms, that appeared
ever intent on catching and holding one in their loath-
some embraces, intensified the impression.
The atmosphere was stifling, and permeated with a hot,
miasmatic vapour. The’silence was intense, and broken
only by faint sounds of something moving forward, the
gasping of shellfish that lay in the mud or clung to the
roots and trunks of the mangroves. So still was it, one
could almost hear the mdisture exuding from the
ooze, or the sap coursing in the veins of trees...
There was no distance, no vista, no perspective, only
knotted and twisted trunks, a tangle of boughs and
branches and roots, of roots and and boughs.
Above, a roof of leaden leaves; underfoot, a slushy, noisome
ooze of decaying leaves, roots, shells, and mud.
LONDON LERICS
THE UNREALIZED IDEAL .
My only Love is always near, —
In country or in town
I see ller twinkling feet, I hear
The whisper of her gown.
She foots it ever fair and young,
Her locks are tied in haste,
And one is o’er her shoulder flung,
And hangs below her waist.
She ran before me in the meads ;
And down this world-worn track
She leads me on ; but while she leads
She never gazes back.
And yet her voice is in my dreams,
, To witch me more and more ;
That wooing voice ! Ah me, it seems
Less near me than of yore.
Lightly I sped when hope was high,
And youth beguiled the chaSe ;
I follow—follow still'; but I
Shall never see her Face.
AT HER WINDOW.
Ah, Minstrel, how strange is
The carol you sing !
Let Psyche, who ranges-
The garden of spring,
Remember the changes
December will bring.
Beating Heart ! we come again
Where my Love reposes :
This is Mabel’s window-pane ;
These are Mabel’s roses.
Is she nested ? Does she kneel
In the twilight stilly,
Lily clad from throat to heel,
She, my virgin Lily ?
Soon the wan, the wistful stars,
Fading, will forsake her ;
Elves of light, on beamy bars,
Whisper then and wake her.
Let this friendly pebble plead
At her flowery grating ;
If she hear me will she heed ?
Mabel, I am waiting.
Mabel will be deck’d anon,
Zoned in bride’s apparel;
Happy zone ! Oh hark to yon
Passion-shaken carol !
Sing thy song, thou tranced thrush
Pipe thy best, thy clearest;
Hush, her lattice moves, oh hush,
Dearest Mabel! — dearest.
ADVICE TO A POET.
Dear Poet, do not rhyme at all!
But if you must don’t tell your neighbours.
Or five in six, who cannot scrawl,
Will dub you “ donkey ” for your labours.
This epithet may seem unjust
To you, or any verse-begetter ;
Must we admit, I fear we must,
That nine in ten deserve no better ?
Then let them bray with leathern lungs.
And match you with the beast that grazes;
Or wag their heads and hold their tongues,
Or damn you with the faintest praises.
Be patient, for be sure you won t
Win vogue without extreme vexation;
And hope for sympathy,—but don’t
Expect it from a near relation.
When strangers first approved my books,
My kindred marvell’d what the praise meant;
They now wear more respectful looks,
But can’t get over their amazement.
Indeed, they’ve power to wound beyond
That wielded by the fiercest hater,
For all the time they are so fond—
Which makes the aggravation greater.
Most warblers only half express
The threadbare'thoughts they feebly utter :
Now if they tried for something less,
They might not sink, and gasp, and flutter.
Fly low at first, —then mount and win
The niche for which the town’s contesting.;
And never mind your kith and kin,—
But never give them cause for jesting.
Hold Pegasus in hand, control
A taste for ornament ensnaring :
Simplicity is yet the soul
Of all that Time deems worth the sparing.
Long lays are not a lively sport,
So clip your own to half a quarter ;
If readers now don’t think them short,
Posterity will cut them shorter,
I look on bards who whine for praise
With feelings of profoundest pity :
They hunger for the poet’s bays,
And swear one’s waspish when one’s witty.
The critic’s lot is passing hard.
Between ourselves, I think, reviewers,
When call’d to truss a crowing bard,
Should not be sparing of the skewers.
We all, the foolish and the wise,
. .Regard our verse with fascination,
Through asinine-paternal eyes,
And hues of Fancy’s own creation ;
Prythee, then, check that passing sneer
At any self-deluded rhymer
Who thinks his beer (the smallest beer !)
Has all the gust of alt Hoclihnmer.
Oh, for the Poet-Voice that swells
To lofty truths, or noble curses—
I only wear the cap and bells,
And yet some tears are in my verses.
I softly trill my sparrow reed,
Pleased if but one should like the twitter ;
Humbly I lay it down to to heed
A music or a minstrel fitter.
Man's Responsibilitji, “or How, and Why, the
Almighty Introduced Evil upon the Earth,” comes
from G. P. Putnam’s Sons, N.Y. Read with
difficulty to p. 3(5, where :
<But here, in the Conscience implanted by the Almighty
in man, we have a spectroscope which can reach beyond
the remotest star, aud, surveying infinity, can nnaivse
and search) out the essence of the Almighty Himself.. i
After that unveracious statement read no more.
There is room for dogma with the least shred of
evidence to back it, but no room for a dogma which
explains the Incomprehensibility of the ’nscru-
table without any evidence whatever.
TWO NEW BOOKS
CUNNINGHAME GRAHAM, praised as
an illuminating Scot, has published
another collection of sketches, titled Pro-
gress (Loud., Duckworth; 65.) it is a
fine series, with a general message not imagi-
native but ratiocinative, and reasoned always
in the same fashion from data that Graham
has travelled far to find. He needs Ins data
in order to write ; bis outlook does not vary ,
vet his vision is so lofty upon a landscape so
diversified that one cannot have better company.
The modernity of his literary style urges the
radical difference between this generation and a I
others preceding. English did not contain such
phrases, such contrasts and harmonies of phrases,
before the last century-end. See how a language
recreates itself, turning ever a fresh face to the
light! dt is primitive races, untravelled places,
that attract Graham. He considers civilisation
with a sardonic humour that is close to contempt;
and as The Bulletin will reach here and there a
beach-comber, one may cite this passage for
pondering :
If it is true that only simple folk shall be the real
inheritors of the earth, it may be said with equal truth
that those who fail possess it presently. whilst the
world claims a man, and he enjoys the esteem or hatred
of his fellows, he can inherit nothing-, or, at best, only
inherit property, the true primeval curse.
The praise of men, the pettiness of greatness, and the
attachment to the thousand nothings which ensure
success, so cramp a man that he is left without the
leisure to enjoy his life. Life only really is understood
either by simple men whose cares and joys are bounded
by the parish where they live, or by thos.| disillusioned
folk who look out on the world as a covy looks out on a
road, resting her head upon a gate. Your true Nirvana
can onlv he attained by those who, in the sun, the tides,
the phases of the moon, the miracle of buds and flowers,,
green leaf and then dry boughs again, find happiness, and
pass their lives in thinking without bitterness on that which
might have been. Occasionally on some lost island beach
in the Pacific, in ranches on the plains, in hulks upon the
Oil rivers, in seeming uncongenial places up and down
the world, we come across them. Sometimes, indeed,
amongst the busv haunts of men they live detached, aloof
from all around them ; but in every case their touchstone
is the apparent failure of their lives.
That is, thev must have had some quality which put
them out of time, made them too sharp or flat, not up to
concert pitch ; in fact; unfit for excellence in the
pursuits their fellows prize, and rise to eminence
by following, becoming county councillors, generals,
and admirals of routine -- eminent, worthy, and
uninteresting dying high in the respect of those
who, born -without "appreciation, endue their heroes
with their own qualities intensified. Something of pathos
clings to those who, having left the world alone to run its
maddening course, have thus beeome, as it were, moth-
eaten gods in threadbare marriage garments. To be a
god is to be quite detached from all around, or else so
permeated with everything as to be part of it. So live
the trees, the grass, the birds and beasts, and, as a
general rule, those men from Whom alone one can expect
something out of the common ; something within them-
selves which far surpasses all accomplishment. A painter
or a poet (all but the greatest) may excel and yet be
common as a countess in Mayfair, or as a shop-girl m
the Brompton Road ; but the beach-eoomber or the man
left stranded by the -waves of life may be a drunkard,
possibly a thief, but it is hard for him to be a snob, for, if
he had been, he must inevitably have drifted to success.
The world is to the snob ; he easily outgoes the cad, the
merchant, the philosopher; the artist, poet, or what-not;
for they, if they are really true, must show themselves
such as they are, whilst the snob’s art is to appear some-
thing that fie is not.
In general, the storm-stayed prisoners of Fate pass
all their time in waiting for a wind ; but their
peculiarity is that if, after their years of wait-
ing, it should blow fair towards the port, which they
have dreamed about, making it in their vision only an
antechamber to the heaven of the Apocalypse, they would
not set their sails. Pride may be keeps them back, or a dim
feeling that they are happy where they are, or a vague
fear of the continual roar of cabs, or something which
they cannot formulate; in the same way a bird long
prisoned in a cage fears to fly out, although the floor is
open wide and not a cat in sight to make him doubtful of
the strength of his long-unaccustomed wings.
F. S. Delmer, well-known in these latitudes (he
contributed to The Bulletin, and was represented
in The Bulletin Storg Book ; had the honour of
being pedagogue in-1. B. O'Hara's school at Mel-
bourne, and brought away the belief that to be
a Victorian pedagogue; and a successful one, damns
a man... out of humanity ; that is a moderateand
impartial judgment ” ; —had the glory of being
pedagogue and so many other peda-things a.t Syd.
Grammar School, where duties are multifarious,
that his proud spirit rebelled ;--and then, being
told by a University professor that his Australian
career was at an end, shook off the dust of this
disconsolate continent and departed to Germany,
where he became a Uni. lecturer at Heidelberg,
and later at Berlin ; was appreciated and praised,
and is now doing useful work in converting'
English literature to German, and German to
English all that in one breath) has translated
the most popular of to-day s homely German
novels: Jiirn Uhl , by Gustav Frenssen (Loud.,
Constable; 2s. 6d.). The translation is so very-
well done that it has original virtue : only in the
earlier pages is there a little stiffness (30-43 e.g.).
Jorn Uhl is a good, long, honest book, with the
wholesonTe heartiness of Lorita Doone , allowing
for the German systole ; a picture of rural life and
character painted on the broad canvas of human
emotion. The matter is realistic; the manner of
German simplicity brightened by German enthu-
siasm, and not without lyrical passages and pas-
sages of wise commentary. “ Self-condensed
diligence ”, is a neat phrase for a farmer’s savings
put out at interest. One of the characters “beamed
and smiled the sort of wisdom which says to
Sorrow, ‘I will softly laugh at you,’ and to Joy
‘ I will softly weep over you.’ ’’ The ideas of a
girl who reverses the incident of one of Maitland’s
novels, and sees unexpectedly the nude figure of a
man in twilight,—a girl who until then has been
of a temperament too frigid for love, yet revolting
against herself, —are described in words full of
suggestion :
.. . like a revelation there came over her the brave,
enlightening,thought that this apparition was not there
by mere chance, but in order that she might be healed by
a return to Nature. She saw the lithe, proud, strong
frame, how as in some harmonious temple one part stood
free and strong upon the other, rising to the knees, then
growing broader in strong and youthful power to the
hips, then strong and rushing, like a cry of delight, up to
the breast and the head, which was bowed down in
thought ; and as she looked only for a moment, her
impost soul told her that here pure truth had her dwel-
ling, here, where God and Nature are housed together in
sweet, pure union. She felt that the man there was the
comrade of her innermost being, with whom, in giving
and taking, each with his especial gifts would roundoff
his own incomplete nature to a nature full and whole.
And there is a picture of fighting at Gravelotte
that rivals the similar descriptions of Tolstoy and
Crane. Altogether the book is well worth buying.
It is written in a large spirit, and opens a new
window upon the old spectacle of individual des-
tiny amid tlie universal life.

The new edition of Rossetti’s poems includes
the famous sonnet of “Nuptial Sleep” that scan-
dalised Robert Buchanan on its original appear-
ance. It was dropped from the “Collected
Works ” and now reappears : as under—
NUPTIAL SLEEP.
At length their long kiss severed, with sweet smart :
And as the last slow sudden drops kre shed
From sparkling eaves token all the storm has fled,
So singly flagged the pulses of each heart.
Their bosoms sundered, with the opening start
Of married flowers to either side outspread
From the knit stem ; yet still their mouths, burnt red,
Fawned on. each other where they lay apart.
Sleep sank them lower than the tide of dreams,
And their dreams tvatched them sink, and slid away.
Slowly their souls swam up again, through gleams
Of watered light and dull drowned waifs of day;
Till from some wonder of new ivoods and streams
He woke, c*nd wondered more : for there she lag.

More stories of Sophie Arnould :
The Due de Praslin once asked after a fille de
Vopera with whom he had been acquainted, but
whose name he had forgotten. He described her
to Sophie as well as he was able, and concluded
by saying that though lie could not remember all
the name, he knew it ended in “ain.” “Ah!
Monsieur le Due,” replied Sophie, “you must
give a more exact description than that, or you ’ll
never find her. We all have a name that ends in
‘ain.’” .
To the leader of an orchestra, who insisted upon
playing music in the tempo in which it was
written, Sophie said, “ Sir, your orchestra pre-
vents me from singing as I wish ; it puts me out.’
“Nevertheless, Mademoiselle, we were keeping
the time.” “Quelle bete est-ce celh? Follow
me, if you please, and learn that your accom-
paniment is nothing more than the servant of the
actress who is reciting.”
Upon Mile. Daguerre the Due de Bouillon spent
800,000 francs in three months. During her ill-
ness, someone asked Sophie if Mile. Laguerre was
better. “ Poor girl, she is still very ill,” replied
Sophie, “and lives on nothing but bouillon.”
One day Sophie met a money-lender, famous for
the ruin'that followed in his path. He was
carrying a splendid muff. “ How can he have
cold hands,’ she exclaimed,” when he has them
always in our pockets?
Produced in Sophie’s day, the pifece of William
Tell turned out a rank failure. “ Well,” said she,
“ proverbs go by contraries. Here we have plenty
of Swiss and no money.” m ■
John Davidson, in Bond. Outlook :
The more I think of Byron, the more clear it becomes
to me that he is, first, second, and third, a tragic figure.
He was the child of a loveless marriage, that constant
source of huge armies of discordant natures. His up-
bringing was tragic ; his marriage was tragic ; his loves
were tragic ; his death, which at first I thought only
tragic-force, is actual tragedy. Byron and Napoleon,
contemporaries, Were the analogues and complements of
each other. Byron is the passive tragedy of the imagina-
tive temperament as poet, using expression ; Napoleon is
the active tragedy of the imaginative temperament as
warrior and world-eonipeller, employing deeds. Byron
inevitably ends in an abortive attempt at action in
Greece ; Napoleon, as inevitably, in an abortive attempt
at expression (ttie dictated memoirs) in St. Helena.
A personal poem, by Arthur Adams : “A
Memory”:
In these slack days, when larger memories faded sink,
One placid hour of happiness swims into view :
Of that day’s country ramble, and its end, I think,
When we together supped—l too tired aught to do
But eat and drink and leaning, watch you eat and drink,
With the warm thought behind—after the coffee, You 1
All, these poets !
A.G.S.

A VIVID ACCOUNT
FOR YOUR BENEFIT.
The Case of Mr. S. SHAW.
(By a Sydney Reporter.)
A vivid account appears below of
the extraordinary experiences of Mr.
Samuel Shaw, of Mo. 208 Liverpool-
street, Sydney, where he has been liv-
ing1 for the past fifteen years.
“It is over half a century ago since
I came from Manchester to this coun-
try," Mr. Shaw observed to a report
or, “and up to a certain period I had
nothing to complain of about my
health. Then my luck changed, and for
fully ten years I had an annual visit
from a complaint that seemed to have
got hold of me for good."
“What was the nature of it ?’’ en-
quired the pressman.
“It was neuralgia that paid me
those yearly attentions, hut in addi-
tion to that I was greatly troubled
with indigestion. Indeed. 1 blame the
latter for causing the former, as it
was when I became unable to digest
my food that I first noticed how sad-
ly my general health was falling off.
My system seemed to go to pieces bit
by bit, and then my nerves had just
about broken down altogether, when
my physical tortures/ were at their
worsit. The pains in my head were so
terribly severe that the softest pillow
felt as if it were filled with sharp
edged stones that cut right into me.
All about my jaws and gums I was
so sore and tender that I could not
bear anything to touch me, and 1
would flinch from the application of
a piece of wadding as though it was
a hard, rough substanco of a pene-
trating character/'
“I suppose your sleep would be
greatly interfered with ?’’
“Oh ! don't mention anything about
sleep, for i positively dread to recall
the numberless nights that T put in
without getting, any at all -simply
turning from side to side in suen
agony that often I wished myself dead
instead of existing in such misery.
Sometimes the pains would late one
side of my head and face, and on other
occasions the opposite side would be
al’ected. My eyes, round the back of
my neck —goodness ! 1 don’t know
where the pain did not exist. It was
awful, and you can easily imagine how
miu h suffering can ma: e one’s life a
burden to him. I know mine was, for
1 was dreadfully melancholy. There
was always some fearful thing going
to happen, according to my mined
Trouble in every direction. and the
strange part of it was tint. although
the calamit.es did not okc.tr when 1
imagined they would, still I cou d not
help worrying over the same notions
on subsequent days. That will show
you what an unhappy frame of mind I
found myself in owing to my excessive
weakness, and it was surprising how
irritable the least thing used to make
me. The slightest noise coming sud-
denly threw me into a state of excite-
ment and caused my nerves to- shake
quite visibly, and if 1 exerted myself
a little bit the trembling was just as
bad. The trouble was that the longer
my sufferings continued the worse
they became. My organs of digestion
were getting more incapable of doing
their work every day, and although,
owing to the poorness of my appetite,
J. took but very little food, still it
was always enough to greatly increase
the torments 1 was already enduring."
“You had made efforts’to overcome
those afflictions, i expect ?"
“Repeatedly .1 had tried, but it all
seemed useless. Years ago 1 had
consulted a doctor in Victoria, and
again in Svdnev I had placed
myself under the care of seve-
ral medical experts, who could not
cure me, and naturally 1 thought
ray load of suffering would eventually
drag me down to the grave. My at-
tempts to get better did not cease
when the doctors’ medicines failed.
Mo ; I tried many others, only to be
again doomed to disappointment, for
the heavy foelings in the chest were
still there after taking a little food,
the annoying pains between my shoul-
ders had not moved an inch, and the
aching sensations across toy loiris
were just as troublesome as ever. 1
still got up with a coated tongue and
a nasty tasite of a morning, the sud-
den giddy turns had not yet been
checked, and the appalling sense of
utter feebleness and dejection was
bearing down upon me more forcibly
and more alarmingly than ever. One
thing was certairv—that a change one
way or the other would soon take
place, and it was a decided change for
the better that came, and the cause
of it all was nothing but Clements
Tonic, and that is the reason why 1
have since recommended that remedy
to hundreds of other people, who have
always thanked me for my advice af-
terwards, Do you know that I had
quite given up hope of ever being
cured, and had lost all faith in medi-
cines because of having tried every
one of them without any good result-
ing, and would never have ta' en
Clements'Tonic only that 1 happened
to pick up an old paper and saw in
it the names of some influential peo-
ple, friends of mine, who wore testify-
ing to its efficacy. _ That day 1 got
some Clements Tome, and the very
hrst night J obtained a little ease." ’
"Mow long ago is that '!"
"Tight years or more, and I am in-
deed grateful to be able to say that
ever since my health has been all that
1 could wish, for by continuing Clem-
ents Tonic lor a while 1 succeeded in
throwing oil all the ailments that had
made me thoroughly sick and tired
of my life. Tittle by little the neural-
gic pains that for years had nearly
driven me mad abated. it rea.ly
seemed too good to be true, but there
was no doubt about the complete
manner in which Clements Tonic cured
me. blot only did my head pains ul-
timately die away and never occur
again, 'but i gained in health ail round,
it was something new for me to fall
asleep soon after settling down in
red and not wake again till breakfast
timo, and didn’t I leel the better for
it ! Truly, it is only fair to say that
Clements Tonic made an entirely
new man of me, for in every way 1
felt dii.erent. In a few weeks I was
taking food as heartily as ever, but
what i appreciate more was being a Je
to digest all I ate. res ; Clemen ta
Tonic put my digestion riaht and no
mistake, and 1 often ihin.r whait a
wonderful medicine it must be in view
of my many previous failures with
oil er so-called remedies, jvjy doctors
told me my liver and nerves were out
of order, so 1 am sure of not makin >•
a mistake when 1 advise my mentis to
take Clements Tonic for those com-
plaints, as it cured me thoroughly.
After all .1 had endured it was delight-
ful to again feel fresh and vigorous,
to forge ahead and find enjoyment in
everything that I came in’ contact
with. So different from being weak
and shaky, melancholy and irritable,
like 1 had been .; but I soon had every
cause to be cheerful, for Clements
Tonic had driven away each pain and
discomfort that had clouded my life
so long. My system was splendidly
toned and strengthened throughout,
and ever since I have enjoyed good,
sound, robust health i; so you may pub-
lish tnese facts in any way it pleases
you."
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
], SAMUEL SHAW, of 208 Liverpool-
street, Sydney. In the State of New
South Wales, Commonwealth of Australia, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have
'’arefully read the annexed document, consist-
ing: of fiv* folios and consecutively numbered
from one to five, and that it contains and
is a true and faithful account of my ill-
ness and cure by Clement# Tonic, and also
contains my full permission to publish in any
way my statement#—which I give voluntarily,
without receiving any payment : and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believ-
ing the same to be true, and by virtue of the
provision# of the Oaths Act, 1900.
— 1
Subscribed and declared at Sydney this
fourth day of November, one thousand nine
hundred and four, before me,
WALTER J. GROGAN, J.P.

Ayer’s

You know the story—how good
Queen Bess, pointing to the beautiful
hair of a peasant girl, said, “ There’s a
real royal crown. I would trade my
golden one for it.” That was long ago.
Now you can have a “real royal crown”
of your own, simply by using Ayer’s
Hair Vigor. It makes the hair grow
thick and long and stops it falling

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Prayer of a Hungry Soul.
Lord, in Thy mercy, hear our prayers,
And when Thou’st finished counting hairs
And watching sparrows as they fly,
And fixing planets in the sky,
Give Man some small attention.
He fain would live in constant ease ;
He’d gladly banish all disease ;
He’d like to draw a longer breath ;
He’d like to know a cure for death,
Or, better still, prevention.
Thou gavest man the carnal wish
To feed on meat and meal and fish ;
Implanted in his soul divine
An appetite for milk and wine—
Also for beer and gladness.
Thou hast enrooted in his brain
A mortal dread of work and pain ;
He still remembers they were given
In vengeance by offended Heaven
For Eve’s and Adam’s badness
But, Lord, Thy man is so increast
That where, at one time, he might feast,
And rest and feast and rest again,
Now he can scarce, with toil and pain,
Find half enough to feed him.
So now, Lord, in these latter days
Improve Thy man and mend his ways ;
Give him more bread and beef and beer.
Or, Lord, by some short path from here
To better pastures lead him.
Endow Thy man with stronger jaws ;
Sharpen his teeth, good Lord ; because
When fruits and flesh and flowers decay
He needs must pass the time away,
And eat one thing or other.
And, when at last the world’s consumed,
And planets, suns, and space are doomed,
Then, Lord, be near him in Thy might,
And, O, reduce his appetite,
And save a lot of bother.
W.A.
TOM BEASLEY.
Autumn.
Autumn wanders in the wood,
Trailing robes of red and gold,
Shudders coursing through her blood
As she dreams of winter cold.
“Butterflies and bees,” she sings,
“ It is time to fly away.
Swallows, blackbirds, spread your wings,
Winter comes; you may not stay.
“Blushing poppies, hide your heads,
For the cruel tyrant comes,
Close behind old Winter treads,
Hear his noisy fifes and drums !
Haze nut and berry black,
Hide your overflowing store;
Winter thunders at my back,
Let us cheat the villain hoar.
Clover bloom and Cape flower, gold
Buds and blossoms all, good-bye !
Autumn wails, our days are told,
Winter comes, and we must die.
“ Skies of rose and amethyst
Don your robes of hodden grey.”
Autumn sobbed, her hands she kissed.
Smiled and sighed, and flew away !
vio.
DOROTHY FRANCES M'CRAE.
In a Slam Suburb.
In a slummy, unsweet suburb,
On a level with the sea,
Where no harbor and no ocean
Irrigate the scenery;
Where the “ City girt with Pictures ”
Is a smudge of brown and gray,
With a view of aged nail-cans
Littering the narrow way.
All the streets are drab and shabby,
And the housewives dwelling there
They are faded as to garments,
And indefinite of hair.
There the loaf is 3^d.,
And it rises day by day,
And the eggs at 12 for lOd.
Turn the worried women gray.
It’s a low-down, smelly suburb
In the pit of city life,
Where the women wither early
In the struggle and the strife—
Yet no show-place by the waters,
Where the rich and gay crowd whirls,
Boasts as sturdy-limbed a legion
As its slum-bred boys and girls.
Up at daylight from each crowded
And insanitary den,
Running messages for mothers,
Little women, little men ;
And the little women bargain
For a ha’penny to the good.
While the little men are bending
To the ragged sacks of wood.
After school it’s final “shopping,”
Then a Bedlam in the street,
Where the bursted football’s lifted
By the little naked feet.
Till the wearied, worried mothers
Call their offspring home again,
Packing, anywhere, together,
Little women, little men.
But such little men and women
Are the country’s fairest hope,
And Australia wants more like them
With its coming woes to cope.
Still the moon of glass and silver
Seems a touch of irony,
When it lights the sleeping suburb
On a level with the sea.
N.S.W.
H. C. C.

THURSDAY. JUNE 29, 1905.
AN APPEAL TO THE FEDERAL LABOR
PARTY.
A very few days hence the Labor party meets in
conference, when it will have to decide how the
next great Australian political battle is to be
fought, and if it does not take this opportunity
to re-consider its ways, many of its best friends
will begin to despair of it. There is no room in
Australian polities, nor in any country’s politico
for a party with no definite fiscal opinions, and
there is nothing to be gained by pretending any
longer that the livest issue in all Australia is
dead, or that the most buoyant issue in all
Australia is sunk. At present the Freetrade
party in Australia is the party which is fight-
ing democratic progress tooth and claw, yet
it is the party which Labor-in-politics refuses
to attack on its most vulnerable point. And
the Protectionist party is the party which
consistently holds out the hand of friendship
and assistance to Labor, yet the Federal Labor
party, in its corporate capacity, declines to aid it.
in all the frenzied campaign of lies, slanders and
misrepresentation which constitutes what is called
the anti-Socialist movement, there is not one
prominent mover, or one prominent liar, or one
conspicuous slandererer, who is not a Freetrader.
It is exclusively a Freetrade uprising, because the
I reetraders recognise, even if the Labor men do
not, that a Freetrade policy is incompatible with
short hours, minimum wage laws, arbitration
courts, and everything else which tends to make
the Australian worker a more expensive worker
than the foreigner. Therefore it is natural to find
that it is a State Government composed of the
remnants of the old Freetrade party which is
undermining the N.S.W. Arbitration Act
and whittling it away to nothing. It is
equally natural to find that the pompous
office-bearers and leaders of the anti-Socialist
league are Freetraders practically to a man. It is
a part of the recognised order of things that the
prominent opponents of the White Australia
gospel, from Bruce Smith right down to the
vacillating Dobson, are Freetraders. It is in
keeping with the general fitness of circumstances,
and the orderly procession of the universe, that
practically every Freetrade paper now alive, or
only recently dead, in Australia, from the Bris-
bane Courier, Sydney Herald and Telegraph,
and Melbourne Argus, down to Jos. Symes’ athe-
istic Liberator, is, or has been, Labor’s bitter
enemy ; while the one influential daily, the Age,
which lends the d mocratic cause a helping
is also the most strenuous Protectionist daily paper
in Australasia; and every other paper—apart from
Publications owned and run by the party—
which has battled for Labor principles and Labor
ideals is Protectionist. And it is part of the
same orderly progress of events that every poli-
tician, outside the Labor party, who has fought
tor its principles—such men as O’Sullivan, Lynk,
miggins, Trenwith, Mauger, Chanter, Hume
Look, Isaacs, Kingston, and others —has been a
Protectionist. Those should be sufficiently broad
hints for any party.
* * • #
If the Federal Labor party likes to become the
party of High Protection, then power—the power
to do infinite good and to prevent infinite harm —
is within its grasp. It is possible to count about
a. dozen Federal members —say Lyne, Chanter,
Mauger, Chapman, Hume Cook, Groom, Kings-
ton, Keating, Trenwith, Mulcahy, Higgins,
Playford, and Isaacs —who, so far as can be
seen, are outside the party solely because it
lacks a strong Protectionist policy; and several
more, such as Deakin and Bonython, from
whom it is divided principally by the tariff issue.
.Li the party gives up its old negative attitude it
can gather in the bulk of these, and they are
mostly men with safe seats—men who can bring
their constituencies along with them. As against
this the party, by making a new departure,
might lose three seats, all in N.S.W.— those of
Canoblas, West Sydney and Darling. It is much
dkely, however, that it would lose none.
Ihe Freetraders have become so definitely and so
openiy the crowd that is opposed to every Labor
ideal that it is impossible to imagine a Labor con-
stituency rejecting a Labor candidate to elect one
of the Wilks or Bruce Smith brand. A year or
two ago, when Freetraders still posed as, in some
measure, the friends of Labor, it was possible to
imagine Hughes, as a High Protectionist, being
rejected m favor of a follower of Reid at West
bydney, but to-day such a thing is just about
impossible.
* * * *
If the Federal Labor party does not take this
short cut to power, and if it continues to stay out
in the cold and sink the fiscal issue, it will pro-
bab!y be a good many years before it arrives in
office with a reliable majority of its own. And
the irony of the position is that, when it does
arrive, it will have to give up sinking the fiscal
issue after all. It will have fooled away all these
years sacrificed them, not for the preservation of
a belief but to preserve the want of a befief—and
the sacrifice will be utterly wasted. The tariff is
a thing that Won’t Keep Still—at least not for
any length of time. It is so utterly impossible to
, e P, lt; that even the powerful Coalition
which attained office by sinking the fiscal issue
had not been in office six months before it was
dnven to appoint a Tariff Commission which tore
the buried problem out of its grave, and filled
the country with clamor. The tariff ghost
wifi be there, sitting on the front Treasury
bench, the day Watson assumes office. And
then, as one rampant anomaly after another
demands settlement, the Labor Government will
have to decide whether to settle each difficulty
by levelling the anomaly down towards Freetrade
or up towards Protection. It will have to take
one course or another. Of course, it may palter
with its conscience by being a very poor, apolo-
getic and half-hearted Freetrader or a very
inefficient and useless'.Protectionist, but it wiil
have to find a fiscal belief of some sort none the
less. It would have had to provide itself with
one by now if it had not been thrown out of office,
for it held office during its precarious four months 5,
tenure solely by the aid and countenance of Pro-
tectionists outside its own ranks, and it could not
be supposed that they would have gone on sup-
porting it for ever without large tariff concessions
m return. The principle of support in return for
concessions works both ways.
* * * *
Those who ask the Federal Labor party to be-
come the party of High Protection—the party
which stands for Australian patriotism, for Aus-
tralian progress, for Australian industry, for Aus-
traJian wealth, and for work and wages for Aus-
tralian citizens—do not ask it to give up a prin-
ciple. Ihey ask it rather to acquire a principle
u AT o I ?T? St T- Urgent matterin Australian politics
the JN.b. W. Labor party, which was the beginning
of Labor m Australian politics, said, for ten weary
years* ‘‘l dunno!” It handed on its negative
shibboleth to the Federal Labor party, and it in
turn, for four years, has said “I dunno!” To
“dunno ”is not a principle nor a belief—it is only
an And when the party is called upon
to KIN OW, it is only called upon to acquire in-
formation, to fill a void, to get some kind of a faith
to be a positive instead of a negative, and a fiscal
power instead of a shadow.
* * * .
Further, it is worth while for the present Labor
party to remember that if it continues not to know
m a fiscal sense it may yet have to give place to
another Labor party that does know. In that case
the present party may never get into power at all -
nor even into office, which, as its last year’s experi-
ence proved, is not quite the same thing. At pre-
sent it is putting a very heavy strain on the good-
will of a great mass of its supporters. When it
reaches power it will have to find a tariff policy
• U j i* . is , t° postpone its arrival almost
indefinitely, merely because it will not find a tariff
policy till it gets into power. Many of its sup-
porters begin to think that the mere joy and
pleasure and satisfaction of having no tariff policy
are not so joyful and pleasant and satisfactory as
to be worth the delay. The support which the
party gets from outside its own ranks comes almost
exclusively from Protectionist politicians, and Pro-
tectionist papers, and from the great mass of people
who elect these politicians and support these papers,
-these all represent a force which may grow weary
of too long waiting on the policy of “dunno’’
To them Protection—real Protection—is a matter
of the first importance, whatever it may be to the
a S rTregate political conscience of the Labor party
and their patience may give out. Again, while
the Labor party remains out of office, because it
would rather be a party which does not know its
mind on the tariff problem than have a definite
mind, and gather the democratic Protectionists
into its fold, and reach for power and office with
both hands while it does this, the enemy is in
charge, the White Australia policy is in cumber
the State Bank idea is defunct, Federal arbitration
is practically a dead letter, the hopes of getting a
Federal territory in which the private land-
grabber won t intrude are fading day by day and
the fabric of democratic progress threatens to go
to pieces. To see all this going on because the
Labor party declines to sacrifice the joy of
not being officially aware of its own fiscal views is
more than the loyalty of the party’s well-wishers
can stand for ever. The Labor party itself con-
sists very largely of Protectionists, and, in the end
these may secede. They may coalesce with the
democratic Protectionists outside on a platform
which embodies the Labor party’s principles and a
patriotic policy of promoting Australian wealth
and industries. In that case there would be two
Labor parties, and the party that did know would
probably so entirely overshadow the one that
didn’t know that the latter would amount to very
little. This may seem a fanciful danger, but it
is more real now than it ever was before.
-The only thing that can hold the present
nefarious Coalition together through the next elec-
tion is the continued sinking of the fiscal issue.
.The frantic demands of certain Coalition leaders
that the fight shall be solely on the question
of Socialism and anti-Socialism, and that
the tariff question. shall not be raised under any
circumstances, are in themselves evidence of that.
Already there is a distinct rift in the combina-
tion, as evidenced by the fact that the Protec-
tionist members of the Government stand almost
wholly aloof from the acti-Socialist agitation.
Mi Lean says little; Turner says practically
nothing; Drake says less than Turner, though,
so far as speaking to the purpose is concerned,
that is as much as Drake usually says. If all the
opponents of the Reid-M‘Lean Government went
to the country with High Protection in the front
of their programme, then half-Premier Reid must
needs defend himself where he was attacked. He
must needs defend Preetrade when his political
life depended upon it. And if Turner, M‘Lean
and their Protectionist adherents were placed
m such a position that they could not fight
against Watson without fighting against Protec-
tion, and could not fight for Reid without fighting
for Freetrade, the ill-jointed league would go to
pieces like a crate of crockery struck by lightning.
On the other hand, if the Labor party meets Reid
on the ground he has selected, and only hits
M‘Millan where he tells them to hit him, and
poses as the Socialistic party pure and simple
because Bruce Smith bids them do so, then it has
no chance—unless, indeed, these wily old political
battlers have greatly miscalculated. At all events,
by refusing to hit the Coalition on the one weak
spot—on the joint where the two ill-matched
halves of it are so badly fastened together—it will
give that body a fair chance of another lease of
life, and give it more time to undo what little
good has been accomplished. And not a
few people are apt to grow impatient with
a party which sacrifices _so many chances
mu • sa : ke preserving its fiscal indecision.
Ihe indecision hardly seems worth it. Especially
tho Protectionist members of tbe Labor party
who, if they raised the tariff war-cry, could smite
Lie enemy hip and thigh by themselves at next
general election, may grow weary of being held in,
and angry at seeing their opportunities offered up
as a burnt sacrifice on the altar which bears the
cheerless inscription “I dunno.”
• • * •
But if the Labor party does take to Protection
it must do so in real earnest or no permanent good
will result. Australia has no use for another
Dibbs tariff, which did little save bring Protection
into contempt. It doesn’t want the ineffective
Victorian tariff, which used to admit into that one
small state £13,000,000 worth of goods annually
for Victorian consumption alone. It doesn’t want
a mere barren, forgotten, formal plank, like that
vir kh? restriction of borrowing, which the
*l. 11 '• ®k*-te Labor party adopted and then
wholly ignored. It looks for something living and
vigorous—something like the tariff policy of Canada
or the United States, which is a gospel rather
than a mere dry plank. The aim should be, in
every industry that can be carried on in Aus-
tralia, to give the local producer a substantial
advantage, even with high Australian wages and
short Australian hours, over all competitors—even
the cheapest of them. It is necessary to get away,
not only from the bad present condition of things,
but from the very best past condition, and to aim
at something better than has ever yet been
attempted. And in discussing this matter it is
well to remind the party that there is nothing to
be gained by falling back on the old argument
about the lamentable condition of the workers in
some Protectionist countries, where trusts bloat,
and millionaires get gout, and men decay. No one
has asserted that Protection, without arbitration
laws and similar safeguards, secures to the
worker his fair share of the general wealth.
It is just to secure to the worker his fair share of
the general wealth that all the existing Labor
legislation of Australia, and all the further legis-
lation that will exist when the Labor party comes
really into power, is intended. But if it is
claimed that Australia dare not create industries
because the best safeguards that its democratic
party can devise will not prevent the worker being
oppressed in these industries, and that it dare not
accumulate wealth because the best Socialistic
policy it can construct won’t secure the worker his
fair share of that wealth, then the position is dis-
appointing beyond expression in words. The
I ederal Labor party can’t afford to pose as a
party which is afraid to come into power with a
mission to build up works like Carnegie’s in
Australia because it has no idea how to so use its
power as to prevent these works being run on
Carnegie principles.
LOANS AND IMMIGRATION.
In reply to an article (Bulletin, 22/6/’O5), Dr.
Arthur, M.L.A., writes :
At last election I belonged to the anti-borrowing party.
lam charged now with having forgotten it. I admit—
not that I have forgotten, but that I have changed my
nnnd. Much water has run under the bridge since last
August. A yellow race now holds the hegemony of the
Pacific, and the prestige of the white man in Asia is gone.
There is not at present a battleship belonging to a white
nation in the Pacific. Port Darwin is ten days from
Yokohama ; swift cruisers could do it in seven. We can
possibly rely on the British fleet, for 20 years. Perhaps
not so long, for immediately the German fleet increases to
anything like equality, England will need all her ships in
European waters.
Now, the problem—four million people in Australia,
with a rapidly declining birthrate and three million
square miles, some of it, at any rate, very fertile. Japan,
44 million inhabitants, increasing half-a-million a year,
and 160,000 square miles.
Next, read the opinions of Mr. Colk’s Japanese friends
as to where the surplus population of Japan must go;
and study history as to how militant nations have treated
their weak and WEALTHY neighbors, and then do a little
furious thinking. If this empty continent cannot get an
enormous increment of population in the next 20 years,
there will not be much doubt as to our destiny. All our
elaborate Anglo-Saxon civilisation will be swamped by
invasion, either peaceful or the reverse, from the Asia we
have despised ; our children mean whites and helots.
I affirm again, immigration of white races on a huge
scale is the chief remedy. I did not say it was the only
one. If there are others—High Protection, increased land
tax—let us try them all. I PROPOSE TO IMPORT
MONEY that we may import men. And import men to
settle them on the soil. The stunted factory hand doesn’t
supply the stuff to make soldiers of. Look at the recruit-
ing results in England during the South African war.
I only dealt with one side of the question in the Herald
letter. I would give our own people free land, too, with—
a matter you overlooked—a 2J per cent, land-tax on
unimproved value after five years. The late Charles
Pearson said “ The distant future of a country is so unim-
portant by the side of its immediate needs to the men in
possession, that even if they were reasonably certain that
a particular evil ought to be guarded against at an
immediate sacrifice, they would rarely be possessed of the
moral force required for the effort.” Is that to be true
for us ?
The above large capitals are intended to draw
attention to the brilliant idea of importing money
into a wealthy country.
* ♦ * *
The Bulletin has known cases in which a
member, when, like Dr. Arthur, he had changed
his mind on a most important part of the policy
on which he was elected, has thought it necessary
to inform his constituents of the fact and give
them a chance to object. Some members have
been so beastly conscientious as to resign and
stand for re-election, on the ground that the man
who gains his seat on one policy and holds it on
another, retains that seat under false pretences.
Dr. Arthur, apparently, has no such scruples.
When he was elected, he was on the non-borrowing
ticket, and he thought so little of his own alleged
political principles that he started to advocate
borrowing without even mentioning that, in so
doing, he had shifted his views. Some people
would call this easy turning of the coat a mean
political treachery ; others wouldn’t.
* * * *
Much water —and also a good deal of slush —has
certainly “run under the bridge since last
August.” Australia looks as if it were nearer to
a great danger than it ever was before, arid it
wants men—men —and yet more men. It has
been trying for the last 24 years or so to
attract population by heaping up debts, and
now it is, of all the great “new” countries
on earth, the one with the biggest debt per
head and the least immigration. And the
only remedy Dr. Arthur can see is to borrow
more, and to keep on borrowing. Canada and the
United States have practically left off borrowing,
and are reducing their debts; and they are rushed
with immigrants. Australia, in the view of Dr.
Arthur and the party to which he belongs, should
rather stick to the methods which have landed it
in the present bog than follow their example.
Australia has already made a huge experiment in
the way of assisted immigration—the kind that
consists of bringing people here and turning them
out to graze on the “magnificent natural resources
of the country,” and to “go on the land.” Up to
1903, it brought out 645,987 people in that way,
and spent much land revenue and over £3,400,000
of loan money on the work. The present result
seems to be stagnation, poverty, and an anxious
desire on the part of many of these purchased im-
migrants to get away again. _ Queensland did most
of all in the assisted loan-immigration business;
it spent £2,943,963 of borrowed money to bring out
169,538 immigrants, and its present position is al-
most the worst in Australasia.
• • • •
The “stunted factory hand” is a threadbare
jest. No doubt he is stunted in countries where
there are long hours, poor wages, crowded slums,
insanitary factories, ignorance and dirt. But with
clean and proper conditions, such as the Labor
party, which Dr. Arthur opposes, is trying to
enforce in Australia, he need not be more stunted
than anybody else. And even if he must be
stunted—even if the brawny iron worker, for in-
stance, must be a hollow-chested dwarf, and the
blacksmith, or brass-founder, or brickmaker, or
shipwright a weakling—the stunted man is better
behind a gun than no man at all. Also, unless we
get the stunted artisan here in large numbers
there is no market nearer than Europe for the
output of Dr. Arthur’s assisted loan immigrant,
who is to go on the land; and if the immigrant
has to depend on that, it would pay him better to
go to some place where there is quite as good soil
nearer to his market. That fact makes him diffi-
cult to get and still more difficult to keep.
* * • *
When war comes,. money, men and munitions
are the first essentials. Every country has to
borrow in war time. The gospel of the loan party
to which Dr. Arthur has attached himself seems
to be roughly this :
(1) To exhaust our credit in time of peace so that, when
war comes, we won’t be able to raise a copper.
(2) To make no special effort to establish the works
necessary for producing- the materials of defence—even
though those works, if established on a large enough scale
to meet all the requirements of war, would give employ-
ment to an immense number of men, and be in themselves
the biggest immigration scheme this country has ever
seen—lest we produce the “ stunted factory hand.”
This party, too, wants to “import money”—in
other words, to export money in interest—“that
we may import men.” What need can there be
to import money ? Australia, Dr. Arthur says,
is wealthy. It certainly pays away over £36,000,000
a year for foreign goods; in its consumption of
foreign goods it is almost the most luxurious place
on earth. If it wants to import men, let it raise
an extra £1.000,000 or £2,000,000 a year in taxa-
tion, do with £1,000,000 or £2,000,000 a year less
imports, and pay cash for its men. If it feels it
can t do without the goods, it will probably set to
work—if it gets a decent Protective tariff—and
make them, and thus find work for its imported
men. We want a morally sturdy and self-reliant
race when war comes our way; not a morally
stunted people who have been taught that they are
so cowardly and helpless that they can’t pay their
own way, despite the wealth which Dr. Arthur
acknowledges this country to possess.
* * « *
The Bulletin can picture Dr. Arthur’s Aus-
tralia when the great war strikes it—the Australia
which is to be saved by loan-immigration, and
“settling people on the land,” combined with an
off-hand disregard for local industries, and a
careful avoidance of the “stunted factory hand.”
We will, perhaps, have gained 1,000,000 immi-
grants, if we can borrow £15,000,000 or so to
bring them out; though we may have lost a good
many of them again. Past experience has shown
that if we make no serious efforts to provide any
work save “ going on the land,” we get verv few
immigrants save those we pay for, and when we
stop, paying, not only do immigrants stop
coming, but they often start going away again.
The command of the sea will, presumably, be lost,
or nearly so, otherwise we could not be invaded.
We will have gone bankrupt in Europe for various
reasons, including inability to export anything to
pay the interest. We will naturally have no
credit to raise foreign loans upon. As soon as the
supply actually on hand has been used up, we will
have no guns, rifles, ammunition, no steel plates
to build ships with, no steel rails to lay down light
railways for strategic purposes, or to replace lines
torn up by the enemy, and hardly any rolling
stock or locomotives to replace those injured, lost
or destroyed. We will be almost destitute of hats,
boots, clothes, medicines, spirits, surgical instru-
ments, bedding, tents, tools, telegraph wire, elec-
trical machinery, telescopes to look for the enemy
with, paper, and almost everything else—all for
want of local industries and the “ stunted factory
hand ” who runs them. When the enemy holds
the sea, the crops raised by the wonderful
array of loan-immigrants can’t be exported, and
there will be no local “stunted factory hand”
to buy them, so the surplus will rot
in the ground. And there will be a
ragged army attacking the enemy with clods and
with sheep-shears. On the other hand, what The
Bulletin aims at is to make Australia a country
which can supply itself with everything that will
be wanted in war—which also involves supplying
itself with everything that can be wanted in
peace. That means a country which has almost
entirely shut out imports, and which has had to
advertise for 1,000,000 artisans to make the things
it has hitherto imported—also a country where
artisans are not necessarily stunted. Further, it
means a country which has had to advertise for
another million or two of agriculturists to feed its


artisans and still more artisans to supply its new
agriculturists. Also, incidentally, it means a
country which hasn’t exhausted its credit before
the war begins. And before Australia can fill tle
bill in that respect it must leave off deficits, pay
for necessary works out of revenue, and reduce its
debt—the debt must be reduced to leave any use-
ful margin. And all that is possible-despite Dr.
Arthur’s theory that this is a wealthy country
which is so poor that it can’t do without borrowing.
THE HIGH COURT AND THE CABINET.
The telephones, with the exception of one, have
been cut off from the High Court Judges’ cham-
bers in Sydney, and the members of the supreme
judicial body of the Commonwealth are now
denied the convenience which practically every
civil servant above the grade of a clerk enjoys.
Three judges, three associates, and three tip-
staffs, occupying widely-apart rooms, must now
rush to the one telephone. It doesn’t seem a very
big grievance, but as the crowning point of the
Reid-Symon campaign of small, mean attempts
to bully and degrade the High Court it bulks
large. 'The telephones were installed m the first
instance without any request on the part of the
Judges—as part of the ordinary equipment of
the offices. They cost the bederal Government
nothing but the bare charge of the instrument, as the
’phone service is aF ederal monopoly; and removing
them is purely a bit of mischievous spite. All the
State Judges have telephones—though, in their
case, the State has to pay for them—as have all
the officials of any rank m either public or private
employ. If the'Federal Gqvernment wished to
save the cost of the several instruments, a switch
service with telephonette attachments might ha\e
been resorted to. But the motive isn t economy;
it’s mischief.
* * * *
The tipstaffs attached to the High Court have
received abrupt notice of dismissal. I his is
another pin-prick at the Federal judiciary. Ihe
tipstaff is, practically, a messenger. It s his busi-
ness to run errands for the Judge, to bring lum
what books he needs on the Bench, and generally
to do at one shilling an hour work that he can do
just as well as the man who draws £2 an hour.
Abolish office-boys in a mercantile house and the
demands on the time of the principals would so
increase as to cause a congestion of work, and call
for the employment of more officials. Abolish
tipstaffs for Judges and you bring within measure-
able distance the time when you must appoint an
additional Judge. It’s the saving of time effected
by getting a cheap unskilled worker to do the
little incidental bits of cheap work that enables big
officials to give good value for their big salaries.
But the Reid-Symon spite defies all econo-
mics. The High Court Ju.: o es are to be their
own errand-boys in future. And m their hurry
to have a tilt at the judiciary Reid and SyMon
haven’t considered even the law. Ine ± übiic
Service Act calls for independent reports from the
permanent head of a department and the 1 übLc
Service Commissioner before an office can be
abolished. The tipstaffs have been dismissed,
seemingly, on the mere say-so of Reid and Symon.
The Cabinet has fixed a new scale of expenses
for the High Court Judges. A Judge travelling
is to get £3 3s. a day for himself and Ins clerk-
associate, except when he is on original jurisdic-
tion, when he is to get, with his clerk, £2 IJs. oa.,
or if he is travelling with his brother Judges
each will get £1155.; the idea seems to be that
three Judges travelling together can sleep in the
same bed. The experience of the Federal Judges
so far, charging only out-of-pocket expenses, is that
nearly £4 a day is the average amount spent by a
and his clerk. That amount may seem
large, but it is to be kept in mind that a J udge
on circuit can’t stay at a cheap coffee-palace, hie
must be housed as befits his dignity, and hotel-
keepers charge for that. What is important to
note in this matter is that
1 The expense rate allowed is much less than, say,
the rate allowed to State Judges in N.S.W. .
2. It will, without doubt, involve the Judges m some
loss when travelling.
Reid and Symon further tap at the salaries of the
Judges with a proviso that the seat of the Oourt
is to be stationed at Melbourne, and if the Judges
do not live there they must lose any travelling
expenses incurred in going to Melbourne.
The Federal Judges’ salaries were, before these
exactions, lower than the judicial salaries m
NS W. and Victoria. Victoria pays from £3OOO
to'£8500; N.S.W. £2600 to £3OOO-in each case
with pension rights of, roughly, two-thirds of the
salary. A N.S.W. or Victorian Judge, disabled
by illness a month after his appointment, can
draw during the rest of his life nearly £2OOO a year.
The Federal Judges get from £3OOO to £3ooo a
vear with no pension rights. To all intents and
purposes their salaries have been. now reduced by
ILxecutive fiction, and the threat is held over them
of another reduction—presumably if they are not
obsequious to the Government. The independence
of the court is thus insidiously attacked. Attorney -
General Symon’s announced intentions at the
outset were to confine the sittings of the -Hugh
Court to Melbourne, and generally to ‘ take it
out” of its members. He has had to abandon any
direct attempt at the first part of his programme,
but he has succeeded in making it a matter of
penalty for the Judges to sit out of Melbourne,
and in heaping on them a mass of mean insmts
and petty indignities.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Mr. "Willis Comes Back.
It is not within human nature to expect that
the Carruthers Government should show any
freat eagerness to push home the N.b. W.
lands Department Inquiry. At least three
members of the Cabinet—perhaps innocently
enough have at different times had fees
from° those land agents who show such a
strenuous objection to the Commission. Some
of the Ministry’s supporters in the Assembly
are implicated in what has been going on. And the
Premier himself is head of the law firm which
acted for one of the most uneasy of the witnesses
before the Commission. But, as The Bulletin
put it before, the public will demand to get
at the whole truth, even if afterwards it is
willing to condone a good deal of what was
improper, and to insist only on punishing
actual bribery and corruption. For any mem-
ber of Parliament to act as a land agent m
obtaining concessions from the Bands Depart-
ment whether he used his political influence in
the matter or not, is utterly and completely
objectionable. Yet several members of Parlia-
ment have so acted, and some of them, probably,
without recognition of the impropriety of the
action, certainly without any knowingly corrupt
motive. The people can afford to be charitable,
in dealing with them, as to the past, provided
the principal rogues are punished, and the future
is guarded against any repetition of the scandals
now being slowly disclosed. But the essential
thing is the truth—the whole truth. In eliciting
it, by the way, the frauds Commission must
have the further assistance of Mr. W. N. Willis.
The sincerity of the Government can fairly be
tested by that point. The Government alleged
that it had no legal power to ensure that. Still
Mr. Willis is coming back. Possibly not as a
witness before the Royal Commission, but certainly
in some way. Everybody knows that without the
help of Mr. Willis and Mr. Willis’ books the
inquiry will become practically a it.wdl
be time to order whitewash all round. The failure
of the Government to take steps to induce Mr.
Willis to return to Sydney, puts the Government
in much the same boat as Mr. Willis as a party
anxious to avoid the disclosure of the truth.
In Praise of Bent.
Last week Premier Bent, of Victoria, made a
dull speech of 150 minutes duration, and tried to
brighten up his statement of last year’s doings and
next year’s policy with limelight views. As a re-
sult of various hitches, he was compelled to read
his notes by the light of a dim candle, and his
angry countenance, as he perspired freely into the
candlestick, was a matter of public interest.
Nevertheless, he had something remarkable to
say. The borrowings for the current year, he
said, consisted of £104,000, all raised locally. The
amount paid off the public debt out of revenue
was £226,000, largely paid off in London; conse-
quently the Victorian debt was actually reduced
by £62,000. If this amazing statement proves
true, then Bent —even the dull, bear-like, clown-
ing Bent —is a man that Victoria can’t spare
until it finds another financier as good as
he. He looms up—or looms horizontally; it
is hard to say which —as the _ first Premier
Australia has possessed in something like lialf-a-
century who has reduced his State s debt, lie is
the first one who has aimed at any such reduction,
and almost the first one who seems to think public
solvency a matter to be proud of. F urthermore,
Bent hopes to drag together a surplus of over
£400,000, and with this he proposes to reduce the
accumulated shortage a little, to do a few public
works, and to pav for one or two resumed
estates. When the' land, which was originally
sold by the State so that the proceeds might help
the revenue, is re-purchased by the State, it is only
common honesty that the revenue should stand
the cost, but it h;.s been left for the common Bent,
of all men, to realise this simple fact. No doubt
Bent’s success is largely the result of economies
which have their basis in meanness and gross op-
pression, but there is a desperate necessity in Aus-
tralia for financial reform, and if the oppressor is
the only man who will supply it, then it becomes
necessary to hold a candle to the oppressor. It
is advisable to get rid of the Bent Govern-
ment, and replace it by one with some
liberalism and some sense of fairness in its treat-
ment of the public servants, but unless the new
Government will be as honest with the finances
as Bent (if his accounts can stand auditing) has
been, it is still more advisable to leave things as
thev are. If it is a choice between the coarse but
economical oppressor and the democratic spend-
thrift, Australia has reached such a point of debt
and financial degradation that it had better choose
the coarse but economical oppressor. Of course
Bent’s surplus is swelled by the inclusion of the
proceeds of land sales, said proceeds being treated
as revenue ; but the proceeds of land sal ys are not
a large item now in Victoria, and if Bent s figures
can stand official inspection, he seems likely, at the
close of the financial year, to accomplish three
things that sound almost incredible in Australian
finance: (1) To slightly reduce the debt; (2) to
set aside ail the land sale revenue for public
works and undertakings; and (3) to have some
small amount over, out of ordinary revenue,
to devote to public works and undertakings.
Even this may not be all that it should be, but
when it is remembered that there have been in
Australia Premiers quite as objectionable as
Bent, who used four times his land-sale revenue
to cover ordinary expenses, who borrowed two or
three millions a year and charged all their new
works, and all their land resumptions, and many
of their repairs to loans, and who then wound up
with a big deficit and a plausible lie, then Thomas
—even Thomas, of Brighton, the land-boomer, the
man of strange jobs and speculations, the ungrain-
matical person who says “ bloomin’” and ‘ * bang ”
—promises to shine like the countenance of a full
moon in a dark land. His financial success, should
it prove to be all it is alleged, will give the
democratic party a great set-back, and if Bent
undertakes to make the steady reduction of the
public debt and payment for works out of revenue
two fixed features of his policy, the set-back
will be still worse. The democratic party all
over Australia has had the chance to make
public honesty and solvency the front items
of its programme—and by its indifference it has
left that honor, glory, credit, and advertisement to
Bent. Supposing that person’s accounts really
stand the audit at the close of the financial year,
this paper proposes trying to be much more
respectful to Bent as a general tiling though it
will be a difficult undertaking.

A Motion of Censure.
The motion of censure putupinN.S.W. Assembly
by Labor-leader M‘Go wen tottered last week to
an aged and ignominious conclusion. There was
hardly anything else that it could totter to under
the circumstances. The motion was a general
one, but it was founded principally, and in the
first place, on the neglect of Premier Caeeuthebs
to appoint a new Judge of the_ Arbitration Court.
Premier Caeeuthebs, who is a most bendable
man, almost broke his neck, or at least managed
to look as if he were almost breaking his neck, to
get a Judge appointed, whereby lie poured a con-
siderable amount of water over that particular hot
coal. Apart from the Arbitration question the
motion depended mostly on the local manu-
facture of locomotives, and CABEUTHEES’breatli-
less assurance that he couldn’t sleep at night for
attending to the matter of those same locomotives
was a considerable quantity of mud heaped on
that cinder. After Cabbuthees had turned the
water-tap of apology on those two matters, and had
backed down as far as possible, and crawled out
as far as possible, there was little heart in
the debate. It got down to a drivelsome level,
and included complaints about a flood or a burst
water-pipe in a back street of Sydney, some per-
sonal matter about a policeman, and a ridiculous
wail by a very ignorant Tory member concerning
the alleged height of ant-hills. The Labor party
might have made the debate a great fight on the
largest question in Australian politics, but it pre-
ferred to grope along on a low level and be a politi-
cal caterpillar down in the basement of things.
The party is pledged to oppose all loan expendi-
ture on new works except such as will pay full
interest on their cost and something over for sink-
ing fund. Premier Caekuthees pledged himself
almost as solemnly at last election to cut down
loan expenditure to £1,500,000 a year. He lias
broken his own pledge, and lias impudently stated,
in the speech with which Parliament opened, that
he will continue breaking it; and as for the Labor
party’s pledge, he takes as little notice of it as does
the Labor party itself. Then there is a considerable
number of members who have given their word of
honor, as members who are gentlemen and not
liars, to oppose all borrowing. Yet with all these
disturbing elements on hand, the financial ques-
tion —the question which, above all others, is a
matter of national importance—was not once
dealt with in the two weeks’ debate. The dis-
cussion might have been raised to the level of
something tnat suggested statesmanship; instead
it was, except as regards the Arbitration Court
matter, a long-dragged-out twaddling about small
matters, a weary procession of small jokes, and a
purposeless stonewall. Presumably no one out-
side Australia takes much interest in the matter.
But, if any extraneous person does care to study
it, this careful avoidance of great issues, and this
purposeless creeping among problems that are
almost too small to attract the attention of a
Drainage Board, must make him wonder whether
the N.S.W. Parliament knows that it is a Parlia-
ment or mistakes itself for its own office boy.
