The N.S.W. Arbitration Act.
Dr. Victor Clark was recently commissioned by
the Government of the United States to visit
Australia and report upon its labor conditions.
His report, the result of a long and exhaustive
investigation, has just been published in Aus-
tralia. Sydney Telegraph issued a “summary
of the report, but strangely enough, the
summarizer couldn’t find room for any of Dr.
Clark’s observations which told in favor of
Industrial Arbitration, though these observations
were both strong and numerous. In regard to
preference to unionists, after referring to the
arguments for and against, he says (p. 107): —
To an outside observer it would look as if the grievances
on both sides were exaggerated. It is doubtful if the
preference clause in an award works special hardship upon
the average employer in the administration of his business.
At least it was very hard to pin any employer interviewed
down to a concrete instance where it had done so ; and
even where this was possible, the hardship complained
was in no case so great as those frequently attending the
adjustment of relations between employers and unions in
America.
Dr. Clark also refers to our old friend, the
capitalist, who has been driven by these tyran-
nical laws to remove himself and his money to
another country (p. 149): —
Sometimes the statement is made that they (the
Arbitration laws) drive capital out of a country. No
specific instance was discovered by the writer where
money had been withdrawn from an industry because of
Labor legislation in any Australian State.
At pp. 160-161, Dr. Clark sums up his criticisms
of the Arbitration system :
Due stress has been laid upon the weak points in the
Arbitration laws of Australia in the above review. All
have not been enumerated, but the others are of
relatively minor importance . . . There are two kinds of
radical legislation, a kind that conquers attention and
demands support from the time it stands on the Statute
books, that becomes a question of vital interest, around
which parties rise and fall; and a kind that depends for
its existence on a momentary wave of moral enthusiasm.
The Arbitration laws of Australia seem to represent
the former class of laws. Their cen:ral principle, com-
pulsory State intervention to adjudicate serious industrial
disputes, promises to persist. They are reported to have
secured for the laborer a clear definition of his right to a
living wage, reasonable security against involuntary
interruption of employment by the caprice either of his
own leaders or of arbitrary employers, and to have
checked sweating’ in certain lines of manufacturing. Ihe.v
have also assured employers, in a great majority of
instances, though not in all, against a cessation of mdus-
trv by strikes. Business men have been able to take con-
tracts and make plans for future operations with this par-
ticular element of uncertainty left out. Above all—and
here may be the secret of the growing power of the Labor
party—the general public has not been worried or put to
inconvenience and loss by the industrial dissensions of
other people.
The Sydney Telegraph reviewer seems to have
been peculiarly unfortunate in overlooking, or. in
not having space for, any of these interesting
criticisms, and many others to a similar effect.
And that is just like the Telegraph’s luck.
Victoria’s Neglected. Lunatic.
Victoria’s newly-imported Inspector-General of
the Insane, Dr. Jones, having taken stock of the
State’s asylums, has handed Premier Bent a
report which scathingly condemns the neglect and
stupid meanness that have hitherto prevailed. The
report recommends an immediate expenditure of
£250,000 in putting things into decent order. Dr.
Jones finds that the weekly average cost of
patients is only 11s. 7d., ss. sd. of which goes in
salaries and wages, leaving but 6s. 2d. a week for
food, clothing, medical comforts, and necessaries.
(This is about the best recommendation for
“ thrift’’ a Tory Treasurer is ever likely to get—
but it is one that even Bent will scarcely boast of.)
The maintenance and repair funds have been
starved, consequently heavy outlay is now neces-
sary ; Ararat gaol ward should be closed alto-
gether ; Yarra Bend should be rebuilt from end to
end; Sunbury asylum should be enlarged; and
extensive alterations are imperative at Kew,
Ballarat and Beechlvorth. The existing asylums
are overcrowded to an astounding degree, having
600 patients more than there is proper accommo-
dation for. Additional assistants are urgently
needed —two more medical officers, two clerks,
two assistant matrons, two head attendants,
36 tradesmen and assistants of various sorts and
two ornamental gardeners. It is said that
Bent snorted when he read this last item, yet
capable gardeners, carpenters, etc., are probably
almost as efficacious in restoring sanity to the
afflicted as even the best medical officer on the
premises. Dr. Jones further animadverted on
the wastefulness, want of cleanliness and danger
of the wooden buildings that compose Yarra
Bend and some of the other institutions ; on the
utter lack of provision for epileptics and the sick
and infirm, while such things as observation
wards are non-existent; on the absence of any
provision for paying-patients; on the dearth of
comfortable ward accommodation —“ bare, com-
fortless barns, void of any object of interest that
could brighten the lives of the inmates.” The
crude arrangements for bathing patients and their
lavatory accommodation he describes as dis-
graceful, and much of it has been con-
demned again and again without any improvement
resulting. The kitchen, laundry and lighting ac-
commodation is unscientific and out-of-date, while
in every ward on a high floor the patients are in
danger of being burnt in such a holocaust as re-
cently occurred at one of the London County
Council asylums. The report is a bitter satire on
“financial reform’’of the Kyabram brand, and
it says plainly that Victoria has practically got to
build up and set going a complete, new system of
caring for and curing its insane. But it will cost
£250,000, and there is no immediate visible return.
Bent will probably bellow that there is no finan-
cial return whatever —but there is. Under a
scientific system — such as Dr. Jones was imported
to inaugurate—probably less than half the present
number of Victorian lunatics would need to be
cared for in the asylums. Senile patients could
be maintained in separate homes at even less
cost than at present; while the percentage of
cures could be raised 60 high that the whole cost
of the system need be no more, and would pro-
bably be less, than it is to-day. And in addition
Victoria would relieve itself of the stigma that,
once a man loses his reason, there is for lunuio
hope of cure, nor even of common comfort and de-
cency.

The Remnant of an Arbitration Act.
By three successive decisions the High Court has
practically abolished the N.S.W. Arbitration Act..
There is now hardly enough of that much-harried
legislation left to be worth worrying about, and
when the Caiuujtheks Government, which, for a
long time, reduced the Act to a state of catalepsy,
has done some further whittling away of the
powers of the court, the small remnant of the
Arbitration law will be invisible to the naked eye,
The decisions of the High Court have limited trie
effectiveness of the Arbitration Court m this
way :
(1) In the Clancy case, the right of the Court to
enforce early closing as the logical corollary of reasonable
hours of employment was denied. The Court could
declare that all employees in the butchering trade should
be released from duty at a certain hour, but could not
order the butchering shops to close at that hour, thus
the only effective means of enforcing the Court’s decision
was over-ruled. . .
(2) In the Lassetter case it was denied that the Arbitra-
tion Court had power to order a “ common rule” in any
industry on the basis of an amicable arrangement
between an employer and his employees—though the
proposal was first subjected to examination and objec-
tion in court by all the persons interested. Before there
could be a common rule there must be a dispute, not an
amicable agreement. In fact, peace was only to be
arranged on condition that there w r as a preliminary row,
and the only way to make peace was to have war.
(3) In the Trol'lymen’s case, the right of the Arbitration
Court to award preference to unionists was practically
destroyed.
The third decision, which is the most important,
calls for explanation. In the Trollymen’s case,
the Arbitration Court awarded preference to
unionists, and to make that award properly effec-
tive, ordered that employers should apprise the
union before making engagements, so that unionists
should have an equal chance with non-unionists in
applying (a previous decision enacted that the
“ preference ” could only be given to unionists
applying before, or at tbe same time with, non-
unionists). The High Court now says that it is
ultra vires to order an employer to apprise the
unions when he is in want of labor. Thus the
employer is left free to keep a register ox non-
unionists, and, by first apprising men on that
register of vacancies, to absolutely boycott
unionists. This may be good law, but it is very
poor sense, and it takes from the Arbitration Act
its keystone principle—which is to encourage the
workers to enter trades-unions, so that they may
collectively bargain and have a collective
responsibility for the keeping of the in-
dustrial peace. The High Court .took the
narrowest technical view of the case. A reason-
able mind would argue that since the Arbitration
Act gives the Arbitration Court power to grant
preference to unionists, consequently the Arbi-
tration Act gives the Arbitration Court power to
order all the necessary steps to make that prefer-
ence effective. But that is not the view of the
High Court. It insists that the Act should have
paraded and set forth all the administrative details
necessary to make a preference possible. Just as
reasonably it might be argued that a law against
stealing is operative only in regard to the
things it expressly mentions as not to be
stolen —that you mustn’t steal chairs, or eggs,
or hats if the law mentions them, but you may
steel hat-pins, bird-cages, and electric trams, if it
should happen that the Act didn’t include them
in its category, while balloons and stuffed croco-
diles, as things that the Act would almost certainly
forget to enumerate, would be anybody’s property.
The effect of the decision is to deal a vital blow at
the Arbitration Act. There is no reason why a
union should register under the Act when it gets
thereby only disabilities and no privileges. There
is no reason why any worker should join a union
registered under the Act. And there is every
reason why workers, if urged thereto by grievance,
should resort to the old disastrous remedy of the
strike and the riot. It is a bitter disappointment
to find the High Oourt of Australia* taking up tins
attitude in regard to Industrial Arbitration. Only
an unconscious bias towards the ideas of the rich
can explain its decisions—all legally defensible,
but all logically questionable.

The First Bulwark.
The case for Protection was, from the economic
view-point, as clear as possible in Australia so
soon as the falling-off in the gold rushes made
available a surplus of population, and faced the
continent with the problem of maintaining itself
without the aid of nuggets. There was then as
clear a certaintv as there is now that a flourishing
White Australia was only possible by the growth
of manufactures. But new political developments
have from time to time cmne forward to re-inforce
the economic argument in impressing upon Aus-
tralia the necessity of a self-reliant policy. Ihe latest
is the sudden upgrowth in the nortli of an aggressive
war-power which brings the Yellow Peril within
sight of this generation. The victorious Japanese
guns have thundered nothing more certainly than
a warning to Australia. The Russian has stood
in the path of the Mongol wave and been over-
whelmed. Australia may be the next to meet the
brunt; and most surely is towards the head of the
list of those who stand in the way of the new
warrior people, and must prepare to withstand
them or perish. The first necessary step of that
preparation is industrial rather than military
—the establishment of a local iron industry.
An iron industry would mean providing the
first essentials of war —the iron and steel
from which are made guns and ships, rail-
ways to carry troops and stores, locomo-
tives, and other essentials. Without iron-
works no nation could hope ta carry on
a modern war. With them- —carried to the point
at which the highest products of iron are evolved—
and with brave men, all things are possible. At the
outbreak of war the seas would probably be sealed
against imports. Even the obsolete and discarded
gun of the British Army could not be imported for
the Australian soldier. With iron and steel works,
and with our own iron ore deposits opened up,
and with the allied industries of gun foundries and
ship-building yards in full working order, and
with men, all the raw materials of defence are to
hand. The iron industry would provide the ma-
terial for arms, and also to some extent the men.
Australia imports now over £3,000,000 worth of
iron products a year, and that sum represents, in
the main, labor. The constituents of steel
are, in the rough, almost valueless, not
much higher up in the grade of worth than
road-metal. It is labor which gives them
price Iron ore is of so little value in situ that its
worth at the puddling furnace is very little more
than the cost of digging and handling. A highly-
finished product of iron ore, such as a watch-spring
or a knife-blade, is to be valued by the ounce. The

♦
“ The Yeoman of the Guard ” is, from a
musical and story point of view, the best of Gilbert
and Sullivan’s great series of works. Its music is
worthy of grand opera (a pity that “grand opera”
is rarely so sensible); its dialogue has a poetic
quality suggestive in places of Shakspeare; but it
has not humor to anything like the degree of
“Pinafore” or “The Mikado;” and so it makes
great demands on the cast without helping the
actors with much material for raising an easy
laugh. “ The Yeoman,” to be satisfactorily inter-
preted, calls for a company equal to Yerdi’s works.
The “Repertoire C 0.,” which staged the piece at
Sydney Royal on Saturday night, has given some
charming revivals of Gilbert and Sullivan’s plays,
and the presentations have been as a rule ade-
quate ; but in this case the co. is hardly equal to
its task. The orchestra, under August Juncker,
and the chorus are perfect, illustrating again the
wide dissemination of musical talent among
Australians: but the principals in some instances
fail.
* » # *
Notable exceptions are Kenningham, who,
as Colonel Fairfax, sings like an angel and acts
almost a 3 well; and Miss Aggie Thorne, whose
Phoebe is distinguished by dainty acting and
sympathetic singing. Of course, too, the veteran
Howard Vernon scores as the gruesome gaoler.
But Miss Dolly Castles’ voice is not quite equal to
the part of Elsie Maynard, and the extremely
difficult part of Jack Point makes too heavy
demands on that promising junior, H. H. Wallace.
Notwithstanding, the revival is altogether wel-
come. There is enough of glee, song and concerted
music in “The Yeoman of the Guard” to
minimise individual deficiencies, and there has
been so much of the pitiful trash of musi-
cal comedy of late that a, Gilbert and
Sullivan show suggests coming out from
a George Reid meeting—where wit has been flailed
with a bladder of lard—to look at the moonshine
on a great expanse of water, or the shadows on a
great mountain, or the dancing tops of great trees,
or anything that isn’t fat and common and loudly
insistent and tin-kettly. Gilbert’s operas could
not be so badly played as to make them unattrac-
tive, and the Repertoire Co. never plays badly.
All that can be accused against it is that it ought
to play better.
** * *
“Naughty Nancy,” staged for the first
time in Sydney on Monday night, is made up of
many things, There is in it a school for girls—
called “Eden House.” A beautiful walled garden
in which beautiful school-girls of the magnetic
age congregate, a Gorgon lady principal named
Martha, a tubby little man masquerading in
female attire, much farcical music, a very attrac-
tive ballet, much jumble, in which the
story loses itself, and much comic work from
the orchestra when it imitates motor-car noises.
The night of washing day is an unusual one for a
first appearance, and suggests that the scenery
was being painted on Sunday, or that Mr. Sheri-
dan’s gowns hadn’t come home. However, at the
opening performance the paint seemed dry.
Miss Maud Amber didn’t carry away any of
the ancestral oaks on her white jewelled
frock, and Miss Watts-Phillips’ orange
wig was not exaggerated by taking up
the sunlight that frescoes the porch. And as
Mr. Sheridan’s skirts are of the kind that might
be borrowed off the nearest charwoman, the play
could hardly have been waiting for his begemmed
wardrobe to come home, so any delay may be
set down to Providence or Cussedness. In any
case, it doesn’t matter, but things that do matter,
like immortality and the National Debt, are so
much written about that it’s only fair to the
doesn’t-matters to give them a line occasionally.
Martha—doesn’t that name, for Scriptural
reasons, suggest the narrow things of the
house—is played by Miss Watts - Phillips,
and everything she does is well done. Mr.
Sheridan, who doubles the roles of Stiggs, a
company promoter, and Matilda Jones of the
Woman’s Rights Party, has only the memory of
his Widow O’Brien for rivalry. His lecture on
Bold, Bandy-legged, Bald-headed Man was
recognised on Monday as a funny old friend
of the audience, which gave it a glad hand,
and would willingly have asked the lecturer
out to look at a street hydrant. Miss Heba
Barlow, as Naughty Nancy, does her flirt-
ing, dancing and singing with elfish vivacity;
and a new-comer, Miss Waldon-Taylor, a Queens-
land girl with a pleasant voice and a good stage
appearance, plays another flirt’s part as Lady
Barbara Dawn. The impish Little Gulliver per-
vades the piece ; and the interpolation of a coon
serenade by Mr. Winfield Blake, and an imitation
of grand opera by this artist and Miss Maud
Amber, are things calculated to make glad
the public soul. A new bill, “The Earl and the
Girl,” is under rehearsal.
“When Other Lips and Other Hearts,”
&c., is the air that runs through “East Lynne”—
the play that is now lamenting at Sydney Palace.
It laments with the persistency of a spook haunt-
ing the bad man who murdered the solid part of
him and turned him loose in a cold, unfeeling
corridor and a damp cellar. Still, appreciative
audiences listen to it nightly as Miss Duggan,
in the capacity of Lady Isabel, sings it to
her husband, and when she has forgotten all about
it the orchestra fills in spaces with it, and the
damsel who rustles the paper bag says to her
friend, “ I think all the airs in ‘ Maritana ’ per-
fectly divine.” The tearful drama continues to
draw tears successfully, and the principals and
subordinates do their best with what is just a
dramatised Sob.

The four-leaved shamrock still flourishes
at Sydney Her Majesty’s, where Andrew Mack,
as Tom Moore, continues to show with what ease
and grace an Irishman can make love, and sing,
and jest in either rags or gorgeous trappings, and
stave off an angry landlady who wants the rint.

The man who interrupts a good story to
tell a better one is still the man in the large type
in the Tivoli bills. Fred. Bluett, Winton,
M'Ginty, Maud Beatty, Adson and Thorn, and
other people wear well, and continue to make a
show which never gets quite as high as Mt.
Everest, nor quite as low as G-. Reid’s spirits after
A. Deakin had made his Ballarat speech.

If “The Fires of St. John,” which met
with patient forbearance at Her Majesty’s (Melb.)
last Saturday night, was “presented with Elec-
trifying Success during Miss Nance O’Neil’s
Recent Triumphal Season in Boston, U.5.A.,” it
is the duty of all good Australians to feel sorry for
Boston. The translation of Sudermann’s play
that Miss Nance O’Neil presents may be a bald
translation, lacking certain suggestions of dim
debateable meanings that appealed to the Suder-
mann cult in the German original, but it is, one
supposes, at least a truthful statement of the plot.
The mechanism must be Sudermann’s, and judged
by its mechanism alone “ The Fires of St. John ” is
a pretentious piece of nonsense. A stream of small
talk flows sluggishly through the first act, bearing
on its bosom a note of interrogation. The audi-
ence is practically requested to wonder when the
story will begin. In the second act a drunken old
woman promises to cause trouble, but the pro-
mise remains unfulfilled. In the third act the
heavy father of the piece comes to high words
with his prospective son-in-law—and the alter-
cation ends in smoke. A drivelling pastor—the
“Magda” pastor, or his twin brother—hangs
around the proceedings, and “proposes” to the
heroine. She accepts him in the first place and
rejects him afterwards, nevertheless the parson is
a person of no more consequence than the casual
man-servant who comes along from time to time
with a muttered allusion to the drunken old
female or a joyous remark concerning beer.
* » * *
There are three characters in the story.
Melbourne has already met them a dozen times
under more entertaining circumstances. A colorless
girl is betrothed to a commonplace man, and her elder
sister (foster-sister in this play) unselfishly re-
frains from upsetting the arrangement. She
allows the colorless girl to go off with the man
whilst she—the self-sacrificer—strikes an appro-
priate attitude of despair. Curtain ! As a rule,
the three parties to this transaction are strictly
virtuous. The one claim to originality in Suder-
mann’s play lies in the misbehaviors of the elder
sister and the man. They are left together at
night in the same atmosphere of improbability—-
impossibility—which robbed the seduction scene
in Tolstoi’s “Resurrection ” of all critical human
interest. The elder sister seizes her chance to
show the gentleman how much she loves
him, and the scene is positively totter-
ing on the verge of impropriety, when a
great darkness comes to pass, and the drop curtain
falls upon the darkness. What happens in the
darkness has been previously symbolised by the
Fires of St. John, which are annual bonfires,
ihese misdemeanants enjoy a first annual bonfire
on the night before the man’s wedding, and next
morning they decide to let the marriage take place
as though nothing had happened. The tempestuous
spinster is willing to give her new lover to her
silly foster-sister instead of keeping him for her-
self. The lover, who is supposed to be a sturdy
independent fellow, consents to the double
sacrifice, and the wedding bells ring for Gertie.
* * * *
In apology for Sudermann’s play as pre-
sented by Miss Nance O’Neil and co. it may be
admitted that the tone of the performance, in its
serious moments, is highly unnatural. Miss
O Neil, as the gloomy, amorous elder sister, is
less unsatisfactory than the two new male actors
°f the co. M‘Kee Rankin is cheery, but some-
what indistinct, as an uneventful father, and the
lady who impersonates the heroine’s disreputable
mother may be commended for her shrieking off
the stage. When she talks (on the stage) the lad' v
is uncommendable. A revival of “Elizabeth”
will take place when “ The Fires ” go out, and in
thecharacter of the loud, red-haired monarch Miss
O Neil will be expected to settle the vexed
question as to whether she can talk as tremen-
dously as of yore.

Bland Holt changes his bill at Melbourne

son who has lately been figuring on the Royal

serious job at Fitzgerald’s Circus building.
Bland’s forthcoming event is “The Flood Tide,”
a Drury Lane farcical drama that came in at the
tail end of his season last year, and was with-
drawn before its popularity had lost the bloom of
youth.
# * *
Hugo Heermann, the masterly violinist
who has stamped his name upon Melbourne’s
musical soul, will make his fourth and final appear-
ance at the Town Hall next Saturday night. Last
Saturday there were two Heermanns on the
platform, Emil, the clever son of Hugo, playing
second fiddle in a concerto and making a further
appearance on his own.
* * #
At the Opera House a capital novelty in
“ turns ” is provided by the silent performance of
The Modern Milo and Cruikshank the Carica-
turist. The Milo represents a series of shapely
stone goddesses on a pedestal, and every time a
goddess disappears the entertainment rests with
Cruikshank, who draws lightning caricature
portraits, which appear in magnified white strokes
against a dark background on a sheet. The
Gotham trio is another recent and bright item in
the Rickards show. Otherwise the programme
makes lavish announcements of Last Nights.
# * *
An Adelaide correspondent:—
“ The Walls of Jericho ” had eventually to be put on at
Adelaide Royal without Herbert Flemming. Brough took
the leading part of the man from Queensland who has
sickened of London in three or four years, his own part
(Cayley Brummie to the other’s Tanqueray) being handed
over to a fairly-good junior. This is a big loss. Brough,
as the rougher diamond, should be great. As the partly-
polished one he has a fine part, and plays it for all it is
worth. The play is going to be a big success in Australia,
where every patriotic citizen who sees it can thank Heaven
that he is not as these Londoners are. Sutro shows as a
born playwright still lacking in experience. He has a
great eye for stagecraft and for a telling situation.
The weak part of the play is its ending, two im-
portant points being thrown at the audience with-
out being adequately led up to. The play introduced
Beatrice Day, who had no part in “Beauty and the
Barge.” She is somewhat on GeraldineOlliffe lines, hand-
some, rather massive, soundly emotional, richly humorous,
altogether cut out for the part of the frivolous wife with
an affectionate disposition (towards the man from Queens-
land) underneath. The young people are chiefly Carter
Pickford and Winifred Fraser, who are both very good to
look upon. So far, they have done the light' work en-
trusted to them to perfection. Miss Temple has been
effective as two ladies of uncertain age. Mrs. Brough is
to be seen later, in “ Niobe ” or otherwise.

COLUMN
On the whole, Australasian banking profits were
rather smaller in the last 12 months for which figures
are now available than in the previous 12 months. Banks
took risks during- the drought, charged high rates, and,
as it happened, had then two good seasons to wipe the
risk out. The good seasons have now made people more
independent of the money-lender, and the money-lender’s
profits sag in consequence. The following summarises
the situation for the year :

The Bank of New Zealand disposed of its profits this
way:—£179,022 to the Assets Trust, to make good losses
incurred in the days before Ballanee and Seddon started
to save the country by doing the things which the Aus-
tralian Tory party says it would be disastrous to do;
£BO,OOO in interest on a £2,000,000 State loan, half of
which has now been put into the bank as capital; £12,500
to provide for various old losses; £513 to ordinary
reserves. The Australasia wrote off £39,000, the Union
£35,000, and the Commercial of Australia £15,521. The
Queensland National wrote off £19,645, repaid the State
£3OOO and private depositors £15,000. If other banks
wrote off anything, they charged the amount to profit
and loss, and wrote it off before showing profit.

The Bank of New Zealand figures for 1904-5 were recently
made available, but the Assets Board figures, which have
to be read with them, are not yet forthcoming, wherefore
the figures above are those for 1902-3 and 1903-4 respec-
tively. When this bank got into its mess in 1893-4, it
bailed up the State for £2,000,000, at 4 p.c. If that
ranked as capital, the institution would show a return
of £lO 18s. per cent. The Queensland National figures
stand high in the list for much the same reason. It
held £954,874 of non-interest-bearing fixed deposits
and 4* millions of very cheap fixed deposits. If
it paid ordinary interest it would show something
under 4 per cent. Banks which have increased their
dividends during the year are : National (Maoriland),
from 10 to 12 p.c.; National (Tasmania"), sto 6 ; Ballarat,
6to 7. The City of Sydney has cut down its div. from 4
to 2* p.c. The A.J.S., whose figures show a great change,
has written down its capital from £1,168,042 to £154,528.
The Aust. Deposit and Mortgage, of Melbourne, has also
had a long-deferred cleaning-up, writing capital down
from £909,979 to £198,946. On the new capital the profit
would represent £2 19s. 6d. per cent.

The London was thus the only bank which paid away
more than it earned, trenched on its reserves, and thus
reduced its own future earning' power. The size of the
distribution ought, no doubt, to be considered partly in
the light of existing reserves, which are shown in a pre-
vious table. In most cases it will be found that the worst
offenders in the way of excessive dividends are those
which have the poorest reserves. The last half-dozen,
anyway, are in that category. Considering the
size of their reserves, the bright examples are the
National (Maoriland) and the Westralia ; though, as these
two nevertheless pay the largest dividends in the list,
their position seems to be mainly due to the extraordinary
prosperity of Maoriland and Westralia.

terest earnings, suggests that there is still a very heavy
dead-weight on the books. Certainly, though it has this
vast amount of deposits compared with shareholders’
funds, the non-interest-bearing deposits are very small;
but, taken as a whole, the figures mean that the securities
are so poor that they can barely earn their own interest.
All new business in this bank is, however, fully

The ordinary dividend of 10 per cent, is paid, with a bonus
of Is. per share on the 20,000 shares of 10s. each. On
capital and reserves the earnings were at the extra-fine
rate of £l7 2s. lOd per cent, per annum ; there
are only two other Australasian trustee offices in
that class. In three years the return on funds has
doubled, and in five years it has nearly trebled,
and the improvement has been mainly through com-
missions. The trust accounts on hand amount to only
£249,053, a twofold increase in three years. Still, m spite
of the fact that some of the Westralian charges are a bit
higher than the corresponding charges on this side, the
trustee business must be helped along very considerably
by the profitable agent-for-absentee business which
flourishes in the absentee-ridden West. The co.’s invest-
ments are mainly premises (£12,963), mortgages (£2150),
and cash (£4220), and the whole of these lumped together
seem to be yielding something over 8 per cent. The co.
isn’t building up its reserves very fast; but so far as they
go they seem a solid lot on which to hang a trust or a
power of attorney. # # »

N.S. Wales and Victoria (J per cent, rise) have anti-Labor
Governments ; Queensland (2 per cent, rise) has a half-
Labor Government, Westralia (3 per cent, rise) has a whole
Labor Government. That’s Cohen’s practical answer to
the wild yarn that foreign investors look with alarm and
dread upon the progress of the “ Socialistic ” party in
Australia.
* • * *
Some elaborate ass, in drawing up a recent
mortgage in Sydney, is alleged to have made careful pre-
paration for a new slander on the Labor party by getting a
clause inserted which laid down that the mortgage was
not to be paid off in debased currency. Then a conveni-
ent friend wrote wildly to the Sydney dailies the very day
the document was signed, to ask if this was another evi-
dence of the popular alarm at the “trend of Socialistic
legislation,” or some such sloppy verbiage—the Tory is
dreadfully fond of “trend.” The S.M. Herald, which
has been speaking reproachfully of late about “Slanders
on Australia,” gave publicity to the childish conspiracy in
its columns, and said with the gravity of an owl that it
“ hoped the fears that prompted the insertion of such a
clause were ungrounded.” Yet even the Herald must
surely have sense enough to know that it is 10,000 to 1
there were no “ fears ” in the case, and that the
clause was merely put in that it might be published
as another sham evidence of how the Labor party was
terrorising capital. It was obviously of no earthly use,
except as a silly joke of that sort. Such a proviso can
give no protection to anybody, for if George Reid’s
Government, or any other Government, debases the
coin, it will naturally enact that the debased coin
shall be accepted as ‘full legal tender, and that all
documents to the contrary shall be null and void.
The man who sent in the fearful news said that the clause
was inserted by “ a well-known firm of solicitors. If he
had really wanted to know whether it was “ the outcome
of the Socialistic craze,” he would, presumably, have asked
the “well-known solicitors,” seeing that the}'were the
persons who had the information —not the Herald.
Possibly, however, the reason why he didn’t ask the
“ well-known solicitors ” was that neither they nor the
alleged clause had any existence.

In addition to the profit made in the past year as
shown above, £2500 has been set aside “ for repairs to
business premises ** \ this is in addition to £3073 written
off. For the present this £2500 appears as a sort of extra
reserve, though it isn’t shown in the table, because it
most likely represents depreciation which has already
occurred. The accounts disclose very tew striking move-
ments tor the year; the most striking is in the matter of
profits, and that appears to be due to increased wool
business. Liabilities are practically unaltered; and the

1894. 1904. 1905.

After paying interest on debenture stock a profit remains
at the rate of £3 2s. sd. p.c. on shareholders’ capital; and
a few years ago it didn’t look as though anything like
that was going to happen. But, even if the whole of the
present profit is sent on to the “ primary ” reserve, the
latter will still be a good way off the minimum size ac
which the last reconstruction provided that dividends
may be paid. This reserve must first reach £75,000 before
shareholders get anything ; after that 25 p.c. of profits
have to go to a secondary reserve, the £75,000 has to be
maintained, and then the shareholders may get 3 p.c. ; if
anything 1 is left the “ B ” debenture stockholders take
1 p.c., and it takes £7277 to pay 1 p.c. on that stock. The
chance of shareholders getting 3 p.c. isn’t, then, very im-
mediate, and the chance of their getting more than 3 p.c.
is horribly remote. But, even so, the shareholders might
have done worse. Some of them, were let out of a huge
liability, and, from one point of view, that was equal to a
huge dividend, paid at the expense of people who didn t
stand to make the very large profits that shareholders at
one time stood to make.

This is one of those “ tariff-fed monopolies” that the
Freetrade papers talk about, though it isn’t a monopoly,
and though nobody has observed any increase m the
price of salt since the tariff began to help it. While the
tariff was new the co. did pretty well, until the importer
got his breath; then he discovered that he could sell
salt a good deal cheaper than he had been doing
(in other words, the public hadn’t, under Freetrade,
been getting goods at alleged “Freetrade prices”).
Instead, therefore, of putting up prices under Protec-
tion, he began to lower them : as the co. reports, com-
petition from abroad has been keener, and selling prices
have, consequently, been depressed, so as to make profit
somewhat less than that of last year.” On capital and
reserves, the net profit showed a return of £6 18s. 6d.
p c • and even then it isn’t certain that enough has been
written off plant, &c., which in this business perishes at
an appalling rate. In the past five years shareholders
have averaged 4f- p.c., which can hardly be called bloated-
monopoly figures ; and it is obvious from the trend of the
figures that the present div. is not justified. During the
year the co. has reduced its overdraft and current debts
from £13,035 to £10,390, mainly by reducing stocks ; and
that is one satisfactory feature, anyhow. The assets in-
clude a goodwill item of unknown size, lumped with all
the rest: and there isn’t any evidence that this paper
asset is being got rid of. While it is there, the reserves
can only be regarded as nominal.

The black Labor party is notoriously unscrupu-
lous as to its facts. The last report of the Victorian
Mines Department, after giving instances of Victorian
mines that are paying dividends on averages of ljdwt.,
3dwt., and 4dwt., says : “In view of such facts, comment
on the absurdity of the following in the January number
of the Review or Reviews is needless * In Australia,
where gold is a white man’s industry, ore that does not
yield an ounce to the ton is hardly worth mining. In the
Rand, with cheap coal and cheap labor, they can work
ore at a profit when it.yields only 9dwt. to the ton.’”

But the reserves have to some extent a suspicious
look, and the profit shown above doesn’t seem the
best sort of profit either. The co. has a “Replace-
ment and Reparation Fund,” which now stands at £7055,
and is included in the reserves shown above. Apparently
this is intended to buy a new wash-jug when the cracked
handle comes off finally ; and if the jug is cracked already
the fund is really of the nature of a writing-off. While this
reflects upon the reserves to the extent of £7055, it also
reflects upon the past year’s profits to the extent of £1059,
latter being the amount added to the Fund out of profits.
This addition is at the rate of about 5 per cent, on the
book-value of the furniture ; and a 5 per cent, writing-off
from the book-value of hotel furniture doesn’t look exces-
sive, even though it does cut net profits down from £2OlB
to £959. Latter on capital alone is at the rate of £3 2s.
per cent. ; and if the figures are no better than that, it
doesn’t look as though any of the reserves are very real.
The co.’s freehold is down in the balance-sheet for
£102,990 : this carries a load of £62,000 of debt, apparently
at 4 per cent, or a trifle less—a huge load, more than twice
as large as the capital, notwithstanding that it is being
steadily but slowly reduced.
* * * *
The “London syndicate” which, since Lionel
Robinson took a train-load of brokers and speculators to
Broken Hill, has been haunting Barrier mining co.s’
offices with offers and options bulging out of its pockets,
has now secured a call on Round Hill. It proposes to
form a new co., in 200,000 shares of £1 each, and put up
£30,000 as working capital. Present owners are a small
syndicate, which has been pottering about for a few
months ; before that the big hungry-looking hill had been
lyin"- idle for 13 years, held under that blastiferous title,
mineral conditional purchase. It is described as being
“at Broken Hillbut, so far as prospecting has shown,
it has no connection with the Broken Hill lode. Silver
and lead can be got in it in numerous veins and streaks,
just as they can be got in 200 other hills on the Barrier,
and whether there is any more metal in this hill
than in the 200 others is at least doubtful. The whole
thing is pure gamble, with the chances largely against
anything profitable. There will, however, be enough
metal to string on the plungers and make an occasional
market with. Even in the old days some people managed
to make money out of Round Hill.
* • * •
Broken Hill Junction North is still in diffi-
culties. Manager Stockdale has resigned, unable to get
along with the local directors, whom he accuses of inter-
ferino- too far in the working of the mine. Present inten-
tion is to put anything up to 800 tons through the mill
weekly. This quantity can, no doubt, be supplied, but a
good part of it will be found to be refractory rhodonite
stuff, and costs will be high and extraction low.
* • * *
Barrier stocks have, with one or two exceptions,
had big jumps lately. Share dealers will have to look
out; for the movement has been made, not by the mines,
but by the market. , * ,
Burragorang (N.S.W.) silver-lead mines make
headway, in spite of having to drag their ores over
45 miles of the roughest roads in the State before they
reach rail at Picton. Five years’ output (net value re-
ceived from smelters) has been:—l9oo, £9125 ; 1901,
£11,000; 1902, £18,373 ; 1903, £18,304; 1904, £32,068.
The Yarranderie (formerly Hilder’s) is the busiest show,
owned by a Sydney co.; capital, £8437, in 6000 shares
paid to £1 and 5000 now paid to 13s. 9d. Calls are being
made on latter shares, and the money put on fixed
deposit, which at date of last balance (Oct., 1904)
amounted to £1405. In 1902 co. paid 2s. in divs., in 1903
another 2s. Last year six divs. were paid, amounting to
4s. 3d. per share —which is the best showing of any Aus-
tralian co.-owned silver mine for the year, taking paid
capital into account. Next best-known mine on the field
is J. V. Bartlett’s; owner is supposed to be making a
nice dry bit out of it. Still, with communication so
bad and costly, the field can’t be worked in the most
desirable way, and a lot of picking is done. The veins
lend themselves to this sort of thing; they are very
irregular, both as to size and quality. Richest portion is
fine-grained galena, lying close to bands of quartz, where
small bunches of native silver are occasionally found.
Last) year’s output from the whole field averaged just
upon 3dwt. gold and 70oz. silver per ton and 12 per cent,
lead ; but on account of the picking, this gives no true
idea of the general value of the lodes. A good deal of
prospecting is being done in the district, which seems
well worth it.
* # * *
J. Bull’s Scottish-Australian Co., which owns
the Lambton and Burwood mines, Newcastle (N.S.W.), is
having a pretty cold time now. It used to pay 10 to 20
per cent., but in 1903 it paid only 3 per cent., and last
year only at the rate of 2J per cent, for the first half, and
nothing at all in the second, when it showed a loss of
£4545. Directors’ report, of course, blames labor-costs
chiefly—“ wages remained at the level fixed when the
value of coal was considerably higher ”; and, if market
conditions don’t improve, one of two things must happen
—either the miners must take lower wages, or the mines
must shut up, when miners will take no wages at all.
And there is only one certain way to improve market
conditions, and that is by improving the Australian
demand. Australia can’t make certain of improving
the foreign demand, simply because it can’t control
the foreign supply; but it can enormously increase
the Australian demand by smelting here the iron which
at present is smelted in foreign parts, and building the
machinery here that is now built in foreign forges ; and
making here the cottons, woollens and silks that our
people now get from foreign parts. It can do that in one
act by putting up the tariff on all manufactured goods.
If Australia doesn’t commit itself to that, then there
seems no hope for the Newcastle miner except either
lower wages or no wages at all. Nothing is more certain,
anyway, than that a co. won’t continue making 1J per
cent, one half-year, nothing at all the next, and a loss of
£4545 the third.

Re the cancelling of the Mousetrap lease,
Wyalong (N.S.W.). The Warden’s report, before the
cancellation, affirmed that prior to a contract being put in
hand for sinking a main shaft, some three months ago,
the property was worked, or rather scratched, on the sur-
face by a number of old-age pensioners. If true, quite a
new way of complying with labor^covenants.^

dividends, £30,000 odd. Winze .below 200 ft. is on very

of rich ore is still going down, and may yield a lot of
stone yet.

Westralia Waihi is again looking “ dicky.” For
March, co. made a profit of £2OO, for April £lOO ; for May
there is a loss of £lOO. This means calls to explore pro-
perty at depth of 280 ft. Outlook is anything but hopeful,
and future of co. depends on how new east reef pans out.
Even manager Watts can’t make a gold-mine out of a
diorite hill.
* * * *
North Kalgurli (W.A.) can hardly be expected
to go on much longer. May return showed a loss of £lOO,
in spite of 1000 tons gold slimes, returning 4dwt., being
included. Without these slimes yield would drop to about
235. 6d. a ton, against costs of 315., showing a dead loss of
7s. 6d. a ton, or, say, £5OO to £6OO a month. Co. is said
to have £15,000 cash in hand, but, even so, outlook is
gloomy unless a fresh property can be secured.
Hannan’s Star, Kalgoorlie, seems going from
bad to worse, and treatment plant may shortly close down.
During past three months average yield has been 7'3, 5-6,
and 4 ; 9dwt., showing £3OO profit, £lOO profit, and £5OO loss.
This sort of thing can’t go on indefinitely. Better things
are hoped for in Morrison’s lode, but that will-o’-the-wisp
has been sought for for over five years fruitlessly, and
there seems no reason to hope that good ore will be found
now.
* * * *
South New Moon, Bendigo, has dropped from
the pinnacle it occupied, and is now below the New
Moon. But there is no fear of it petering out to the ex-
tent the Queen Cross Reef did at Charters Towers, as it
has big ore reserves. What has happened is that the
grade of stone has got down, and divs. are not likely to be
so high as during the early part of the past 12 months.
New Moon has come out with a 4s. div., but such
disbursements must be regarded as exceptional.
South New Moon has paid in _ all £357,000, or
£ll 3s. 6d. per share. Stone is coming from
500, 580, 980, 1060, 1140, and 1220 feet levels.
What is termed a fine lot of quartz remains at the two
first-named levels, and there are big blocks of stone above
the others. Costs last half-year ran into 17s. per ton,
of which 11s. went for wages and 2s. 6d. for battery treat-
ment. Co. has shaft down to 1500 ft., and is about to go
down another 100 ft. to prospect at 1480 and 1580 ft.
* * * *
Specks of gold have been seen in a new make of
quartz in New Chum Railway mine, Bendigo, at 4200 ft.
This is a little deeper than where co. previously alighted
on a little quartz and a few colors. So down the winze is
to go again, and another crosscut put out to see if the
mine is on to golden stone at last. It is hot work in the
bottom, and there isn’t much to boast about in working
there, even though there may be the honor of sweating at
the deepest point in any of the world’s gold mines.
It is all very nice for Mr. T. Lees-Field, member
of the London board of the Victorian Cornish, Daylesford,
to pipe a lay to Vic. shareholders, who want their
balance-sheets, about shortness of cash and reconstruc-
tion. There has been plenty of time to issue a statement
that would have satisfied all. Worst omission was to
carry through re-organisation without submitting details
to the local holders. Lees-Field—beautiful name—kindly
says that every penny earned is carried to the credit of
the co. That is not the point. What shareholders want
to know is how the money go;

• * * *
No great cheer is to be derived from the reading
of the Briseis yearly report. It is so brutally frank as to
confound some of the Melbourne bull crowd, who can’t
understand why everything should be told. Thus it is
related that a slice of the heavy basalt on the Briseis Hill
has subsided, owing to the collapse of the Brothers No. 1
underground workings. In plain English, this means that
in the old days a big hole was made in the drift
by the miners, and it is just this fly in the No. 1
ointment that spoils its virtue. Figures in Lindsay
Clarke’s report demonstrates how tremendously out were
the original Stock Exchange puffers of the Briseis. Thus
the general average for the year was only sJlb. per cub.
yd. , instead of the predicted 1 per cent. Even the bottom
at the No. 1 Ringarooma face only went lOflb., while In
the east end the lower drifts stopped at lb. No. 2on
the other hand only averaged 8-841 b. and No. 3 face
1'221b., while at the Briseis Hill 31b. was not reached. It
looks more than ever as if the average would be what
Hawley mentioned in his later reports—about 51b.
Good points about the show are that the bulk of the
rocky overburden is gone, that 51b. will pay handsomely,
that co.'s water scheme gives it control of the North
Ringarooma, and that the basin under the hill at the
south end is widening while the drift yields well. Still
600,000 shares are a stunning lot on which to pay divs.

jug of water. The O.K. copper show is booming tempo-
rarily and has added zest to Willcocks’ normal enthusiasm.
His syndicate-cwra-Government exploration work at Clon-

thusiasm, however, reaches high-water mark when he
says that “ Me. Garnet will be heard of before long.” He
proposes to tackle the mine again in a couple of months
with a drill. Considering the money his firm sank in the
Mt. Garnet railway, exploration with drill is perhaps ad-
visable as a last chance. But Willcocks’ seems quite
haDPV about it. He certainly deserves success.

The British Broken Hill Silver Mining Co.,
after careful consideration of all types of boilers, have
placed an order with Babcock and Wilcox for three large
boilers, each of 500 h.p. Superheaters, chain grate
mechanical stokers and economiser haye also been
ordered from the same firm. This now makes 40 boilers
of the Babcock and Wilcox type supplied to the various
mines and industries at Broken Hill. Wherever economy
in fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. Hence the recent unanimous decision of the
Expert Admiralty Committee to install Babcock boilers as
the standard for the British Navy.*

The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long' again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discrintion of cast or wrought iron work.

“Timbarra”: The emu is a first-rate gold
accumulator (! Ed. Bulletin). Saw a miner shoot one,
recently, and pan off nearly 2oz. of coarse gold from the
contents of its crop and stomach (!! Ed. Bulletin). It is
a common thing in surface-gold country to see fellows out
“ emu panning ” (!! ! Ed. Bulletin). Some good runs of
gold are picked up by following the leads given by the
quartz obtained from the birds (Mill! Ed. Bulletin).

Edward Langton, chairman of Melbourne
.Public Library trustees, lately told what he
doubtless thought a good “ red-tape ” yarn. The
trustees wanted an assistant to the sailorman who
cleans the skylights, and had chosen an outsider,
when the Public Service Board vetoed him and
sent a man who was properly qualified by exam-
ination, but otherwise as useless as a child. He
immediately tumbled from aloft and was killed.
It wasn t a bad tale, for Langton, but it turned
out that some one had been pulling that regrettable
person s leg, for the assistant sent by the Board
had done his work all right, and was still doing it,
fallen down Ve * qUlte unm j ured ' and hadn’t
“ K.K.” : What’s all this growl about
Empire Day ? I enjoyed it immensely. The
school procession passed down our street. At the
Japanese laundry they had two flags up, the
Union Jack and the IVTonkey Banner entwined.
Ihe whole staff stood inside and smiled. The
European laundry opposite made no display
because the woman who used to run it was away
trying to collect the money owing to her by the
™ s ! .who had deserted her in favor of the Japs,
and her late staff was away trying to get another
job. As the procession passed the vegetable and
fruit shop, the Chow proprietor came outside and
said “Welly good ” three distinct times. At the
corner there was a slight check caused by an en-
thusiastic German and a drunken Dago sailor, who
had fallen out of the Italian wine shop and were
fighting in the road. But it was a great day. I
felt that the silken bonds had been drawn close.
In fact, if they had been drawn any closer and the
top end of them had been tied to a tree, I would
have been hanged.
* * • *
~ Though, on the authority of Darwin’s
“Origin of Species,” fish-ova possess such
amazing vitality that live fish drop from whirl-
winds in India, while ova are also successfully
carried in duckweed clinging to the feet of water-
birds, Bent’s railway, the other day, killed as
dead as a door-nail 1740 out of 1800 yearling trout,
which it vainly endeavored to convey in good
order the comparatively short distance from Bal-
larat to Bairnsdale (Vic.). Young fish are hardy,
but a Victorian railway truck quickly converts
them, like many other things, into perishable
products.

The bridegroom seems to have been so
small an incident in a Melbourne fashionable
wedding the other day that the Australasian
overlooked him altogether. It traced the pedi-
gree of the bride, and mentioned her clothes
down to the last 3in. of chiffon, then surveyed
the bridesmaids, and went into particulars
regarding the “best man,” and the wedding
presents right through to a pair of salt cellars and
a candlestickbut the bridegroom was the mere
speck in the distance that escaped notice.

“ Edyson ” : Seeing that the churches (the
.R 0. church in particular) insist that marriage is
purely a celestial business which no mundane
power can abrogate, isn’t it
up to the people to point out
to parson and priest that it
is incumbent on them to
make no more bad marriages ?
The ghostly person in the
cataract of black gown de-
clares that the marriage he
makes is ir-
revocable,
that it is
holy, that it
cannot b e
undone;
and yet he
is ready,
practically
at a mo-
ment’s no-
tice, to tie-
up, for a
small cash
considera-
tion, any
Jack and Jill,
quite regardless
of Jack’s suita-
bility for Jill or
Jill’s affinity for
Jack. Since the
reverend’s work
is everlasting and
no man may
undo it, the pul-
piteer who off-
handedly throws
together, for
ready money, two
people who are
often total strangers
, him, is a maker of
mischief and misery. Also
he is quite too casual; the
divorce court takes its eud of
the job much more seriously.
The conscientious parson who believes there is in
the eye of Heaven no escape from the marriages
he makes should make no marriages without
having first put forth every effort in his power to
satisfy himself that the couple will be a perfect
match, but so far all the parsons I have come in
contact with have been reckless promoters of
weddings quite regardless of consequences. If
marriages are made in heaven I am convinced it is
bad policy to have the fees paid on earth.

Judge Backhouse, two barristers, and two
solictors were engaged in Sydney District Court
!u te i y «° Ver a ten shlllln £ dispute. Counsel said
tha,t important points were involved,” and the
Judge retorted that the smaller the claim the
more important the points. The legal profession
has always a craving to settle some important
point, but after all nobody can eat or drink the
point, and it isn’t clothes. Only when eminent
counsel is adrift on a raft in mid-ocean with an
important point will the full unimportance of it
become apparent.

. The Mayor of Huddersfield (Eng.) has
impressed on his council the desirableness of
giving some Socialistic aid to the rearing of the
local infant. As a start, he personally offers £1 to
every youngster born, and reared to the age of 12
months, m the district he represents. The Council
« capped this by adopting a scheme whereby a
shilling is offered the person who first notifies the
council of any birth within 48 hours. Having
thus located the new infant, the health officer is to
call on it, and give the parents detailed advice
regarding its rearing. Two lady health officers
are also to be appointed, and will visit whenever a
birth occurs, and instruct the mother in proper
nursing, &c. The scheme culminates with the
establishment of a day nursery as a experiment,
and the distribution of special milk to infants
under 12 months old. If anyone suggested that
Sydney Council should appoint medical officers to
see that its possible citizens weren’t lost by their
parents’ ignorance or neglect, M‘Linen would rave
to the point of bursting.
• * * *
The best news this week is that the row
over the true meaning of Socialism has been
settled. ‘Socialism,” said the chairman of the

A Melbourne pressman writes :
The murder of Constable Johnston at St. Kilda, nearly
three years ago, was followed by a public subscription on
behalf of Mrs. Johnston and her children. About £l6O
was raised for widow Johnston by her sympathisers, and
thus far she is nothing- the richer for it. A few other
pounds remitted to a newspaper were duly handed over
to her, but that was a separate matter. She has not re-
ceived even an official account of the £l6O, and when she
applies to leading members of the committee she gets the
impression that she is regarded as a nuisance. The
reason given for withholding the subscription is that it
was got together to provide her with a home, and as it is
not sufficient to buy a habitable house, nothing can be
C|ol ?. < ;\, J'' lemone .V is to remain at compound interest
until it tote up to the price of a desirable villa residence,
Mrs. Johnston will be a good many years older ere she
begins to live rent free.
High police officials in Melbourne have
been endeavoring to introduce the system which
led to the undoing of Ko-lco in “The Mikado”—
she person whose office was threatened because of
insufficiency of executions. A constable on traffic
duty was called upon to explain why he prose-
cuted so few people for breach of regulations, and
—? explanation, that he endeavored to so run
things that the regulations wouldn’t be breached,
wa3 deemed insufficient. There was apparently
no evidence that furious driving or any other sin
wa,s going on, but the authorities held that the
policeman should summon somebody, whether
anybody deserved it or not, in order to justify hi 3
existence. He was just on the point of being
reduced to street duty when Chief-Secretary
Cillott interfered. As a matter of fact, the worst
blocks m Melbourne streets are caused by frantic
policemen stopping a long string of vehicles while
they take the name of some driver suspected of
breathing on the wrong side of the street.
THE GREAT SUPERLATIVE.
LECTURER: “And the scenery of that isle was so
superb, so perfectly glorious, so—er —er”
SMALL BOY (at back): “ Just say it was a boshter,
Mister!”
George Willis, the clerk who committed
suicide at Watson’s Bay, Sydney, will probably
serve as a text for the dreary temperance lecturer
for many a day. “ Died merrily—drunk as a
fiddle,” was the written message he left behind
him. Then lie took arsenic, and made things
doubly sure with a pistol, and after that his corpse
sat out on the rock all night and froze by way of a
further guarantee of its deadness. Yet a fiddle is
a sober implement, and if a man is no drunker
than that there is very little to complain of.
OELIMER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Gelimer, King of the Vandals,
In mountain-prison pent,
To Pharas, the Roman captain,
A message of sorrow sent.
He had been Lord of Carthage,
The splendid Daughter of Tyre,
And he wrote thus—“ Send me, O Pharas,
A loaf of bread and a lyre.”
He would comfort his heart that stifled
’Midst Moorish swine in a stye,
By singing of Genseric’s glories,
And his own good days gone by.
Gelimer, King of the Vandals,
Died in the ancient years ;
Yet his words so quaint and simple
Have filled mine eyes with tears.
And I think I will send this message,
His case being nearly mine—
“ I have a lyre—but send me
A loaf and a jar of wine.”
N.S.W.

Adelaide crematorium is having a long
rest, and for a year or more no one has evinced a
desire to frizzle his dear departed friend there into
fuller’s earth. At first the frying establishment
threatened to become fashionable, but in spite of
the efforts of certain doctors, who died and gave
the place a leg-up with their patronage the ten-
dency to cremation seems to have died out. x et,
as a method of disposing of the dead, it is clean,
healthy, in no way repugnant to the feelings, and
costs only £5. But cremation has to struggle against
ages of prejudice, fostered by tear-sodden literature
about how little Henry loved to visit his mother s
grave, by tales about “ the quiet God s acre,
and the yew-tree and the cypress therein, and by
creepy tales, like long sad caterpillars on the spine,
about ghosts, and body-snatchers, and corpse
lights, and things that sit on tombstones and
mo-e-o-o-o-o-oan. Then there is the opposition of
gravediggers, undertakers, mutes, cemetery trus-
tees, parsons, and weepy people who l°ye °
linger in the place of worms and spell out the
saddening inscription “ Sacred to the Memory o
J. Briggs.” So cremation has to skirmish on a
steep gradient.

A Paris newspaper has got off this re-
markable confusion of ideas: —
Athletics are carried to peculiar lengths in the British
colonies. Should the Australian cricket eleven now
landing in Maoriland, win the majority of the cricket
tournaments, every member could, on his
State employment at a large salary; but funding
of all, MrlG. Beid, the Australian Premier, is coming over
to swim from Dover to Calais.
If that misguided Parisian paper only knew the
amazing George it wouldn’t discuss the matter so
calmly. A two-headed statesman landing on
Calais sands, and saying “Oui” with one head and
“Non ” with the other, would be the most start-
ling invasion thereabouts, since the days w en
Louis Napoleon came ashore at Boulogne with, a
tame eagle on his shoulder and a bit of ham m
his hat.

“ S.S.” : Cookery classes have been estab-
lished in connection with the State schools
in Melbourne, and in the other centres too,
I presume, and I suppose that in the other
centres the fat man is as busy as he is in
the metropolis turning the circumstance to
his own
stomach’s ad-
vantage. The
fair, round
man who is
something of
a personage
is generally a
busy eater.
He is ever in
quest of free
lunches, free
dinners, and
free teas, and
the State
school cook-
ery classes
are employed
to fill his
aching va-
cancy for
food. You
read that
Or. Corpo,
Mayor of
this suburb
or that, has
taken a
choice little
Earty to see
o w the

read of the enthusiasm ex-
pressed by the good men
of the goodly company
over the surprising success
of the little students. But
it is not impressed upon
you that the Mayor and
his stout cronies have
waded through a big four-
course meal at the ex-
pense of the tax-
payers. This kind of
thing is continually
happening, and the
gorge is always pre-
pared allegedly to enable
the dear little girls to prove
their efficiency, although the real
fact is that the crowd which attends is obtaining
victuals under false pretences. If the abilities
of the children must be tested, why not let them
experiment upon somebody who needs a dinner ?

A crocodile was killed last week in the
Logan River, south of Brisbane. The croc, is
almost unknown so far down the map, but this
one was probably on his way to Sydney to see
another land agent, not knowing that he had
already sailed.
English ha’penny papers
are “starring” an alleged
Ballarat man, who, they say,
has “scoured three conti-
nents ” in search of his runa-
way daughter. The yarn is
that the girl, who is 17, left
for Melbourne, and a 40-year-
old friend of the family had
business there at the same
time. It was a fortnight
before the alleged stern Bal-
larat father found out what
had happened; then he lit
out with his heaviest boots
on. He reckoned he could
catch up to his old friend
and kick him quick, but the
heavy boots were evidently a
mistake, for the fly-aways
kept ahead of him through
S’Africa, U.S., England, and
France, and when he fell
into the hands of the re-
porters he was at Trieste,
and had missed them by
three days again. “He is a
strongly-built man of 45,”
says one. “If they are mar-
ried, my daughter can go her
own way. If not, I shall
thrash the man within an
inch of his life.” Probably
the European reporters have invented either part
Q f the yarn, or the whole man. Present writer
only invented his boots.

The Chinaman is a strangely elusive being.
When he gets into trouble he is never, by any
chance, the person mentioned on his signboard ;
ke never was that person, and he never expects to
be. He doesn’t know even why the sign is there;
personally he is a simple child of Nature gaping
mildly at the universe. In Melbourne, last week,
the law tried to argue a point with Sam Yick, but
he swore that, though that was the name over his
door he was really Ah Hung Then it went on
to the case of Sara Wah, who declared the t,
though his business sign was Sam Wah, he was
really Sam Yick. The lawfined them 10s. apiece,
with , d«k suspicion in its mind that the, were
both Oat. They both looked as if the, might
be anything.

Melbourne Herald, in reprinting articles
from the local Morning Herald of 50 years back
is presumably going over much of the ground that
“Garryowen” covered with his Chronicles of
Early Melbourne.” But the reprints are none the
less interesting to the generation that has arisen
since “Garryowen” Finn published his chronicles.
To realise the newness of Melbourne, one needs to
learn from the Morning Herald of, say, June
21st 1855, that “The hounds met on luesday at
the kennel a short distance beyond Prahran
There were about forty gentlemen in the held, all
well mounted. A kangaroo was started at
Leman’s swamp, and, after a smart run of about
three miles, was run into and killed. The kennel
was located in the district now known as Windsor,
and the kangaroo was chivied around Elstern-
wick, or Prahran, where the alarming drapery
sacrifices come from.
A quaint excuse was offered by a Timaru
(M L.) publican charged with removing duty
stamps from seven hogsheads with intent to defraud
the revenue. Mr. Pubhchouse swore that the
stamps were eaten off by rats, with which his cellar
swarmed : and he called evidence which disclosed
a similar happening previously at his own ho.el,
and one at another hotel. The doubting beak
eventually dismissed the case.
# * * *
A recent Thames (M.L.) hotel fire swept
so rapidly through the kauri building that two
upstairs boarders were smothered by smoke.
Everyone else escaped in more or less undress.
The brigade, having fought the blaze to a stand-
still, went to bed, returning next morning to stamp
out any remaining sparks and overhaul the rums.
Turning over a sodden mass that had once been a
bed, a fire-quencher concluded he’d struck a third
corpse until the object grunted angrily, and asked
“ Why can’t you leave a bloke to have his sleep
out ? ” The most surprised man on the goldfields
was dragged out to see an uninterrupted view ot
the street; and standing there in the public high-
way, clad in a wet blanket, he demanded fiercely
if anything had been seen of his new boots.

A contributor digs among the suicides :
Ever since Australia lost its head and decided to
wander out over the earth and shoot perfect strangers, it
has been shooting itself in steadily increasing numbers.
In N.S.W. 26 men suicided at the end of a muzzle in
1901; since then the totals year by year have crept up to
43, 60, and 59. There are fashions in suicide as in otner
bad habits. Seven or eight years ago drowning was the
thing. In the four years 1896-1899, 155 men and 49
women in N.S.W. passed out that way ; in 1902 only 4
men and 2 women, and in the past four years but 48 men
and 25 women altogether. But, generally speaking, in
the matter of suicides rock-bottom was reached in
1902 and 1903, when only 90 per 1,000,000 of the
N.S.W. population anticipated what would arrive
anyhow before long. As to whether the lowness
of the figures was due to Federation, or to trie
fact that there wasn’t enough water for most of tne
people to drown themselves in (the drowning figures
almost disappeared those years), or because G. Reid was
out of office, the coroner’s verdict didn’t say. Australia,
bv the way, doesn’t hang itself to nearly the same extent
as most countries. Taking N.S.W. figures as a fair
average (they are thereabouts), only 18 per cent, of the
male suicides have strangled themselves in the past five
years, whereas there is hardly another country in the
world which doesn’t show over 40 per cent., and many of
them show V 0 per cent. Italy is the only one with lover
figures as regards suspension by the neck than the Au»-
tralian—ltaly mostly either drowns itself, or adopts some-
thing weird and wonderful by way of suicide that
doesn’t come into any ordinary class. And it isn t Italy,
but England, that stabs itself or cuts its own throat
the most times —twice as often as any other peop.e,
according to Mulhall’s figures. The more fastidious
Frenchman, of course, abhors that splashy, blotty way of
doing things, and when he doesn’t hang himself he
drowns himself. But nobody rushes to the gun like tie
Australian.

ATTITUDES AND PLATITUDES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Mr. Reid is surprised at Mr. Deakin’s altered atti-
tude.” — Press announcement on Monday morning.
Now Alfred’s changed his attitude,
And George is quite surprised,
For Alfred’s views are not as crude
As George had once surmised.
And divers papers now allude
To dangers from a coming feud,
Since Alfred changed his attitude,
And George is so surpris:d.
There is more grief than gratitude
At Alfred’s change of attitude.
And why is George so much surprised
At Alfred’s attitude ?
The fact is plain and undisguised
If things are rightly viewed.
It seems that Alfred realised
A partnership was ill-advised
In which one partner exerc'sed
No power for ill or good.
And he found no beatitude
In that lop-sided attitude 1
When George got Alfred to conclude
A “ truce,” ’twas criticised
In language that was sometimes rude
Because the “ truce ” comprised
A kind of vacuous interlude
(’Twas meant to soothe the multitude)
But all the gain to George accrued,
While AH. was hypnotised.
Friend George had lots of latitude
For striking any attitude.
Their principles were compromised ;
The Yes-No attitude,
And “Anti-Anything” comprised
The policy pursued!
But though poor Alt. was mesmerised,
It seems the demon’s exorcised,
And George is painfully surprised
At Alfred’s attitude 1
A pleasing form of platitude
When George denounces attitude 1
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.
