A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, June 27, 1905.
My Dear Artni.—
Mr. Rupert Clarke, with a few friends, made
another start last week for Thursday Island. His
brother, Mr. Ernest Clarke, Captain of La
Carabine yacht, has recovered, and resumed his
place on the dog watch, or the binnacle, or what-
ever place captains have to be.
How significantly does a red hat show up
certain characteristics in certain faces. A girl,
with a complexion naturally pallid, flashes by you.
Her pallor makes you think of faces blanched by
moonlight, her eyes burn under the forward tilt of
her fiery hat. A cheap string of pearls encircles
a round, long, slender throat, the throat of early
youth. She looks a thing of frost and fire,
amorous and enigmatic. If you meet her in the
tempered daylight of the arcades the impression
°f her mysteriousness is increased, and you are still
thinking about her, and speculating as to whether
she would make you think if she wore any other
colored hat, when the tram-driver grinds the brakes
into his wheels and asks you in language the same
color as the hat if you want to be a dead remains.
Miss Fanshawe, daughter of our merman-o’-war,
has been doing the Cold Lakes of Maoriland in
company with Miss Portal, a daughter of an
English clergyman. They were snowballing in
Dunedin at a late date—at least, I don’t know
what else they could do in that bleak town at this
season.
Saw Julius. Knight in the street on Monday.
He has sufficiently recovered to get back to his
old quarters at the Wentworth Hotel, and with
the divine winter weather we’re getting should
soon be able to give back-talk to Nero with all his
old-time energy.
On the subject of weather, visiting actor Andrew
Mack insists that Sydney has the greatest climate
on earth. “This is not winter,” he insisted, the
other day. “ Don’t call this winter. It’s —well,
it’s Just It.’’ And with this aphorism from the
great American language the comedian closed the
discussion.
Miss Isa Struan Robertson, daughter of a
Murrumbidgee squatter, whose marriage to Dr.
Broad, one of an old Manly Beach family, took
place at the Australia Hotel last week, was as a
child made the heroine of an Australian story.
She was a beautiful and precocious youngster,
fearless, and surrounded with picturesque acces-
sories, hence an attractive subject for the fictionist.
The Woollahra Ladies’ Auxiliary of the Chil-
dren’s Hospital Fund is united with the Sydney
artists to promote the ball on July 3 in aid of that
charity. And the ladies’ committee, which, I
understand, is taking the major share of
the responsibility, has requested the artists
to forego the proposed pourtrayal of the
Haunted Czar, which was originally on the
ball programme as described last week. The
Governor is to be present, and the naval
and military officers are engaged to help con-
siderably, and it would amount to an inter-
national incident if, under such circumstances,
the Czar was. haunted, or the Mikado was repre-
sented climbing up a pole after peanuts, or any
other great foreign dignitary was cartooned, ca-
ricatured, mocked, or in any way represented as
leading other than the most blissful of innocent
lives. Probably the Haunted Czar would have
been the brightest item on the programme, but
joy must be sacrificed to dignity.
An Australian lady, recently returned from a
long tour, tells how, on presenting letters of in-
troduction to an artistic French family in Paris,
she was taken by its members to an artists’ fancy
dress ball. It was all very delightful, and nothing
startling occurred until supper-time, when a group
of painters came in carrying on a huge silver
salver a beautiful model. She was standing erect,
with fruit in one hand and flowers in the other,
and was dressed in a very slight draping of parsley.
The Parisians gathered round to admire and pass
art comments. But the Australienne ! She has a
dazed memory of getting under a supper-table, and
then leaving the room, with face hidden in her skirts.
The event so engraved itself on her mind that, no
matter what the future may have in store, it will
always tower up as the landmark of her existence.
Her life now falls naturally into three divisions—
that from her birth to the moment when the
Horror took place; the period spent under the
table; and the period beginning at the moment
when she fled out of the room, and ending only
with death. The Australian lady learned that
the model, after she had been sufficiently admired,
dressed herself and joined the dancers. There is
to be an artists’ ball shortly in Sydney. The
galleries would be filled with Honorables from the
Upper House if the men of paint were sufficiently
daring to follow that example—and if the Ladies
Auxiliary didn’t object.
Freemasons’ Annual Ball, in aid of the aged
and infirm, is dated for July 4, in Sydney Town
Hall. It is under the patronage of the Gov.-Gen.
and others of the elect. Another ball in aid of a
very worthy cause is that of the Montefiore
Home, which occurs next Thursday night at
Paddington Town Hall. St. Patrick’s Annual
Ball also comes off in Paddington Town Hall on
July 4; so, taking one thing with another, next
week will be a giddy period, and society will meet
the milk on the doorstep almost every morning.
With a committee of a hundred ladies selling
tickets, the Mayoress of Sydney’s Ball, in aid of
Children’s Hospital, on August 1, ought to be a
money-spinner. The Mayoress, it may be here
mentioned, will hold her next monthly “At
Home ” at the Town Hall on Monday, July 3.
How good for the piano trade must be the en-
couragement given by that corpulent body, Lon-
don College of Music, to the student who loves to
“paw ivory” ! At its recent annual concert an
army of slaves of the keys were presented with
diplomas, medals, and prizes, and no girl who has
been honored by the L.C.M. will, after that, con-
descend to play on the old piano on which she
mounted to household or township fame. Mother
is instructed not to give father a moment’s peace
until anew beast of burden is bought. L.C.M. is
only one of a number of examining bodies that
have their centres in Sydney, and it holds two
exams, yearly. Has been holding them since ’95.
What becomes of the thousands of the diploma-ed
and medalled girls ? Or, rather, what becomes of
their music ? It is still the exception to find in a
gathering at a friend’s house anyone who can play
otherwise than semi-shockingly. It is difficult to
find anyone to play anyhow. A pigtailed damsel of
my acquaintance was thumping for 14 months at
one test-piece for the L.C.M. She “ passed,” but
I don’t think she could ever play anything else.
The L.C.M. gave a concert, last week, to give
some of its victims a chance to be heard. As befits
an invitation show, the concert was listened to by
some 5000 persons. How the business representa-
tives of the Watkin Mills and Jessie Maclachlan
co’s must have groaned ! Signor Hazon once
wandered out to a football match, and, looking on
the grandstands packed, and the rest of the ground
seeming like the early door of the Black Hole of
Calcutta, he entreated the Almighty to tell him
why these people didn’t come to orchestral con-
certs.
Miss Leverrier’s concert will take place on July
Ist. to suit Lady Nortbcote’s convenience. Said
Lady has secured 20 seats. •
Sydney is filling up for the Sheep Shows and
Sales. Principal hotels reckon that a thousand
visitors will be here by Friday —the day on which
the Northcotes inspect the aristocratic muttons.
Mr. Alfred Canney, who represents an elect
brand of champagne, has, with his wife, come
from Melbourne to live in Sydney.
Earl Fitzwilliam and Mr. Tim Healy are mainly
responsible for Mr. T. B. Curran’s .transference of
himself and wife to London. The Earl and Tim
do not, of course, stand on the same political mat,
but the former was a personal friend ofSbe Aus-
tralian in his old House of Commons days. About
115,000 acres is Wentworth-Fitzwilliam’s share of
this planet; and one of his numerous castles is
called Shillelagh. Anyone with a place so named
ought to be well-disposed to persons named
Curran and Healy.
I was at J. C. Watson’s meeting the other
night—a meeting which the Labor women of
Sydney had promoted. The organisation was ex-
cellent, and a chairwoman kept the peace in a way
that would fill Mr. Speaker with envy. At the
conclusion of Watson’s address half-a-dozen
women speakers made notably good speeches. By
tradition, woman is a garrulous creature whose
tongue knows no stopping. On the platform she,
more often than not, shows admirable brevity.
But the labor woman seems to have a big advant-
age over the she-Conservative in oratory. The
well-to-do woman, as a rule, doesn’t speak up, and
as often as not reads her matter. The feminine
Labor leaguer shows earnestness and some natural
eloquence. “ I have pity for those ladies who get up
and say that the Labor party is against the marriage
laws,” said one woman at Watson’s meeting. And
there were cheers, “ I have contempt for the
men who put them up to say those things,” she
added, and there were great cheers. It was a
shrewd thrust. There is an obvious air of “ I’m
doing as I’m told ” about the women who climb
the platform and laboriously read a very wooden
oration in favor of Reid or the fatally beautiful
Mr. Smith.
The fact that little Lord Wendover, the 10-year-
old son of our one-time Governor Caringtoii, has
gone to school to learn the three R.’s has been
made the subject of an English cable. The ex-
Governor is 62 now.
Miss Alice Roosevelt, mentioned as a probable
visitor to Australia, is theU.S.A. President’s only
child by his first marriage. From her mother she
has inherited the biggest share, both of beauty and
dollars, of any of the Roosevelts. Her step-
mother’s half-dozen children are not lavishly pro-
vided with either. Yank papers are fond of seek-
ing out blue-blooded husbands for Miss Alice.
The latest was a small Swedish prince, but he has
just been annexed by one of the host of minor
royal misses in the Cold Country. If this belle of
New York does come our way, the great American
press will probably engage her to an “Australian
rancher,” who ranches in Pitt-street, Sydney.
Novel and beautiful effects come of a recent in-
vention whereby laces and the most delicate
fabrics, as well as art furniture, are electrically
given a metal coating of gold, silver, or bronze.
The cheapest material may be made to appear
costly and striking. Committees of fancy and
charity balls will be very glad of cloth of gold and
silver at tuppence a yard.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent :
“ Nearly one hundred debutantes have come into
public notice during the past week. That’s a
fearful number, considering the accumulation of
old girls on the shelves of Society. At the
’Varsity hop a detachment of young beginners
evoked catty comments on the alleged bloom'less
species the present season has been showing forth.
The remarks were not deserved. The girleens
were nice enough. Just here and there was a
youngish face with sorrow’s shadows where all the
light should be. You know the story of that face.
Three or four elder sisters are ‘ out ’—have been
1 out ’ campaigning, scouting, challenging, and all
the rest of it—for years. And they’re still ‘out.’
So young Florinda knows the horror of it all—
knows the private vexations, humiliations,
wrangles, scratchings, tears, and curses of the
social spinster’s life, and she takes her turn in the
ball-room _ full of misgivings. I know an eighth
(young) sister who took me to a lumber-room and
pointed to seven ivory satin coming-out dresses.
The horror of it seized me. She laughed with a
catch in her throat. ‘And mine is to be hung up
there next week,’ she said.
“The municipal person is a great lover of a
whirl. I have seen over 2cwt. of civic father
bounce his avoirdupois round a ballroom with a
feather and fluff partner of doll weight, and he
bounced jubilantly as one who should say, ‘lama
man with the heart of a boy.’ But the partner’s
face! Ihe droll pathos of it. Yet well-intentioned
mayoral hosts and aldermen give themselves to the
debutantes for the grand opening Lancers of their
chiffon life, and think their weight and age and
out-of-date heartiness form a combination of
attractions to satisfy any young siren.
“ What becomes of the long Miss Talbot during
the hospital and asylum viewing of the suddenly
energetic Exes ? The poor visitor has either been
forgotten, or she breaks bread in private, or she is
consigned away with the cold storage of preserves
at Stonington. We saw her for a little while and
then no more.
“ The motor-spill is becoming as suspicious a
happening as an attack of appendicitis. The
breakdown in a lonely landscape, with no chance
of an early rescue, has been told so often that an
infant in arms smiles now when it is exploited.
And no bones are ever broken in these accidents.
“Some dentists consider Mrs. Grundy’s pre-
sence superfluous when a young thing is having
her teeth arrested, or taken into custody, or
crowned with a gold piece. The yarns of an
occasional impromptu hug or light rain of kisses
by the molar man have filled the aching girl and
her sympathetic ma with alarm of late; so if ma
can’t come herself, she sends in a crony to listen to
the shrieks of the daughter who is undergoing
painless extraction. One curt member of
the profession tells me that a man of any
artistic or beauty-sense is never so tired as
when he has finished dragging a dishevelled,
pallid, eye-strained, affrighted, unengaging
female round the room by the tooth. ‘Why,
when you have finished looking into her
mouth and have picked up her toupee and her
switch, dusted her complexion off your cuffs, ex-
tracted her fringe-net from your upper waistcoat
button, handed back her removable plate and
seen her without any of those upholsterings, Mrs.
Grundy is a superfluity.’
“Sailed for Europe last week, one of the most
promising young girl singers that Victoria has
produced. Her name is Eda Bennie, and he*
people are seemingly aware of the fact that a
voice which is going to Marchesi’s school in Paris
for education has no claim to public gratitude.
Anyway, Miss Bennie departed without asking
for a benefit. Melba heard her sing when she
was in the ‘ under 15 ’ stage of development,
and advised complete rest for the voice for the
next two years. Parkina and Watkin Mills have
lately endorsed all the sweet prophecies of Melba,
and now Miss B. is on her way to try the test of
competition with the large assortment of aspirants
at Marchesi’s. The damsel appears to be as
clever as she looks. She speaks French and
Italian quite respectably, and is musical to her
finger-tips.
“ The memorial window becomes a pain when
families add to the saintly auras their bloated
crest. The Christian halo, in conjunction with the
carriage crest in several Melbourne churches is, at
the.least, a humorous arrangement. It suggests
a fond hope that there will be carriage crests—and
lower orders to envy them —in Heaven.
“Mr. Harry Osborne, one of the polo cham-
pions of the woolly quarter, has been down with
a lung _ trouble. For some days no hopes were
entertained of his recovery. But he made a
valiant effort, and won.
_ “ The constable Geelan, who is threatened with
dire penalties for not arresting the public more
often, is the man who directs the traffic and
prevents wholesale motor-slaughter, at the Market
and Flinders streets junction. He is a splendid
specimen of the Foorce, to be sure! And the
cycling girl makes a hero of him, and she-tram-
travellers trail their vision after him admiringly.

her old chum—Baronet Clarke’s dame —in London.
Miss O’Loghlen has been abroad with relatives
for about two years.
“The musical Lavers, who have been guiding
the violinist star on his concert course (in con-
junction with one of the Tate brethren), have lots
of money—in which they differ joyously from
most of their kind. Mrs. Laver has a fortune in
her own right and a charming home in Mid
Brighton.
“Mrs. Herbert Embling, wife of Dr. Embling,
takes the horse lead this season. She hunts with
the Melbourne Hounds. Miss Raleigh has been
a close second so far. Miss Watt is a young
sport, but game as any. Miss Whiting and
several other beginners are, so far, shy of stiff
fences and a hard gallop.
“Miss Alison Fairbairn has brought two family-
treed nags from the Cold Country. She had a
rare riding-hack of her own last season, but she
fancied the novelty of importing from ’Ome, don’t
you know. Local nag-fanciers hereabouts are
now waiting to see if the beasties prove in any
way worth the importation. Probably they won’t,
except in so far as they gratify the vanity of the
ypung person who can reflect that she is not
patronising a beastly colonial horse. Australia
exports first-class nags to almost every corner of
the earth, whilst from the yarns I hear about
pleasure-horses in England, they’re sick or lame
most of their time, and you keep them mainly for
the joy of buying embrocation.
“ A dyspeptic male has written complaining of
the boarding-houses of the rural districts. He
hadn’t enjoyed, or suffered, a holiday for three
years. At last he came in for some back-pay, or
something, and packed up his bag for a week of
country life. A cheery pastoral boarding-house
was his aim, and one which mentioned in a simple
Horatian manner that ‘ boarders are taken on our
farm ’ won his heart. He saw himself surrounded
by tilled fields, hay-stacks, ripening corn, bees,
flocks of baa-lambs, and landscape views; and
he went forth to this Sabine farm for
rest, and roses, and cow-breath, and cream.
He found his landlady’s growler at the
country station. Three mouldy-looking boarders
with lantern jaws and a cold, frost-bitten,
ravenous look, were on board. It wasn’t a cheer-
ful sign. However, up he climbed. Sighting a
bleak-looking, wooden place with a melancholy
twirl of grey smoke stealthily rising from one
chimney, like the last lock of winter on a bald
pate, he asked, as an amiable distraction, who
lived there. ‘That is our crib,’ said the dreariest
of the trio, with a low gurgle. And it was—a fowl
run and a smoke-stained cottage. In some mud-
puddles a swarm of ducks played a solemn wet
game. One cow and a tottering calf roamed
towards a bit of a creek.
“A charming though precocious girl-fn who
was ‘Sweet Nell’ at the mayoral hop is Marie
Hall, a daughter of the Cooper-Halls, and a niece
of the M ‘Eaeharns. She is now twelve years of
age and as attractive as kiddies go at that awkward
age. But in four years time she comes into a dot
that will make her about the richest girl in
Melbourne, and then she will be positively lovely,,
anyhow.
“The death of Mrs. Balls-Headley (wife of the
Collins-street medico) removes a familiar and ever-
dignified personage from the pure-merino section
of Society. The tall, dressy dame was a faithful!
Toorak luminary. Her favored chum was an
equally dressy Somebody, Mrs. W. Leonard. The
pair wore important clothes, and gloried in grand
figures. As well-preserved, light-hearted middle-
aged dames they were always good to look upon.
A few months ago Mrs. Balls-Headley was stricken
with a serious illness, and died on Friday.
“ There is to be an inquiry into the matter of
impromptu guesting at the Mayoral hop. The
pasteboards conveyed the warning, ‘Nottrans-
ferable,’ but people thought in the crush of the
galleries their identity would escape notice. There
was no crush, and zealous eyes were out scouting.
So there are to be two or three inquiries which will
clear the atmosphere of much grumble, complaint,
and green-eyed malice.
“At the Saturday Heermann recital the cold
draughts of the Melb. Town Hall blew chill on
naked shoulders. The tall meditative son of
Hugo helped his dad to make music guaranteed
not to set the emotions ablaze. One thinks of
Gothic cathedrals, Cato virtues, bright tumbling
waters, sunrises in springtime, scraps of Milton’s
‘Allegro,’listening to the bearded fiddler. One
feels good. Hugo Heermann is a devoted hus-
band, the father of a large family, and wears a
beard.
‘ ‘ Miss Elms, who has been a journalistic identity
in this quarter for several years, has had a ‘ call ’
to London. She left on Friday, bearing a travel-
ling bag donated by the Argus, and a gew-gaw by
the Writers’ Club, and comforts for the voyage
given by many private friends. The active little
lady has such humor that she was able to supply
plain, solid feminine copy to the Freetrade daily
and jocular views to one or two Melbourne weeklies.
Her new post is that of reporter on the London
Morning Leader.
“ Sudermann has no humorous irrelevancies in
the ‘Fires of St. John.’ No female breaks into
the dark muddle of things by ludicrous flippancies
as in ‘ Magda ’ where certain grey and ominous
situations are interrupted for comic relief by two
adult she-persons. Yet the crowd gets its laugh
out of the long stretch of suffering at places
where the playwright intended tears. When, for
instance, the ingenue of the piece comes in her
dream-garb to say a second and more urgent good-
night to her stout lover in the mustard boots, the
audience giggles. When he adjures her to fly
from him with the reproach, ‘lf anyone should
see you in this condition ! ’ they laugh outright.
When, finally, the stout young man implores the
girl frankly to go back to bed, the whole house roars.
It made me hark back more tolerantly to the two
irrelevant females whose whispered colloquy as to
the quality and expense of Magda’s underwear
and the angular one’s ejaculation, ‘ Silk chemises!
Why, it’s perfectly awful ! ’ openly demanded
a chuckle. The good parts of ‘ The Fires of St.
John ’ are the bad parts. Theatre-goers stare at
a situation in the third act where Miss O’Neil
cowers on the stage, hugging the knees of the
gentleman (still in the mustard boots), and on an-
other episode of most desperate abandon. The
wickedest of them are horrified at what the stage
is abruptly darkened for. Ladies’ seminaries are
herewith warned off this act, unless the lights are
put out five minutes sooner.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.
Augustus Charles Gregory, who has just
gone to join the majority, was 86 when he died.
He was a wonderful explorer in his day, and in
the 13 years, 1846-1859, he covered enormous
distances in Westralia and Queensland. He
found almost everything he went to look for,
except Leichhardt; that lost botanist’s bones
were unfindable. And in the end Australia gave
Augustus Gregory his great reward; it made him
a J.P. and an M.L.C.

DR.
WILLIAMS’ PINK PILLS CURE A WELL-KNOWN SQUATTER.
“ A FTER sixteen years of constant misery,
\ and after wasting £250 on doctors, Dr.
r\. Williams’ Pink Pills cured me for good
of the worst case of Asthma that over a
man suffered from,” said Mr. Andrew James
Dunlop, a prosperous farmer residing in Heather-
ton, Dandenong. .
“One wet night, when coming home from
Brighton, I took the wrong track and got lost, ”
Mr. Dunlop went on to say. “ I had to tramp six
miles in the pouring rain, and arrived home
soaked to the skin. Next morning I could not
speak with the cold. In a week I was wheezing
with every breath. That was the beginning of
the Asthma that nearly cost me my life.
“My nose was completely stuffed up with
Catarrh. It took two hoUrS Steady hawking and
spitting before I cquld draw one good breath. You
could hear me wheezing all over the house. As
soon as it came ■ dark, a -horrible smothering
feeling came on. I used to moan and struggle,
and gasp for breath. In the dead of night I
would have to get out of bed and sit bolt upright
to save myself from smothering. Often I’ve
groped my way out into the cold night-air, so that
I could breathe enough to keep myself alive.
Dozens of times I nearly choked to death
C °“ g Noone in the house could sleep, for I did
nothing but cough, cough, cough,” Mr, Dunlop-
added. “I brought up yellow .phlegm streaked
with blood. My blood was m :x terrible state._
You 'may judge how full it was of poison when
I tell you that great blisters used to come out on
my chest, ijome were as big as a shilling, and
there were thousands, of little ones. When Mrs.
Dim lop cut these blisters open with the scissors,
yellow matter and corruption poured out of them.
My wife tended me day and night. I could not
lie down in bed. As soon as X tried I felt as if
someone were pressing a heavy pillow over my
mouth and nose, I had to get up and sit in a
chair by the lire. Then when I was utterly ex-
hausted with coughing, I might get an hour’s
'sleep, sitting bolt upright. For sixteen years
neither my wife nor I had a night’s sleep.
I ‘The coughing fits nearly lifted the top off my
head. My forehead burned, and there were
aching pains in the back of my head and
across my shoulders. The catarrh filled up my
nose, and the foul droppings into my mouth
sickened me. I had no relish for food. Every-
thing tasted alike to me. I was too weak to do a
stroke of work, and had hardly the strength to
walk. Many a time I prayed to God for death,
and Mrs. Dunlop went on her knees and asked for
relief for me.
“ Over £250 went on doctors and medicines —
but I still 'suffered' on. My ease seemed hopeless
whten Mr. I. J. Eden advised Mrs. Dunlop to get
m&sotne'Dr.-Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.
He; said they cured him after he had Indigestion
and Rheumatism for years. Mrs. Dunlop wrote
to the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co. for advice, and
got word back to use Nasal Balm as well as Dr.
Pills." TheTeffer said that iCstEma
was a strange mixture of nervous trouble and chest
weakness, and showed how good blood was bound
to strengthen both lungs and nerves. I followed
their advice,, arid in eight weeks I was completely
cured. This was four years ago, and I have been
working hard on the farm ever since. Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills have cured me of chronic Asthma and
nasal catarrh after twenty years of misery.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cured Mr. Dunlop’s
Asthma because nothing can strengthen the lungs
and nerves except good rich, red blood'—and no-
thing but Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills can actually
make new blood. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills do
just that one thing, but they do it well. They
don’t act on the bowels. They don’t bother with
mere symptoms. They just root out from the
blood the cause of anremia, indigestion, headaches,
backaches, kidney disease, liver complaint, skin
diseases, general weakness, and the special secret
troubles of growing girls and women. Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills are sold by all chemists and

Justice Owen, of N.S.W., according to
rumor, means to retire from judicial work at the
close of the Lands Commission inquiry. He has
qualified for a Supreme Court Justices pension.
Possibly the Lands Commission smells may save
the pension by suffocating him.

George Fairbairn, old Duncan Gillies’ suc-
cessor at Toorak, has returned from the Cold and
other countries' a much improved person. His
speech seems to have lost .all its old Brasseyan
inclination to get tangled, or to stop, or to fall off
at unexpected moments, and his long-suffering
constituents have again an intelligible—if not
intelligent—representative. .
It seems that Reid,. too,- was,
once on the dramatic stage. He confided. to a
Geelong audience last week that years ago he took
part as an actor in a tragedy, and, after spending
weekfi in learning how to speak 12 words and carry
a dead body off the stage, he forgot the words and
dropped the body. Ever since then he has stuck
to comedy. .
Melb. Herald’s silly idea of a prize com-
petition for selecting the ten best citizens of Vic-
toria has had its inevitable results. The ten best
of Collingwood were voted for the other week,
and, strange to say, Jack Wren, the tote man,
didn’t head the poli. Wren even failed to get a
place. He was declared to be the sixteenth best
citizen of Collingwood.
Rev. E. N. Merrington, a clever student
of Sydney University a few years back, and the
winner of a travelling scholarship; has won a
Ph.’D. at Harvard (U.S.A.), and is said to be the
first Australian to accomplish the feat. Harvard’s
Ph.D. means a stiff examination, unlike the
wooden-nutmeg diplomas of other seats of learn-
ing. .The U.S. has all kinds of Universities.
Some of them are of the best. Some, which exist
for the sale of diplomas, consist of a tenement
house, with the learned professor chopping wood
in the yard.
The difficulties in the way of the historian
are illustrated by the fact that some doubt has
arisen in Adelaide whether Colonel Light was
buried under the monument that (up-till last week)
commemorated him. several venerable
people were found, who said they actually wit-
nessed the funeral on October 10th, 1839. Un-
fortunately, in the interval, Light Square —then
the fashionable quarter —has become mostly the
resort of the Chow and the kind of girl who be-
comes Mrs. Chow, so the forthcoming statue to
Adelaide’s founder is to be put up elsewhere.

In the S.A. House of Assembly a self-opinionated
member, during a speech about the voting
age limit, said truculently, “Why, I ; knew as
much at 18 years of age as I do now. You

“ Dareel” : Be Kinred’s Murrumbidgee-
Murray voyage. Twenty-four years ago, Shaw,
a medical student, canoed from Gundagai to the
Murray’s ocean outlet. Shaw,- who had also
canoed the Darling from Bourlce to Wentworth,
was afterwards the sole survivor of the Ingham
exploration party, attacked in New Guinea.
At 60, G. H. Reid says he will give but
very few years more to public life, as he wants to
enjoy a quiet old age. Therefore, he proposes to
“ devote himself to matters of. greater importance
than the fiscal issue.” In other words, he is going
to “sink the fiscal issue” and struggle' to Keep
office, G. H. Reid’s future comfort being of much
more importance than Australia s. And he calls
that public life !
The late Gilbert Probyn Smith’s most
notable feat in inkslinging was a pamphlet against
John Norton. For this John Norton prosecuted
him criminally, but the jury disagreed, and then
John gave his detractor employment on Mel-
bourne Truth. And when Smith died Norton
paid the funeral expenses, and sent a wreath
with the word “Nepenthe” to be placed upon
the coffin.
“O’Fipp”: Be naming of Pentndge
Stockade, Melbourne (B. l/6/’O5). The original
Pentridge, a little Dorsetshire village, has some
literary fame through its connection with poet
Browning’s family. Browning’s great-granclfather
was footman to a Pentridge squire, and polished
up the silver so accurately that he rose to be
butler, and expired in a blaze of local glory as
keeper of the village inn.
E. H. T. Plant, latest addition to Queens-
land’s nominee House, is an old Northern identity.
He arrived in Ravenswood in the early seventies,
made a fair thing selling the succulent chop to
the hungry “batcher,” and later on built the first
crushing mill. Ravenswood then looked like
going down. Plant bought his partner (Jackson)
out for a nominal sum, sent away for finer screens
for the mill, put all the tailings through again,
netted about £20,000, and from that day has gone
on adding to the pile. He shortly afterwards
shifted his mill to Charters Towers, and got a
controlling interest in many mines. Plant old
mill (with many improvements) is now owned by
the Bonnie Dundee G.M. Co. Plant, who is
grim, smile-less, but a good boss, isn’t suspected
of anything definite in the way of political
opinions, but bis wife is a great organiser and a
solid friend of the Democratic party.

Bishop Donaldson, of Brisbane, suggests a
partial application of communistic doctrines to the
incumbents and curates of Queensland. The in-
comes from some parishes give a good living,
while those from others starve the pastors. He
proposes that all the incomes should be pooled and
distributed, share and share alike, among all the
working ministers. But, alas ! the Bishop does
not indicate that he will throw his own income
into the pool.
The enemies of James Zimri Sellar,
elected to the S.A. Assembly in the Labor
interest, tell of his inability to remember names
and faces. When “Banjo” Paterson returned
from the Boer war, he was publicly welcomed at
Adelaide Town Hall. Sellar occupied a seat on
the platform alongside the war correspondent
and, during a lull in the proceedings, gazed
earnestly at the poet a moment, and murmured
audibly, “ And so this is Plugger Bill.”
Bernard Mathews, who died in, Melbourne
the other day at the age of 72, was the son of the
late B. R. Mathews, and came to Victoria in 52
with his father, who was in command of the
auxiliary screw steamer Great Britain on the
occasion of its first voyage to Australia. Mathews
senior belonged to the old school of navigators
who shortened sail every night, and thought four
months a good passage for a first-class vessel from
England to Australia. After the first voyage he
was ’superseded by up-to-date Captain Gray, w ho
maintained an average of 60 days for the trips
during a long series of years.
Passed out the other day Andrew Petei
Lind', a picturesque Sydney identity, whose huge,
quaintly-garbed figure, genial, bearded face
surmounted by a tall hat, and inseparable brolly
have (been familiar to Botanic Garden frequenters
for the past 10 years. He was the son of Peter
Erlaiidson, farmer, of Lindby, Sweden, and adopted
the name of Lind after his birthplace. Aftei
living some time in America where, it is said, he
was a Lutheran preacher, he came to Australm
about 20 years ago, and worked as a station hand.
Ten years ago he came to Sydney, banked his
savings (£884), and afterwards lived upon the
interest. He was taken in charge a few weeks
back, on suspicion of mental unsoundness, and,
a little later, moved on to the Hospice for the
Dying, where he passed out.
Joke Cook, who is now a more violent
anti-Socialist than even W. M‘Linen, is partly a
political joke and partly a political accident.
When the first State Labor party was being
whipped together, as a far more aggressive
Socialistic party than it is to-day, Joe Was check-
weigher at Lithgow. Smailes, a Primitive
Methodist parson, was the chosen Labor candi-
date, but at the last moment he jibbed, and Joe
elbowed himself into the job of preaching militant
Socialism, and was elected. Later on, the
party elected Joe as chief Socialist, and he led the
Socialist party in the House for some time, until
Reid became Premier, through the vote of the
Socialist party, for which he had angled for several
years, and later on, to further shovv his horror of
Socialism, bo took the chief Socialist into his
Ministry.

The gun and the man behind it :
John Macintosh, of the N.S.W. Leg. Council, who was
SPwitness the other day in a law case in which his old
hardware firm, John Macintosh and Sons, a ? k< T -I
£lO,OOO from a trade-protection office which had said
things, loomed up in the box a hard, grey, solid old man,
who never tasted liquor, never allowed it m his house,
never joked, and unbent so little that in his 60 years in
business in Sydney he hadn’t been inside 20 houses besides
his own. Iron, if a man handles it for 60 years, appears to-
enter soul as well as body ; for every old-established iron-
goods man seems to be much the same sort of person
witness Scarfe, of Adelaide, who died awhile back, and.
cut up for about half a million, though but very few m
even little Adelaide ever saw or heard of the man.
One explanation is that the profits in the hardware
line are smaller than in almost any other, rarely
running to more than 6 per Cent, on capital in-
vested, and that the ironmonger who keeps in the
business has to be of the hard, stern, keep-your-nose-
to-the-grindstone sort. But it’s a rule that seems to-
hold good pretty well all round—a man is fashioned by
the goods he handles. The man who handles ribbons,
and tells the lady that this is really the very latest thing
in torchon, parts his hair in the middle ; or if he doesn e
he ought to. And the man who grows cows, and milks
cows, and thinks cows, becomes more than half cow; just,
as the American who lives in the whirr of machinery
buzzes and jumps, partly because he wants to get out of
the way before the piston drops, partly because he knows
that when the machinery is running it is eating money,
and the sooner the job’s done and the power cut on the
more profit there will be in the contract. The cow-man
doesn’t whirr and buzz ; he knows that the cow’s machinery
is working hardest when the animal is most passive. Ana
the ordinary grain-farmer, who knows that most of the time
things are out of his hands, and rain will do more than-
hustle, gets into the same frame of mind. In a round-
about way all this has some bearing on the question or
defence;'and there aren’t many bigger Australian ques-
tions than that. The importing disease encourages a
population that handles calico over the counter—and
Heaven help the country that has to be defended by the
young man from behind the counter! Also, Heaven help*
the country that has to depend on the man who has been
milking the cow ! The Monkey has been bustling lately-
building things, making things, starting factories, run-
ning mills ; while the Bear has been largely a grain-
raising, butter-making person ; and the same thing is
happening at that meeting as happened under very
Similar circumstances when the U.S. had an argument
with Spain.
STUMPING THE COUNTRY.
Mr. H. E. Kelly, who used to be poultry-
editor of one of the R.C. papers, and began the-
alarm cry against the Labor party’s Socialism on
religious grounds, now- bitterly attacks Cardinal
Moran for not agreeing with him, and R.C.
journalism knows him no more. Mr. Kelly must
go. back to his chookies.
Sergeant Billy Davidson, for 30 years
coroner’s orderly at Melbourne morgue, leaves the
Palace of the Stiff to take the-rank of sub-inspec-
tor. Davidson has been so long associated with
battle, murder, and sudden death that the morgue
won’t seem the same homely old place without
him. Davidson went through all the big tragedies
of the past two decades, and suffered all the comic-
relief of the late coronor-comedian, Dr. Youl.
What the Melbourne “found drowneds” will do
without them both it is hard to conjecture.

N.S.W. Government has wisely decided
not to import a Pasteur Institute scientist to
inoculate bunny with a plague so as to secure his
extermination. It is against all reason and nature
to calculate on annihilating a species by the
promulgation of a disease. Bubonic plague is a
deadly thing, and rats are particularly susceptible
to its attack. The rats are ravaged again and
again by it, but never exterminated. If a disease
were inoculated among Australian rabbits it
would cause great mortality, no doubt, but it
would still leave the rabbit as a pest, and it would
at the same time, spoil the rather valuable market
that exists for bunny products. Bunny now pro-
vides hat-fur, leather, food for men, and do°--
thing ltS ’ anC * 6Ven a industry is some-
.** * *
Premier Bent, one day, when he was in a
promising mood, said he was going to make it
possible for all Victorian children to go “ straight
bang up to the University.” It’s a long, slushy
road some of them will have to travel. At Yanna-
than, near Cranbourne (Vic.), the roads are so im-
passable that the children have to walk along wire
and wire-netting fences to avoid getting bogged,
ouch acrobatic feats are more likely to land the
aspiring kid m the circus than in the University.

“Pelican”: Wellington (N.S.W.) is get-
ting a very Asiatic tone. It supports 13 busi-
nesses run by Syrians and Chinese. Then it has
in its midst about 350 Chows (many of whom have
white girls living with them). Most of the Mac-
quarie and Bell River flats are let to these aliens
One squatter is “on the halves” with his Chows.
XNo one but a Chinaman with his cheap method of
living can, as a rule, afford to pay the enormous
rents asked for the fiats.

“Jay Haim”: T’other day, met a wild-
ooking object. Dropping his chaff-bag bluey, he
said, “ Yer wouldn’t think a dashed liv’d foller a
cove a ’undred miles, would yer?” Told him it
was hardly possible. “Well,” he said, “There’s
a dashed fly that’s follered me all that distance.”
He looked me over, and then stared hard at my
hat. “ Why, blow me, there’s the cow.” He let
out a savage blow and sent my new tile spinning.
Must have missed the fly, for I next saw him
racing up the road, making frantic leaps and
clouts m the air, like a demented scarecrow
lhree days have passed, and a broken-hearted
Matilda still waits by the roadside for the acro-
batic fly chaser.

“ Petramos ” : My dog was called Shandy.
He disappeared. I advertised at once. A boy
brought him back. He got 2s. 6d. Shandy dis-
appeared again. I waited three days and adver-
tised. A man brought him back. He got 2s. fid.
A month later Shandy was missed again. I waited
a week, advertised, and got him for another 2s. 6d.
ror the fourth time he went away, and his value
not being very high I decided to let him go alto-
gether. I didn’t advertise. He came back in ten
days, on the exact date his registration feil due. I
registered him and gave him away to a friend who
can afford to lose a dog. So far he has only had to
advertise for Shandy once, and then he got him
back for 2s. 6d.

“Homo “T.D.” (B. l/6/’O5) says he
‘‘discovered there were practically no uses for
them (Australian police-inspectors)
except to act the bush-lawyer in
minor courts.” etc. Aren’t there,
though ! They’re as keen and
tireless as bloodhounds in tracking
down a delinquent subordinate. One
instance of many I could cite : A
sergeant, who had just been trans-
ferred to a more important metro-
politan station, requisitioned head-
quarters for Is. or so, cab-hire, but
he was met with a big “WHY had
he disregarded the written order in
the book, directing that cabby him-
self, armed with the sergeant’s
voucher, should collect such fare.”
He pleaded that it was customary at
the other station to get the money
on producing the cabby’s receipt, and
that he had not been aware of any
such order. This brought the
whiskers and braid ’’ in charge of
the district down on that station
directly. He didn’t find the order,
but where it should have been he
found evidence of a torn-out leaf.
The luckless sergeant-in-charge was
forthwith arraigned for destroying
State property, and seemed in a
fair way to get 40 years’ “ hard,”
but that a constable chanced to
remember how the fatal leaf, with
others, had been torn out six years
before by the previous sergeant’s
youngster.
“ Eiddal ”
Formalin, mentioned by “Kink” (B. l/6/’O5), is the
new anti-smut specific recommended by the Department
of Agriculture to the cookies on the Kyabramapootra
fringe. There is a large area within cooee of Kyabram
where rust, whiteheads, and smut do much ravage every
year as the crops ripen. Formalin is supposed to be
better than bluestone as a grain-pickle, but, so far,
experiments have put cookies in a quandary. Last year
a Timmering cocky pickled with formalin, and had a
dandy crop, while his neighbors complained about the
smut, even in the adjoining - paddocks. This year, using
the stuff on a large scale, hundreds of acres have had to
be re-sown, a result attributed to sowing formalin-pickled
grain in dry weather, and to the needless violence of the
solution recommended by the Department. On the other
hand, a few miles further south, at Runnymede East,
numbers of cookies used the specific with complete
success, in all soils, and with the pickle as strong - as a lib.
bottle to three bags of wheat. The Department recom-
mends a lib. bottle to 40 gallons of water as sufficient to
pickle 14 bags of wheat. The trouble seems to be in the
soil or seed, or something, but no such trouble arises
when bluestone is used.
Ramofch Gilead ” : A few months ago,
near Inverell (N.S.W.), I was driving with a
farmer in his dray across to another farmer’s
place about a mile and a half distant.
I resently we came to a bit of a swamp.
and when about half-way through
the old mare jibbed. It wa3
an emphatic proceeding on
her part. The farmer tried
various kinds of persuasion
and words to induce her
to proceed, but it all
arrived at where it started.
At last he lost his patience
and swore an outback oath
that he would shift the
“ cow ” and the dray if it
occupied him till next
Christmas (it was just
January). He took a
pint-pot out of the dray,
jumped down regardless
of mud and water, and
commenced filling
pints of water and
pouring it into the
old mare’s ear. I
sat in the dray,
while the farmer,
every now and then,
adjured me to “’ang
on” for all I was
worth (which was
sixpence). For awhile
the mare took no
notice save to oc-
casionally shake the
water out of her hear-
ing apparatus, but
suddenly, just as the
cocky was tipping an-
other pintful, she let out
one mighty bound and
was away through the
swamp, and across the
small plain, as if it were
time to go to work and she
knew it was plough-day,
the old farmer screaming and
yelling to me behind to “ Stop
’er ! Stop ’er ! Pull the blank, blank,
blank, blank, blank, blank, blank up.”
This I did, after she had gone about three miles
and a-half, and broken a shaft and one wheel, and
most of the harness. “It’s not good to do that,”
he said when he caught up ; “ it’ll allers shift ’em
right enough ; but it mostly sends ’em deaf.” And
it did. The mare is deaf to this dav.

Peto : Happened on a weary philosopher
in the Yankalilla (S.A.) district. He was splash-
ing up the remnant of a cheque at a wayside pub.,
and went down among the dead men at closing
time, offering with his last breath to fight the
world and his wife for a small consideration. Prior
to catching the early morning coach I went into
the bar and found the courageous one sitting on
the floor and morosely refusing to budge, despite
the efforts of landlord and ostler. The latter
thereupon emptied a bucket of water over him,
but he exhibited not the least concern. When
the wrathful ostler had departed for another
bucket of water I offered to help the ruin out of
harm s way. But he waved me aside with a lordly
grace, and said, in a voice husky with booze and
dark-brown philosophy, “There are some mo-
ments (hie) when a man prefers to be alone.”

“Harry S.” : Have often hunted wild
pigs on Murray, Goulburn, and Lachlan river
frontages, and in the reed-beds we used to find real
savage old boars. I hunted on horseback with a
gun, or a shear-blade fastened to a light pole, and
with a few good kangaroo dogs. As “S.H.R.”
says (B. 25/5/’O5), some of the boars will make a
man sprint for his life. I have caught young pigs,
and fattened them up. As to the blacks killing
the boars with tomahawks, it would be difficult to
find either black or tomahawk on any of those
rivers nowadays.
BALLAD OF THE MAN FAR INLAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
‘‘Ship me somewhere east of Suez,” where some old
flat-floored canoe is ;
Scoop me out a giant pumpkin ; give me two dry sticks
for oars;
Where the blessed bullfrog bubbles-where the tortoise
tells his troubles—
Let me wander by the moisture where the savage tomtit
roars !
Where the welkin welks with gladness-where there isn’t
any badness ;
Where the world is wet and cheerful, and the wagtail
wags encore;
For I’m sick of music-vampers, and I hate the stunning
stampers— b
Aye, I want to bite the ocean, and I want the sea once
more !
I desire a month of leisure spent in clean aquatic
pleasure—
I request a little dingy or a battleship or raft.
Let me sit astride a barrel, clad in Adam’s scant apparel
With the dampness round my ankles and a buckram
zephyi aft!
Let me swim, and let me swelter, where the seas come
helter-skelter—
Hand me out a tiny ereeklet or a river or lagoon ;
I desire to dip my body in a bath that isn’t shoddy—
I insist on the Pacific being carted to me soon.
In the desert I am wailing, where the brown dust-clouds
are sailing,
And I warn the Lord instanter that it’s wrong to keep me
here ;
If He fails to send me seaward—if lie falls away to
leeward—
I will bust the apparatus of this blessed hemisphere !
For I’m angry and disdainful, and I find the desert
painful,
And I’ll spoil this blooming planet if the Lord neglecteth
me.
I am sulky with creation—my ideal occupation
Were to sit astride an iceberg in a gold-and-purple sea !
“Ship me somewhere east of Suez,” where some water-
logged canoe is —
Scoop me out a giant pumpkin ; let me use my hands for
oars ;
Where the porpoise porpeth gaily—where the turtle turteth
daily —
Let me wander by the moisture where the savage tomtit
roars !
Where the welkin ivelks likes blazes—where the peaceful
moo-cow grazes —
Where the world is wet and winsome, and the wagtail
wags encore ;
For I’m sick of slaving here, Lord! and it’s like to cost
you dear, Lord !
If I fail to bite the ocean and observe the sea once more!
N.S.W.

“ W.P.8.” : I once heard a paddy-melon
(i.e., kangaroo-rat) described as a “melon grown
by an Irishman,” which is not quite correct. But
I haven’t noticed any queries in The Bulletin as
to the origin of the term. Will some Irish-Aus-
tralian stand up on his brogues and give me an
idea, but don’t take up too much space about it,
and write in a distinct voice ?

“ Orthos ” : For cast-iron cruelty com-
mend me to the action of the Government through
its P.P. Boards re rabbit destruction. Recently
in a western town (N.S.W. ), a struggling settler
was sent to gaol for not destroying rabbits on his
property, although it was shown that he had laid
poison and lost a valuable horse through its picking
up the poisoned pollard. He was surrounded on
three sides by Crown lands —breeding grounds for
the vermin, for the Government takes no measures
to exterminate the pests on Crown lands. Prac-
tically he was gaoled for not destroying rabbits on
Government property, a herculean task, which no
doubt provoked him to give “cheek” to the
haughty inspector instead of the usual luncheon.
* * * *
“ Xerxes ” : Be sleeping out. Have known
three occasions where sickness and death followed
(and were very certainly caused by) a spree and
the subsequent “sleep out.” Some chaps think
they’re made of iron and patched with rocks.
INGRATITUDE.

“Chip”: Amongst the reasons for the
non-occupation of Papua by white settlers are
difficulty in obtaining grants, prohibitive survey
fees, and the neglect of Gov. Macgregor’s provi-
sions as to the opening of roads by the natives,
who do nothing except decorate the landscape.
Senator S. Smith is not far wrong in stating that
Papua has been at a standstill for seven years. In
fact, it has retrograded, and will continue to do so
unless drastic measures are adopted.
“ Walrick ” : There is quite a noticeable
dying away of the native cat in many parts of the
N.S.W. bush. All round Walgett, Corinda,
Coonamble, Goodooga, Narrabri, lYtorce, and
numerous other districts, but a very slioit while
since the native feline abounded, whereas now it
is considered a novelty to see one in a month.
Some assert that the lack of cat is caused by a
deficiency in the commissariat department, i.e.,
that there is a falling off in the number of small
birds, etc., on which the animals exist. In the
light of the recent thick howl about mice, however,
this scarcely seems a likely cause. Probably some
disease like that which, a few years ago, wiped
out the opossums, is responsible.
* * * *
“Quad” : N.S.W. politicians who visited
South Australia in support of local candidates are
much impressed with the methods of the local
German farmers. Thrower, M.L.A., says that
nothing is wasted, and their motto is maximum
efficiency out of everything. Reapers, binders,
harvesters, and other implements are carefully
sheltered and well cared for until next season, and
the German farmer’s maintenance charge in this,
respect is small. N.S.W. farmers have a happy
knack of hitching the reaper or harvester or
plough under the handiest gum tree, and leaving it
there exposed to all weathers. Next season, when,
parts give way, they wonder why.

4 4 Timbarra n : Phe fox sooms to preier
black-feathered poultry. Recently chained one
up in a fowl-run, and on returning an hour later
discovered it chewing the head off a black pullet,
the only one of that color in the yard, lhis
struck me as being peculiar, so I placed a giddy
old Spanish rooster and his black missus in the
same run. Though other fowls came within the
fox’s reach, it took not the slightest notice of
them, but directly the ebony pair approached it
made a spring, and clawed both down at once.
Further experiments were interrupted by Mrs.
Timbarra and a broom-handle.
ON WATCH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Lights on the harbor where the ferry-boats are gliding
Fairy ripples flashing where the foaming waters churn ;
-Gloom on the water where an ocean boat is riding,
And whisp’ring of the wavelets that are lapping at the
stern.
Glad voices blending in the chanty they are singing, .
Borne upon the night breeze toward the swelling main.
God! I can see it all and hear the chorus ringing—
See it all, and hear it in the wind upon the plain.
(There that blasted, restless ewe comes sneaking out
again.)
White lights so still, and red lights that are racing—
Streaming through the darkness as they pass a rival
boat ;
Slow lights and glow lights—each other ever chasing
Making ferry-boats like fairy palaces afloat.
Crowds on the decks, and, in the night wind’s crooning,
Sounds of lilting laughter and the music of the bands ;
Out on the beach there are fifty couples spooning,
Whisp’ring words of sweet import and clasping tender
hands.
(Seems to me that break’s not right; I hope the darned
thing stands.)
Roar of the streets and rattle of the tram-cars,
Bearing in their human freight to swell the noisy crowd.
Naught here for me but to sit and watch the d —n stars
Winking down to mock me ere they slink behind a cloud.
Glare' of the footlights—and then the curtain falling ;
After that the suppers and the girls upon our knees.
Red lips and wine. Ah, the siren city’s calling—
Calling in each crooning note that’s humming through
the trees.
(There’s that brute again. She’s scared of everything she
sees.)
Hush o’er the town. The busy streets are sleeping ;
All the pallid human slaves are resting for a space.
Darkness and gloom o’er the sullen harbor creeping.
Gone are all the fairy lights ; black shadows in their place.
Mayhap a girl at her window sits a-dreaming;
Gazing out toward the west with brown eyes that are
wet—
Wet with the hot tears that sometimes will come stream
ihg ;
Tears for the bygone years—tears of vain regret.
(There’s the beggar out again. I'll kill that devil yet.!)
N.S.W.
SPINIFEN.
Ernest Favenc writes :
In all that has been written about our Australian abo-
rigines, I have never seen any reference to their natural
politeness and urbanity. The usual idea of a blackfellow
—a true myall—is that his first notion of politeness
towards a stranger is the civilised one—“ to eave arf a
brick at ’im.” Nothing of the sort-though that becomes
his idea after a very short acquaintance with the white
man, and small blame to him. When you meet the real
mvall, and avoid outraging his sensibilities, you find him,
according to his lights, a well-bred gentleman. Some of
them make what might pass for a bow, and wave their
hand in dignified welcome. They desire you to be seated
(i.e., squatted), and always pull two or three tussocks of
orass for you to subside upon. When you inquire the
name of the creek, river, or water-hole you are on they
give it to you at once ; and when you try to imitate the
sounds they laugh pleasantly as if to say, “ Not bad, for a
first attempt.” Do you desire to know if there is water,
they reply by signs that there are seas, lakes, rivers. Are
there fish in them (a question generally put to judge of
permanency)? Immense quantities, you are told not
with the view of deceiving you, but out of politeness.
You will be pleased to hear that there is plenty of water ;
then why not please you? And anything in the camp is
yours, from a dusky maiden to a buck’s pet boomerang,
thouo-h offers of this kind are to be understood bpanisb
fashion, and you would be foolish to attempt to walk oft
with everything that in courtesy is offered to you. Ex-
ceptions to the rule are the older gins, who will roundly
abuse you, order you away, and threaten you with a
yam-stick. Nor can their men-folk prevent them. W hen
an old black amazon gets the floor she is as deaf to reason
as an adder, or a book-canvasser in hysterics.

No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-
taken in any case re contributions voluntarily sub-
mitted, whether sent by post or handed in ; and in
no case will MS. be returned unless stamps (of any
State) accompany.

drops at bedtime.
I tell all my friends up here the good news.

JOHN J. MURPHY.

Griffith and Allen, Patent Attorneys of
Sydney, have opened a branch of their business in
Melbourne —(Broken Hill Chambers, QueeA-st.),
under the management of Mr. H. P. Keogh, of
' Melbourne.*
The chief ingredients in successful business
organisation are brains and enterprise. And these vital
qualities have been used to such good effect by Dri
Thomas as to have produced a surprising perfection of
equipment and suniptuousness Of appointment at his
palatial Dental Establishment, in Swanston-street,
Melbourne. Not only are his operating surgeries fitted
with all the latest and best in pain and trouble saving*
appliances, and his waiting rooms luxurious with artistic
furnishings, but his mechanical d(partments, where the
most wonderful dental work is achieved, abound in the
latest inventions for turning out the beautiful, the
sound, and the effective in artificialteeth. One marvellous
feature in this respect is that a full set of teeth can be
supplied without a plate by bridging from' only two
remaining natural teeth. And this is undertaken Cy Dr.
Thomas at a much lower charge than obtains in Eng .•and
or America. In fact it is scarcely too much to say that if
any sort of improvement in dentistry were discovered in
the planet Mars to-day the doctor w'ould have it at his
remarkable surgeries the day after. Also the doctor
shows a sound knowledge of human nature in select;. »_•
such handsome as well as highly skilled staffs of both
sexes.. To be attended to by such Apollo-like craftsmen
and artists at their craft must help lady patients to forget
the last relic of discomfort that may yet linger in denta.
surgery in such a pain-abolishing establishment, while
even unimpressionable male patients must draw delight
from the clean and smart appearance of operators and
lady assistants, as well as from their clean and smart
operations.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
We shall certainly have to send Reid and
Joe (Dook to England to talk seriously to the
House of Lords, which has just passed a Socialis-
tic Bill for the appointment of a Public Trustee.
“ Exile ” : Re payment of N.S.W. old age
pensions. I know of one place where this has
been done for three years by the local post-
master. Am told the practice is general where
there is no branch of the Bank of N.S.W. If
these officials do it in some cases, why not in all?
Parramatta Asylum, now housing State
derelicts, was one time the mill where the re-
nowned Parramatta tweeds were first manufac-
tured by James and William Byrnes. The im-
porting policy works out its natural results, and
the home of young industry becomes the refuge of
old derelicts.
William Affleck, ex-M.P., wants Premier
Carruthers to annul at one stroke all exist.ng
appointments as Justices Qf the Peace in N.S. \v.
There are 7000 odd Jay Pays on the rolls, and no
Cabinet would wilfully alienate that number of
votes, though every Cabinet in turn condemns the
J.P. system as a nuisance.
Geo. Reid was very pathetic and very
characteristic in appealing to his last Sydney
meeting. “Didn’t I give you nice soft jobs?” he
whined. “Didn’t I give you jobs painting park
railings? You don’t get jobs like that since I left
office in N.S. Wales. It’s stone-breaking you get
now.” The self-confessed man of jobs—nice soft
jobs! ,
They formed a branch of the anti-Socialist
League up at Dubbo (N.S.W.) the other day, and
the local mayor became president. And about
the first thing the chief anti-Socialist did after
his election was to advocate that the local
hospital, which is hard-up, should be taken over
and run by the State. These leagues live right
up to the great yes-no principles of their founder.
At the Commercial Travellers’ Club dinner
(Melbourne), Bent cried out that General Booth
should have called first upon him, and not upon
Reid, regarding immigrants and settlers, and im-
plied that he would have granted requests for more
Sopulation, whereat he was cheered by the crowd.
>ut Bent was fooling them, for, asafact, General
Booth did first call on Bent, and was sent on from
him to Reid.
It is commonly reported that Labor
candidate Mat Reid lost the Burrum (Q-) election
through the visit of his namesake, .George—the
man with the eye-glass and the contradiction.
Australia’s half-Premier addressed a meeting at
Childers just about that time, and many people
took him for Mat, and said they wouldn t vote for
such a shocking person if he were ten times a
Labor man.
Sydney City Council is asking the Govern-
ment to pass an Enabling Bill, empowering it to
establish municipal libraries, art galleries, gym-
nasia, abattoirs, and dispensaries. But there is
little real work possible until a Greater Sydney is
achieved. Then control and ownership of all
harbor ferry traffic, tram and motor-’bus ser-
vices, water and sewerage service and lighting
should be aimed at.
Bishop Mercer (Tas.) argues that Tatter-
sail’s sweeps should be suppressed because, while
the Government obtains revenue from that source,
it will not impose adequate land-value taxation.
Does the Bishop really think that the two subjects
are related ? Governments have been chasing Tatt.
from State to State these many years, and not one
of them followed his expulsion by imposing an
adequate land-value tax.
George Reid’s trick of calling for cheers
for the King when a meeting is hostile seems to
have been inspired by Elijah Dowie. When the
crowd would not listen to him, ’Lije whispered his
wife to read the Bible, so that he could make out
they were ‘ 1 jeering at the Word.” Similarly, Reid
whispers in the S.M. Herald reporter’s ear a call
for cheers for King Ned. If the unhearing crowd
continues to hoot G. H. —well, it is disloyal.
The chief mechanical engineer of Maori-
land State railways remarked recently that the
construction of locomotives was proceeding satis-
factorily. The 10 engines that were being locally
built were as good as those produced m any part
of the world. Victoria, too, satisfactorily builds
its own engines. It is onlj N.S.W., seemingly,
that is so ignorant and uncivilised a State as not
to be able to do so (vide Sydney daily press).
Almost the most amazing thing in the
amazing N.S.W. Lands inquiry is the calm, blas-
tiferous, off-hand fashion in which land agents,
such as R. D. Meagher, undertook, for a “sub-
stantial fee,” to get the rent of State property
reduced from £1.13 fi ye&r to between £3O and
£50,” and to get the 14 years’ lease extended to 28
years, and things like that. Did these land agents
own the Minister and the Department body and.
soul—principally soul ? R. D. Meagher even used
the Parliamentary stationery—the letter-paper
and envelopes paid for by his suffering country—
to write to one of his clients—the one who was on
no account to pay the rent that he owed to the suf-
fering country. When a third party borrows our
own envelope to write to our own tenant telling
him to diddle us out of our own rent—it is too
much !
The Anti - Socialist Association, which
G. H. Reid labors over, bids fair to be the last and
most comic of the long line of political organisa-
tions he has founded or used in his own interest.
He began with the Patriotic Association in 1869
W. C. Windeyer was his favorite accomplice then
—and his path since is marked by the tombstones
of dead associations, the policy and. principles of
each being ignored or reversed when it had ex-
hausted its power to shove Reid farther forward,
or to prevent him sliding back.
“E.H.H.”: Seeing the great Liberal-
Labor victory in recent S.A. elections and the
good reception that Watson got when in Adelaide,
the Federal Liberal-Labor people should send one
or two of their top-notchers to address meetings
in the principal Vic. towns. -It would do no end
of good. Certainly Tom Mann has- spoken m
some Gippsland towns ; but what is the good of
him talking six hours a day and ‘ ‘ Awake ! Arise !
to the average cocky ? But plenty would go to
hear men like Watson, Hughes, Mahon, M‘Gregor
and Dawson.
When Watson comes into his own again,
the knowledge he gained on the Queensland trip
will be of benefit to Australia. At Halifax the
other day he learned :
On the Johnstone River there is a Chinese who is ready
to buy or lease any quantity' of cane land. This man de-
clares he can get sufficient Chinese labor from Port
Darwin and Singapore to do all the field work north of
Townsville. Recently practical experience was obtained
of the prevalence of Chinese immigration. A farmer met
a Chinese on the Johnstone River, and failed to make him
understand English. “He new boy,” said another
Chinese, “and no sabee.” That the law is being con-
tinually evaded is asserted on all sides, but no one knows
how it is done. All that everyone is sure about is that
more Chinese are seen about.
ANOTHER UNRELIABLE STEERSMAN.
The Labor party in Parliament still sinks the fiscal issue-the most urgent thing in Australian politics.
Terrified Crew (which voted for the captain at last election): “ Heavens, man ! Where are
you steering? ”
CAPTAIN: "Oh, I’ve nothing at all to do with that. It isn’t my business. I've sunk the
navigation issue."
A deputation of Vic. village settlers in-
formed -Minister Cameron the other day that
had no outlet for their produce unless they paid
12s. a ton to get it carried or sledged to the nearest
road, a mile and a half away, and no State school
for their children within three miles,, the path to
knowledge being covered with thick undergrowth.
During the past 14 years the settlers have pe-
titioned several Governments for a small grant of
money to make a rough road through the bush,
and now they are going to get it. That s how the
Bent Government scores. A little money covers
a multitude of sins. Previous Treasurers had
fewer sins, but less means of covering them.
“Grace Watson,” secretary to the Austra-
lian Women’s National League, makes a loud and
continuous racket because Labor-leader Watson
(who belongs to N. 9. W.) has, as she alleges, not
openly “disowned” certain anti-religious state-
ments which appeared years ago in the Idcsin
nublished in Victoria. Grace Watson herself is of
the political cult of Jos. Symes’ Liberator,
which was also Freetrade and anti-Labor; and as
she has not publicly “ disowned ’’ the frantically
atheistic articles which appeared in that publica-
tion people have a right to assume, bv her own
logic, that she believes in spelling God with a
small “g,” and depicting the Almighty as a
battered old artisan making planets with a turning
lathe.
Lever Bros., the soap people, seem to have
a solution ready for the returned Kanaka problem.
They are setting up copra plantations in the South
Seas, and want native laborers—thousands of
'them—and would give preference to the Kanakas
returned from the Queensland sugar plantations.
Even the-maddest Tory can hardly suggest that
Lever Bros, are cannibals who would eat the
Polynesian, or that he would be in any danger of
“relapsing into barbarism ’’.under their charge.
At Tokio, Japan, lately, the Liberals tried
to -hold an ordinary .peaceful political meeting to
select a candidate for the Japairese House of Repre-
sentatives, but the police scattered them before pro-
ceedings commenced. The force didn’t even wait
to hear if the speakers w-ere going to say anything
against the blessed Mikado. A Sydney daily
described the incident in an approving sort of way
under the heading “How Japan deals with
Socialists.” If the episode had happened in the
Bear’s country, it would have been headed
“ Another Autocratic Outrage.”
That eminent cormorant, the Van Diemen’s
Land Co., carries the huge gristle it calls its
heart on its sleeve. Its directors, at their London
meeting the other day, announced that its pros-
pects of increased protit in the future lay
in the increase in land values due to public
improvements (made at the taxpayers’ expense).
Pursuing the theme, the directors advised that
the co. should not sell the land, but lease it to
small farmers, so that it mightbe able to snatch the-
unearned increment when it came along. Tas-
mania is to put up the money to improve the
V.D.L. Co.’s property, and then pay extra to the
Y.D.L. Co. because of its own. improvements.
And Tasmania is too dull to see the swindle.
No wonjer the “pore farmer” gets
all sorts of concessions. In the new South-
Australian Assembly (42 members) there are
10 of him, not to mention three graziers,
a fruit-grower, and a chaff-merchant. But there
isn’t one single representative of the manufactur-
ing industries, barring, perhaps, Coneybeer, who
is a collar-maker. The farmer is, in fact, begin-
ning to take the place in Parliament that the
lawyer used to have.
Just about the time... when the
N.S. Wales Government was explaining copiously
that it had dug down into the very base and
cellar of the matter, and assured itself that
there was no legal means of preventing W. IS.
Willis leaving the country if he felt so inclined,
John Haynes was taking steps which resulted in
the sudden capture of W. N. Willis at Albany.
Now, either Premier Carruthers didn t want to
see the troublesome Willis back, or he chant
know much law, or he was too lazy and too careless
in public mattefs to look up the law he did know,
or John Haynes has fallen "into a dreadful hole.
If John isn’t in a large hole, then Joseph is in an
enormous orifice. One thing certain is that, when
the new r s arrived, John was looking about twice
his usual size, while Joseph fled to his home ill.
A department of Sydney Telegraph's new
page, “On the Soil,” is headed “Outside Our-
selves.” The editorial columns, devoted to ex-
plaining what the paper thinks is the meaning of
Socialism, is “ Beside Ourselves.”
Last Friday, N.S.W. Premier Carruthers,
who the previous’day had been more or less
attacked in the Assembly, gave evidence before
the Lands Commission. According to S.M.
Herald, he said that “the Sims Land Appeal
Court case was the only land transaction that
Willis ever brought to the office of Carruthers and
Wilson.” Same day, the Herald’s law report'
showed that Willis was suing J. H. Doyle “to-
recover the sum of £l2B claimed to be due for ser-
vices as land agent and for money paid on behalf
of the defendant,” and Mr. McManamy (Messrs.
Carruthers and Wilson) appeared for the plaintiff.
Evidently Carruthers sees worlds of difference'
about a land job and the recovery of money for'
doing the land job.
Alfred Deakin’s Ballarat speech, in which
he declared that he would fight for Protection
next session, moved both Sydney morning papers
to shrieks of anguish. They both explained to-
him frantically .that there were only two courses-
open ; he must be an anti-Socialist or a Socialist.
The tariff issue was sunk ; it was dead ; there was-
no such thing : it mustn’t be spoken of, because it
would upset Reid’s apple-cart; and Deakin “must
be on one side or another,” and must run after one
or other of the Tory papers’ two ghosts. The
Telegraph got so much off its head that morning
that it threw this strangely incomprehensible'
remark of oyer 100 words off its chest :
It is a matter for general congratulation that the pro-
fessional and scientific student is not to be unduly bur-
dened by .the necessity of acquiring a high standard of
knowledge in the subjects outside of those that are
-essential to his future prospects in the battle of life, and'
in this respect the framers of the curriculum appear to
have nicely adjusted the balance of requirements so that,
on the ofle hand, the student shall go forth frtfm the Uni-
versity flagrantly ignorant of the ordinary acquirements
of an educated person, while, on the other hand, his pro-
fessional studies shall not be retarded by an undue
devotion to subjects of pure culture.
When Premier Bent, of Victoria, made
his recent remarkable financial announcement by
candle-light, Premier Carruthers, of N.5.W.,, at
once fled into print and explained that he waa
quite as bright a financier as Bent, and would
have an even bigger'surplus. But this action on
Joe’s part is too thin. Bent promises that the
Victorian public debt will be less at the end of the
year than it was at 'the beginning, and'Jhat he will
devote an amount equal to all the land sale re-
venue to public undertakings. Carruthers bids
fair to add about £1,600,000 to the N.S.W. public
debt, arid charges all his public undertakings to
loans, and even if he has a surplus as large as
Bent’s he will, in addition to all'these borrowings,
have spent two-thirds of his land sale money as if
it were ordinary revenue. Moreover, in Victoria
the expenditure account is kept open till August,
so' that all possible claims may come in and be
charged against the year, and a Vie. surplus is
only arrived at after paying these claims. In
N.S.W., under the political swindle which Reid
devised, the expenditure account closes so sud-
denly on June 30 that a half-year’s interest on
certain loans which is paid a,way in London about
June 15 isn’t included.
“ Melb.” : Posing as a member of the
Imperial Federation League, I went to the initia-
tion of Alfred Deakin as president, in place of
Justice Holroyd. Alfred takes himself with
proper seriousness in this new billet, as he does at
the Bible-in-Schools League. The gathering was
of about 13, including four reporters, one of them
specially retained to pick up or take down every
diamond or pearl that dropped from the states-
man’s lips, for it was arranged that he should
speak an oration an hour and a-half long, to be
published In a pamphlet of 2000 circulation,'
whereby new blood is to be set pulsing through,
the veins of the mighty cause. With very inade-
quate stimulus —indeed, hardly any —Deakin,
launched away. I sat entranced for the whole
hour- and. a-half, like Adam listening to the angel
Raphael. When the magician ceased, Pouf!
the whole thing was gone. I cannot believe that;
Deakin persuades himself. He made out that
what we are doing now is only, in time, the mere;
geological—or shall I say astronomical —infini-
tesimal beginning of .the miraculous empire to be
compounded of Canada, South Africa, Australia,
Maoriland, England, Ireland, Scotland, India,:
Burmah, Hongkong, Singapore, a third of New
Guinea, a bit of Guiana, Bermuda, something in;
the Caribbean Sea, the Straits Settlement, and I
think the Straight Waistcoat, but I forget. It
was all folly—pretty folly, but folly.

Another Case of Tumor.
Doctors Advised Instant Operation.
VITADATIO
Has Removed it.
Read what Mr. ARSCOTT writes.
*
Dear Sir, — I think Vitadatio worthy of great praise as a
genuine remedy. It has saved me a few pounds expenses
in doctors and medicine. My wife had a tumor on her
neck, causing her much pain and alarm. The doctors,
when consulted, advised instant operation—there being
danger in delay—but my wife shrinking from this, we
consulted a herbalist, who wanted 6 guineas per quarter
for an indefinite period to remove the disease. Vitadatio
was then brought under our notice, and we resolved to try
it. By the time the fourth large bottle was used the
tumor had come right away, leaving only a mark on the I
neck wiiere it had been. There has been no return of
the trouble, and my wife has enjoyed good health since.
You are welcome to use this as you please,-1 am so
satisfied with what Vitadatio has done for my wife.

cpHE ingenuity ot the Japanese has never been more clearly
demonstrated than by the invention of the Hot-Box.
The poorest ’ricksha-man in Japan carries a Hot-Box and the
wealthiest nobleman would never be without one. In sick-
ness and in health the Body-Warmer is equally appreciated.
One of these handy little contrivanoes carried in a coat
pocket or in a lady's muff saves the bearer from feeling the
cold of winter. When travelling by coach, train or steamer,
a Hot-Box placed between the feet will keep you as warm
and cosy as if you were beside a bright coal fire. Clerks
and others engaged in sedentary avocations where there is no
heating appliance highly value the Hot-Box as an efficient
substitute for a stove or grate. For the almost instant
relief of Neuralgia, Toothaohe, Bronchitis, Influents, colic
and, in fact, of any ailment or pain in wbioh the application
of heat is beneficial, the Hot-Box is far preferable to any of
the usual methods, such as hot-water bottles, hot compresses,
&o. Alt these soon lose their warmth and have to be replaced ;
the Hot-Box. on the contrary, maintains a regular degree of
heat for several hours. It has the especial advantage of
being instantly available; wilhin one minute it is ready,
with no further trouble than merely striking a match and
setting light to the fuel. It will make a cold bed warm and
cosy in a few minutes. These are but a few of the many uses
.and advantages of the Hot-Box. The price is only 4s. 6d., in-
-cluding a supply of fuels and cariiago paid to any address,
•carefully paokcd In a box with full directions. Additional
fuels can he had at sny time at sixpence per packet (enough
for about forty hours' warmth), which makes it by far
the cheapest heating apparatus ever devised, l'lease bear in
mind that there is no trouble with the Hot-Box and no dan-
ger; it emits no smoke or smell, end it cannot under any
circumstantei set fire to anything else. Send postal note of
