On Becoming Great.
This is a recipe for making a reputation in Aus
tralia
Take of ability one grain, mix with a heaped-up hand
ful of impudence, thoroughly soak in alcohol. There is
no need to bake ; it will be accepted raw.
It is one of the curiosities of the Australian
temperament to stretch generosity in regard to
human failings to the point of folly. How often
is heard the sorrowful : “ There’s Bickley — a
wonderfully able chap Bickley. But unfortu-
nately he drinks. If that man would only keep
sober, he’d be the first in the country.” Investi-
gate Bickley, and it will be found that, some
time or other in his besotted career, he had a
lucid or unbeered interval, and did about two-
pence worth of honest work. His reputation as a
genius has been built on that —and drink. Be-
cause he does drink and doesn’t shine, it’s over-
charitably assumed that, were he to give up the
drink, he would, so to speak, put electricity to
shame for ever afterwards with his refulgence.
In private affairs, this doesn’t matter very
much. Whether Snaggs, who is always
sodden with beer, would, without beer, be a
nightingale of song or a painter of magic pictures
is, from the public point of view, immaterial. But
when the same sentiment obtrudes into politics it
is actively mischievous. More than one Australian
public man remains a public man chiefly because
he is a reckless sample of the giddy ox, or a very
bad specimen of the plain, every-day sot. It ii l
reckoned that beneath the flood of beer there is a
lurking genius which will astonish the universe
some day—when the beer gets off the top of it.
The beer is obvious, the genius is gratuitous
assumption, but beer must stand on something
or it would fall off, and that something, it is
often taken for granted, is genius. It would not
be good policy to utterly boycott from public life
the able man “ with a weakness.” But it is a mis-
take to rush to the other extreme and assume
transcending ability because a man’s character i 3
usually disguised in liquor. Such a disguise
should be a hindrance; it should be held that if a
man is scandalous and drunken, that is a special
reason why he should be called upon to more
fully justify his reputation for brilliance. Instead
of this, the drunken man often gets in where the
sober man is shut out, because if the sober man
doesn’t exhibit his mental capacity, it is assumed
that he has none. His rival can pull through on
the mental capacity he might show if only he were
sober enough to show it.
The Blindness of Britain.
The necessity for adequate representation of Aus-
tralian interests in London grows with each month
and each event. There is at present no means,
for example, by which Britain can get even a
glimmering of the Australian feeling in regard to
Asia. Even the English Radical papers, which
are ready to reprobate Chinese labor for the Rand,
do not understand Australia’s objection to Japanese
immigration. Thus, for example, Reynolds re-
cently :
Both Japs and Chinamen are at present heavily fined
for landing on Australian soil, as also they are for landing
in British Columbia, where a considerable number of
them are settled. So heavy is this fine, that to bring
their wives and families with them is out of the question.
Neither in Australia or British Columbia do the Orientals
compete with white labor. They employ themselves in
occupations which the white man will not willingly under-
take. They are market-gardeners, laundrymen, and
domestic servants. They regard it as a great hardship
that their women-folk cannot follow them to the lands of
their adoption, and this may give rise to trouble in the
future. We may remind the Australians, however, that
the Japs have never yet made any unreasonable inter-
national demands.
The Englishman is, wilfully or through stupidity,
blind to the fact that the chief reason against
Asiatic immigration in Australia is not the
economic, but the racial one; that Australians
want neither to brew their blood with nigger
blood nor face the American alternative of
frequently roasting a nigger—to band music and
a matinee girl audience —in order to keep him in
mind of the fact that he is a nigger, and must
not aspire to mix with white folk. Perhaps
if the British knew something of the strength
of White Australian feeling in the matter
of colored immigration, they would look at
their Asiatic relations in a somewhat different
light. Perhaps the knowledge would not make a
bit of difference. But we at least owe it to the
dear old mother country to let Britain know bow
we stand, so that, if another tea-tax incident is to
come, it will come deliberately and not as a result
of blind blundering. That duty we do not observe.
There are, in London, agencies to represent Aus-
tralian butter and Australian meat and Australian
debt, but none to represent Australian national
feeling.
The Bulletin gets a plaintive little letter full of
hard and bitter truth from a place 300 miles hack
from the coast. The writer had “ gone on the
land,” as the papers and politicians told him to;
and he tells some of the things that are happening
to him: —
We wanted some salt; you can buy it in the city for
75., but the railway freight cost us Bs. My boy has had
to go about 100 miles away to get work. For a treat we
sent him a bottle of butter, a cake, and a bottle of honey.
How much do j'ou think it cost U 3 to send that little lot ?
Four-and-ninepence ! It costs us close on £2OO a year for
food; and then we live dreadfully plainly —I wouldn’t
like to tell you how plainly; I’m sure lots of people
wouldn’t believe me.
And the battler who has “ gone on the land,” 300
miles.out, and has to pay Bs. freight on a 7s- bag
of salt, and 4s. 9d. to give his youngster a bit of
butter in a bottle —he has to pay at the same rate
to get his uncertain produce to the market. Yet
300 miles isn’t so very far (Moree is 414 miles
from Sydney, and Pera Bore 515). And all up
and down tlie coast there are millions of acres of
from first-class to decent country, and not a
graduated land-tax to force the holders to open it
up for settlement. So the men go out 300\ or4oo
miles, and when they get a pot of honey and a
bottle of butter call it a banquet, and pay 4s. 9d.
for carting it.
“ E airplay ” on S’African politics, showing that
there is a borrowing issue there too
Re B. R. Wise on S’African politics (B. 1 S/5/’0o). The
writer seems to have a fair grip of the situation for so
short a stay in the country, but is mistaken over the
efficient Civil Service. That is the real stumbling-block
in the way of the races working together amicably. The
officials are all new to the country and know nothing of
its language and customs. How should we like it here, if
we had officials in the Post Office w;ho only spoke German ?
That is the position the Boers are in now. The language
question crops up everywhere. Any man in business
has to learn Dutch ; why not the officials, too? If the
native laws v ere properly administered there would have
beeu no need of outside labor. But extra taxation is put
on the whites, and even the old taxes are not collected
from the natives. In the Orange River Colony the
revenue last year was over £1,600,000. The highest before
the war was never £500,000, and yet before the war all
public works were built out of revenue. Now-, under
British officialdom, even repairs are paid out of loan
moneys and all public works are charged to loans. These
are the things the Boers do not like ; they say they will
be there when all the gold and diamonds are gone, and
when the English are gone too, and they (the Boers) will
have to pay the debt.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
“F.” : The uncompleted second test should at
least take the edge off the grumbling of those Australians
who profess to see nothing but absurdity in the English
system of limiting cricket matches to three days. An
unlimited allowance of time for the second test would
have made it a “soft thing” for the Englishmen, not
necessarily on their merits, but on the happening of the
weather. Played in Australia on the same weather con-
ditions, the definite result would not have been a fair test.
Given a lead of 2F r ' runs, with five wickets in hand, to
either side at the end of the second day’s play, it goes as a
matter of course that heavy rain on the third day robs
the “out” side of all chance to make a big score on its
second innings. In that case the “ draw ”is a fair escape
from defeat. Moreover, the English system is always fair
enough, whereas the Australian system of playing a
match to a finish, for the sake of the finish, is distinctly
unfair more often than not. Three days play in England
is nearly equivalent to four days in Australia. With a
batsman’s wicket for both sides, the side that can’t win in
three long days is not decisively stronger than the other.
With rain happening during the progress of a match, the
side which gets all the worst of the luck is entitled to the
chance of a draw.
* * * *
With July begins the one had piece of arrange-
ment in the Australian cricketers’ programme. On the Ist
they finish a game at Bristol, in the south ; then rush up
to Leeds, in the north, for the third test; back to South-
ampton-extreme south ; up to Derby (most of the way
to Leeds again) ; back to Bath (within 10 miles or so of
Bristol) ; and then right away (past Derby and Leeds) to
Edinburgh, to try and whack the ball into the Grass-
market.
* * * *
Marylebone C.C. has now taken a hand in the
shindy which is going on among the cricket authorities
in Australia. Having received two invites to send out the
next team—one from the new Board of Control of the
Associations and the other from the Melbourne Cricket
Club—the English body declines to do anything until it
gets only one invite, and that from some institution which
is qualified to speak as one having authority. Away back
in 1887-8, in consequence of disagreements in the Aus-
tralian cricket camp, two English teams, one under
Hawke and the other under Shrewsbury, visited Aus-
tralia. There was hardly enough glory and profit to go
round, and the M. 0.0. wants no more confusion of that
kind.
* * * *
“B.B.”: The exclusion of Gehrs from the
earlier matches (and the subsequent failure) is partly
explained in a letter received t’other day. “Climatic
conditions and dietary changes had an unwholesome
effect, and he was compelled to consult a physician.”
Most of the members were similarly affected, and the
early outlook of the team was o’ershadowed in mystery
and doubt. Gehrs has played better in later matches,
and it will only require a return to Australian form for
the young South Australian to occupy a place among the
best —a miserable best at that.
* * * *
The most recent exhibition by the Australian
XI. against Essex is absolutely the worst of the tour. The
cable described the wicket as fast and good, yet the
Australians, against nearly the worst English county, only
totalled 100 and 202. There were 34 sundries ; while four
Australian players failed to score—Gregory getting a
“pair.” The only redeeming features were Laver’s 10
wickets for 129, and Hopkins’ 67 not out.
* * * *
By suffering defeat at the hands of Essex, the
Australian team, with but a third of the programme
through, has lost its problematical chance of beating
the performance of the 1902 team, which got through
the whole 39 matches with but two defeats. Though one
of the weakest counties, Essex has often upset the calcu-
lations of visiting teams—notably the 1899 team, which
included nine of the present crowd.
* * * •
Death of Bradbridge at Dulwich Hill (N.S.W.)
leaves only Hilliard and George Gilbert alive of the first
N.S.VV. cricket team that played Victoria in Melbourne.
In 1858 George Gilbert carried out his bat for 118 runs at
a match in Sydney Domain—the biggest score made till
then in the State.
* * * *
The N.S.W.-Queensland Rugby match was lost
to the former owing to the failure of the Cornstalk kick-
ing. N.S.W. was apparently gone in the feet, and the
indications are that had the team possessed but one
reliable bootist it would have won the match. But it was
Wickham’s off-day, and nobody else was given much
opportunity, and most of the Cornstalks failed to do any-
thing with even what opportunity came their way ; so
the game which ought to have ended in a decisive defeat for
the Bananalanders resulted in a victory for them by 8 to
6 points. Tries, penalty kicks and marks were mulled
by the Walers, all falling short or going outside the posts.
Presumably the Queensland forwards bustled the Corn-
stalk shooters, as they seem to have been exceptionally
fast. But on the other hand the Cornstalks had an un-
usual number of opportunities to score from the field
and threw them all away. At half-time N.S.W. was
leading by three to nil, having scored a try. In the
second half the Bananalanders came with a mighty rush.
They had speed, and they used it, scoring a goal and a
try in rapid succession. After saving several times by
prompt forcing, N.S.W. managed to struggle across the
line once more, but, as usual, failed to add the points
which would have made the game a draw. It was a sorry
business, for with their opportunities N.S.W. should have
practically won the match in the first half. *
The defeat of N.S.W. by the Maoriland
“Soccer” team was expected, for it was generally con-
ceded that the Metropolitan was a stronger crowd than
N.S.W. Still, as a matter of sober fact, but for the misfor-
tune of losing a couple of good men in the second half,
the Cornstalks would probably have won. They played a
fine game, in spite of the fact that they had had prac-
tically no practice together, and the end of the first half
found the scores—N.S.W. 3, Maoriland 1. It was while
N.S.W. played shorthanded in the second half the Maori -
landers scored five goals while the Cornstalks made one.
Under the ordinary conditions, the chances are that the
N.S.W. team would have surprised the critics by winning
by a larger margin than the Metropolitans did a week ago.
* * * *
The big match between Fitzroy and South Mel-
bourne teams, played at Sydney Cricket Ground, under
Australian rules, was the closest of even things. The
teams seemed to be so nicely balanced that the slightest
bit of smart individual play would decide the issue.
The fourth quarter of the game was played in failing
light, and in the confusion Fitzroy seemed to get the
luck, and the game ended in a victory for it by 9 goals 10
behinds to 8 goals 9 behinds ; or 64 to 57 points.
* * *
How are the mighty crumpled up ! The
hitherto unbeaten Glebe, the hard dribbling team which
had downed every club it met, barring Sydney (and
against Sydney it made a draw), struck Cannae and Liao-
yang and Leipsic all at once when it met South
Sydney on Saturday. Fifteen thousand people were
squeezed into the uncomfortable Sports Ground.
Glebe had four of its best men away two with
the Inter-State team, and two on the sick list. South
Sydney was only short of one of its cracks, but
even then the wreck was something unusual. Twenty-
four points to nil in first-grade Rugby is a serious
thing to contemplate. It is very doubtful if the
absent ones would have made much difference. Glebe
back division was not in the same street as that of South
Sydney at any time ; but General Opinion said that the
Glebe forwards were so superior to those of South Sydney
that if the South’s halves could be smothered, so as to
prevent them feeding the three-quarter line, the trick
was done. As usual, General Opinion was wrong.
South Sydney forwards, though lighter than those of
Glebe, were faster, and if they couldn’t dribble they could
pass. Also, the scrum half and the five-eighth played a
beautiful game, and there was no hope of smothering
them, so Glebe was beaten on every line. South Sydney
is now at the head of the list with 14 points. Glebe, of
course, comes next with 13 points. Western Suburbs
and Newtown follow with 11 each.
Owing to the excitement over the South Sydney-
Glebe match on Saturday the other Rugby contests were
but poorly attended. University downed the unfortunate
Eastern Suburbs (the club that has lost the whole eight
matches, and has not yet even drawn its bye) by 11 to 7.
This was a much better stand than anyone expected
E.S. to make. Balmain was off color, as Western Suburbs
rolled it up by 16 to 6 ; and Sydney District walloped the
North Shore men by 15 to 9.
* • * •
While the Victorian League football teams,
Fitzroy and South Melbourne, were giving the Cornstalks
an exhibition game in Sydney, and Collingwood stood on
its dignity and sulked over the League’s refusal to allow
it to play ex-Westralian Rowell, the other five League
teams combined to play Ballarat, and put the statued
city’s representatives through in sudden style by 110
points to 29. The sizeable Crowd that witnessed the rout
wished afterwards it had joined the 12,000 spectators
on the Richmond ground, where the S. Australians
beat the Victorian Association by 8 goals 14 behinds to 6
goals 19 behinds. The Vic. League fell out last year with
the Croweaters over the distribution of the “ gate ” —the
eternal “ gate w —and played W.A. and Ballarat instead.
This year Adelaide determined to have a match with the
Association (the ten minor clubs) and the result was the
very best game of the season, with close scoring and an
exciting finish. The Croweaters were a younger team,
many being mere boys, but they were faster, and
quicker at passing the ball when blocked, also their
high marking was first-class; but their kicking was
poor. Though the scores don’t indicate it, at least
half their behinds should have been goals. It made
for added excitement that they weren’t, for in the last
quarter the Vies, steadily crept up to within two points
ofS.A. ; then the latter got another behind. The Vies,
replied similarly ; but in the last five minutes the Crow-
eaters rushed the ball down determinedly and notched a
goal—and the game was won. In this match, for the
first time, the players wore numbers—cloth placards on
the back, 18 inches by 12 —and they proved the failure
they were expected, being undistinguishable at a distance
in the dull wintry light, especially on the black-and-white
striped S.A. uniforms. Also, once the game got interest-
ing, nobody bothered about them.
4r * * *
Wellington (M.L.) Rugby Union is dealing out
“ stoush ” pretty heavily. For using language during a
recent match a' player was “ outed ” for five years—an
attempt to make the disqualification “ life ” being lost by
one vote. The referee of the match complained that
language had been heaved at him, and also that he was
kicked hy barrackers ; as a result, one offender was denied
admittance, to %11 grounds under the eye of the Union
for three years." Heavy penalties are the only way to
suppress the wreckers of football.
• • • *
The inclusion of George Smith in the M.L.
team for Britain was one of the surprises of the tour.
Two years ago (after being amateur champion hurdler
and flat-racer, prior to which he was a jockey) he defi-
nitely retired to private life ; but the chance of the trip
to Bull-land was too alluring, and he donned the guernsey
and got the reward. Smith’s pace makes him a very
showy footballer ; but, as with most champion athletes,
there is the fear of injury lurking in his movements.
* * * •
Australian football enthusiasts in W.A. don’t
like the methods taken to introduce Rugby and “ Soccer ”
there. The Australian game has held undisputed sway for
years, but imported school teachers are trying to
substitute the ideas of dear old England and, in some
cases, have gone so far as to prohibit the boys playing
Australian football.
* * * *
Only one lightweight figures in M.L. Rugby
team picked to kick England—Stead, of Invercargill
(10st.). There’s one other under list., five are under
12st., ten under 13st., seven under 14st., and the re-
maining unit is a 14.6 forward, who acts the part of
guiding elephant in the scrums. Jimmy Duncan’s ton-
nage isn’t included in this schedule ; his weight is the
large weight of experience, gleaned during a period of
half a century, or thereabouts.
* • * •
Lieutenant Bill’s name is struck out of the two
Cups, and the Caulfield hero of 1902 may be counted
among the bygones. Bill was a one-race horse. At
sundry times during the past 2£ years he threatened to
return to “ form ” but the threat was never fulfilled.
• * * *
A yearling colt of doubtful parentage (by Come-
dian or Priam from Milkmaid), now in work at Fleming-
ton, has been given the ridiculous name of Comprimil—
formed of the first three letters of the name of each of
the “ accused.”
* * * *
Spite of the fact that, reckoning on previous
public form, the big event at Warwick Farm races (Satur-
day) looked a gift for Kelso’s colt Fanshawe, owner never
invested a shilling on him, and many hard-headed punters
who had already backed the nag hedged because he
looked sore. There was no soreness in the way he ran,
however ; after always being handy from end to end, Fan-
shawe owned the race at the home turn. Luck was
against Kelso in another event same day—his lepper,
Clodian, succeeded at Canterbury previous Saturday, but,
having been “ off his feed ” for a few days, he raced at
Warwick Farm absolutely unbacked by the stable. And
then he won as he liked, and twice in one day the owner
was left thinking of what might have been.

Last Kalgoorlie race meeting provided a couple
of big dividends. Kamballie Hurdles winner Minilyahoy
paid £95 125., and Trial Handicap winner Agnostic
£B9 4s.
* * * *
A New York pawnbroker has been inspired
with the notion of carrying his trade to the racecourses
on a specially-devised motor-car. If the punters are
suffering a more disastrous day than usual the licensed
usurer might take in hundreds of pledges before the last
race, and some of his clients could actually put their
shirts and boots on their off-chance.' The spectacle of
Moses Moses motoring home with the jewellery and
under-garments of a blasphemous multitude would be
comfort and joy for the anti-betting crusaders, anyhow.
“ Melb.”: Talking of veteran jockey Higgerson,
I have just parted from one who knew him well, the brisk
and active Sam Chifney, who rode the Banker to victory
in the Melbourne Cup of 1863, 42 years ago, and he would
excite no surprise if he appeared in the pigskin at Flem-
ington to-day. The next Cup winner in seniority I know
is Billy Anderson, rider of The Quack, in 1872.
* » * *
The bookmaker who lays “ tote odds ” has been
having a rough time in Auckland (M.L.). A sportsman in
a country town despatched several wires to various
bookies, and suspicion was aroused owing to these mes-
sages, which all laid money on the right horse, arriving
after the races were over. Still, they purported to have
been despatched before the race, so the books paid, and
the sportsman won some £2BO. Later on, being still
gnawed by suspicion, the turf accountants placed the
matter in the hands of the police, and asked them to in-
vestigate whether the messages were not really sent after
the races were over, and the wrong hour of receipt put on
them by a hired telegraph minion. The investigation
showed, however, that all was as it should be, but that
the telegraphists had not been over smart in transmitting.
Then the detectives, who didn’t want their time to be
wholly wasted, summoned the bookies for illegally laying
“ tote odds,” and got eleven fines of £2O each.
* * » «
Big Jim Stanbury plugs along laboriously on
the Parramatta River for the match with Towns for the
world’s sculling championship; and the surplus flesh is
rapidly disappearing. Still his work isn’t all that could
be desired, and, in early parts of his sprinting, there is a
clumsiness which tells of long absence from fast boat-
pulling. Towns, who is a much smaller and more wiry
athlete, reckons that only five weeks’ work is necessary to
beat Stanbury. “Old man” Bill Beach says Stanbury
will win.
* * * *
Likely that, after the Towns-Stanbury sculling
match, veterans Bill Beach and Peter Kemp will settle a
question of superiority. Beach has issued a challenge, at
which his old rival Kemp promptly snapped. The pro-
posed match is oreating much interest—more, in fact,
than the championship struggle.
* * * •
Sandwiched among the dog-trials, at Melbourne
Exhibition, last Saturday, was a cock-crowing contest.
Angry male chooks had been kept in the dark all the
morning, and when brought out they naturally supposed
it was breakfast time, and let go. The winner turned up
in E. O’Donnell’s brown Leghorn, which cock-a-doodle-
doo-ed 26 times in 15 minutes, the next bird being a good
second with 25 cock-a-doodles. A lazy Brahmapootra
was last with a measly 10 doodles. The cock-crowing
contest will probably catch on, unless it is eclipsed by a
worm-catching contest for hens.
* * * *
Pretty well the same players play in much the
same old way, with very nearly the same results, year
after year for the amateur billiard supremacy of N.S.W.
The uninitiated onlooker, who knows what an amateur
athlete should be, finds himself sorely puzzled trying to
think out what a billiard amateur really is—this through
seeing him under other conditions at other times and
places. Last week the holder (M. Spencer) emphasised
his right to the title, by beating in the final his fellow-
finalist of the previous year—ex-champion J. B. Belfield —
by 133. Play was mostly slow and uninteresting, and
almost an exact repetition of that shown by the same
pair 12 months before. There’s nothing to enthuse over
in amateur billiards in Sydney.
* * * *
The meeting of the hitherto unbeaten baseball
teams, Paddington (which holds the premiership) and
Woollahra, at Rushcutters Bay (Sya.), resulted in disaster
to Paddington. Jawbone Neild’s suburb behaved as
though he had been reciting poetry at it, and played
a wretched game. Woollahra put its ears right back, and
its best leg right forward, and won by 24 points to 12.
Woollahra now walks alone at the head of the list with
16 points to its own cheek, and Paddington plods respect-
fully behind it with a plain dozen.
* * * *
The licking by 5 to 1 which Marrickville
lacrossers inflicted on Burwood will teach the fellows
from the capitalistic suburb to always keep one eye on
their own back-yard. Marrickville played a good defen-
sive game, and at the slightest opening made for the
rather carelessly guarded goal of their opponents. At
the last, when things were getting desperate, Henriques,
Burwood’s goal-keeper, actually came out and joined in
the attack on Marrickville’s base ; but it was too late.
At North Sydney, Balmain won by 6 to 1. Balmain
played a steady game of consistent pressure, while North
Sydney was aggressive in fits and starts.
■n * * *
The surprise in Sydney hurling on Saturday was
the “ bucking-up ” of Tempe-Leichhardt, a club that, right
through the first round, was invariably beaten, though it
usually put in a big struggle. Redfern was very confident,
and, starting at a gallop, scored a point and a goal in
short order. Then McGowen’s electorate was astonished
to find Tempe-Leichhardt making a great rally, and
before it knew what was happening T.-L. had scored a
goal. After that the Redferners took no chances, and
the hyphenated team let none slip, and the result was a
game that was good to see. A perspiring first half ended
with the score at 10 all. In the second half Redfern
attacked with energy, and gained a slight lead, but T.-L.
pulled itself together, and by fast play scored the third
goal, and regained the lead by two points. After that
Redfern snorted with disgust, and its game became a
tearing, howling fray. Result: T.-L., 16; Redfern, 15.
* * * ■ *
The wrestling match between Clarence Weber
and Buttan Singh will eventuate at Fitzgeralds’ Circus
building, Melbourne, on the night of Saturday, July 1.
Prayers are being offered up for the success of the white
man, and athletes who have not yet prayed will please
take this as an intimation to do so at once. The Pathan
party doesn’t feel so delightfully sure as it did ; it begins to
think about what Hackenschmidt taught the Australian.
Weber is quite big enough to get the “ giant swing ” on
with Buttan if he has the knowledge and strength to do
it—and Buttan doesn’t like the swing. Anyway, the
Pathan will find the Australian a very much tougher
customer than when he choked him into insensibility in
the previous meeting. Weber says that now, when he
understands it, Singh is quite welcome to have his strangle
hold thrown in. But to give it would be foolish. Buttan
would want next to scratch and bite.
* * * *
Balmain Cycling Club (amateur) which has
already won one Dunlop Inter-Club Premiership Shield
for its very own, is out after the second bauble with
which the big tyre company has replaced the thing used
by the gods in the Iliad to stop blue metal. If the club
can only win a few more shields it will be able to go out
for its club runs in the form of a Roman tortoise. On
Saturday there were nearly 1000 cyclists out at Home-
bush to see the first heats' won by Balmain and Enfield
Clubs. Eastern Suburbs, Annandale, Marrickville and
Glebe-Toxteth clubs were beaten, Annandale and Glebe-
Toxteth being biffed right out of the competition. The
remaining clubs which have to be dealt with are North
Sydney and Curzon Park. The latter is a tough proposi-
tion.
# * * *
Swimmer Kieran arrived in London last
Wednesday with just 10 days in which to become
acclimatised and to prepare for the exacting Mile
Championship. To get ready for the “ King’s Cup,”
under conditions mostly new to him and which
will require a great deal of practice, he has only 17
days. Delay was caused through the fears of the Sydney
fund committee that once Kieran left subscriptions
would cease to come. “An English authority” is re-
ported in one of the Sydney dailies as having stated that
Kieran would, “ like all other swimmers from the same
part of the world who had preceded him, find the water
too cold, and consequently shape short of his best form.”
Bunkum ! Dick Cavill, Fred Lane, and Jack Hellings
performed at least as well in England as in Australia, and
one or two of Cavill’s and Lane’s feats in the Cold Country
exceeded anything they ever did in Australia before or
since. Dick Cavill had just about the same time for
preparation as Kieran before swimming - in the Mile, and
was badly beaten, but the winner—Jarvis—took care
never to meet Dick afterwards excepting in the long
distance (sm. 60yd.) event which took place on the Thames
two weeks later.
N.S.W. Amateur Athletic Association has
officially endorsed the season’s records —lour by English-
man Shrubb (two miles, 9.33 3-5 ; three miles, 14.54 J ;
four miles, 20.0 3-5; five miles, 25.4 3-5); Victorian
Wheatley, 880yds. in 1.56 4-5; Nigel Barker, 220yds. in
21 4-ssec. ; Barker and Yankee Duffey, 100yds. in lOsec.
* * * *
Neil-Williams fight, at the new Melbourne
Athletic Club (formerly the “ Demo.”), was a lively 20-
round strife. Neil’s usual smothering tactics did not
prevent Williams pushing a useful left all over his face till
the alterations in his countenance were quite noticeable.
Williams showed a lot of Clever footwork throughout.
Round five saw Neil floored and groggy, just able to stay
till the gong went. The remaining rounds were very even
fighting. Some of Neil’s blows were rather low and wild,
and his opponent’s small and dodgy head evaded most of
the Yankee’s endeavors. Referee Basto awarded Wil-
liams the victory on points. A large and critical audience
accepted the fight as a good display of science mingled
w'ith gore.
For a pretty display of crafty boxing, the Keys-
Turner meeting at Sydney National Sporting Club, last
week, was a champion exhibition. Turner was looking
for gore, but owing to the deft way Ilock would hit him
three times in each eye and then clinch, or simply slide
out of reach, Turner was unable to find what he sought.
Hock’s treble-action blows, in which he appears to hit his
victim three times off one lunge, are curious to watch. If
only Keys had some stamina, some steam, or some plain
devil behind them, they would be very destructive. But
as Keys works them, they are merely bright exhibitions.
The glove contest was awarded to Keys on points, no
other verdict being possible.
* • * *
For a vigorous, enthusiastic banging match, the
little disturbance at Sydney Ga:°ty Club between Micko
Walsh, of Melbourne, and Ned Murphy, featherweight
champion of Australia, would be hard to surpass. It was
cheerful while it lasted, but it was painfully brief,
although in all probability the man who got so suddenly
banged out was quite satisfied with the duration of it.
They rained blows upon each other, and Walsh stopped
a couple with his chin, swayed, caught another on the
frame of his eye, and went down bang. On rising he
caught a whirly-wirly on the jaw, and collapsed once
more. Before he could be counted out, the thing you
dry your face with was thrown up. There was no doubt
about the genuineness of that fight.
* * * *
The entries for the road race from Bathurst to
Sydney, promoted by the Wallace-Continental Tyre and
Rubber Co., under the auspices of the N.S.W. League of
Wheelmen, will close on July 29th. Handicaps will be
announced on August sth, and acceptances will close on
the 12th. The total prize list amounts to £l5O, and the
first prize is £5O in beautiful golden sovereigns. Special
prizes will be awarded to the fastest competitors from the
Northern, Southern and Western districts. Entries
should be addressed to the Wallace-Continental Tyre and
Rubber Co., 7 Wynyard-street, Sydney.*

manufacture of locomotives the other day was

in London. And what does the promotion of
local industry amount to compared with the iob
of Mr. C. W. Darley, in London ? J

It is up to John Stinson, Geo. Pile, and G.
Reid to cable promptly to tlie King drawing his
attention to the fact that the little Prince of
Wales is undermining the throne and putting the
sanctity of the ’ome in deadly peril. The proof
of it is in a cable which mentions that Wales the
other day, “ inaugurated the London County
Council’s steamboat service on the Thames.”
This is the newest bit of Socialism in England ;
and Socialism, according to the most eminent
members of the Bogey party in Australia, means
chaos for the King, the Crown, and the home.
These things evidently haven’t occurred to either
Edward or his advisers, else, no doubt, the
little prince would have been already cast
into a dark, damp dungeon ; and it is the obvious
duty of J. Stinson and G. Pile, as the leaders
of the one clear-sighted party in the Empire, to
open the Royal eyes. It may be a hard thing for
a father to cut off the career of a son in the full
flush of his early manhood ; but the father, as J.
Stinson and the rest will no doubt point out,
must remember that he is a King as well as a
parent. The alternative, of course, is that Wales
didn’t know what he was doing. But that is ab-
surd ; for when Wales was in Australia, didn’t
the Stinson-Pile party assure him over and over
again that he was the very embodiment of wisdom
and knew practically everything ?
»
One amazing circumstance about the
Dunedin (M.L.) police-burglary cases is that ex-
Constable Moses, who got five years “hard,” had
two previous convictions against him. How he
gained admission to the force is a mystery. Moses
certainly went looting in S’Africa with one of the
first contingents, as many bad characters did, and
got into the Permanent Artillery on his return, but
on applying to be drafted to the police an official
was told off to report on his character and past
career. Apparently it was a time when officials
didn’t inquire very hard about the ex-cohen-
tingenter, so Policeman Moses —the mere fact of a
policeman being named Moses should have aroused
suspicion—had a chance to wear a uniform and
burgle liberally when people thought he was
guarding their property. The other two police-
men, who got three and four years respectively,
for breaking into the peaceful homes of Dunedin,
appear to have been led away by Moses. People
named Moses have been leading citizens, ever
since that old unpleasantness in Exodus. The
Commissioner, by the way, has issued a pathetic
appeal asking that the public will still have faith
in the integrity of the force in spite of Moses, but
the public would like first of all to know how
many more ex-farm-burners there are in that
misguided body.
Now that the Victorian Labor leagues have
taken up the question of electing a convention to
draw up a scheme for amalgamating the many
municipalities of Melbourne into a Greater Mel-
bourne, there seems a chance of something being
done. As soon as the matter is taken out of the
hands of aldermen and entrusted to a popular
Convention, elected on any kind of a liberal fran-
chise, success becomes possible. And when Labor
leagues take up matters like this, and show that
they have a platform broad enough to take in all
great public questions, and not only those that are
strictly labor questions, their position cannot but
improve. It is only the want of a sufficiently wide
platform which prevents the Labor party domi-
nating Australia.

AT POVERTY POINT.
From Edmund Downey’s recently published book of
reminiscences : “ The danseuses at a rehearsal of ‘ Faust’
were conducting themselves a little frivolously at one of
the demon scenes, when the ballet-master was heard to
say, 1 Now, then, lydies, recollect you ain’t dancing on
’Ampstead ’Eath ; you’re dancing in ’ell.’ ”
Dan Fitzgerald, of the circus, leaves shortly for New
Caledonia, in search of sunshine undiluted with cold
wind.
W. S. Gilbert’s stern suppression of gags in his Savoy
operas was led up to by the havoc made of his earlier
plays by gagging comedians, in the days when he was not
a large enough personage to command obedience. About
the worst offender was J. B. Buckstone, who packed with
heavy grossness of his own the character of Chrysos in
“ Pygmalion and Galatea ” —a part well played recently in
Sydney by G. W. Carey.
“ Melb.” : Miss O’Neil, in “ Magda,” shows us why she
failed in London and next time she goes there she may be
treated with respect. Her first act, the second of the
play, was formerly infused with a crude power, quite out
of keeping. She failed to recognise that Magda was a
highly-cultured woman of the world. She has taken her-
self to pieces, and put herself together again, with blends
of Duse, Bernhardt, and Mrs. Campbell. Whether she
saw them or not, I don’t know, but they all seem to be
there. She is strikingly like Bernhardt in some moments.
She follows Duse’s example, in eschewing make-up in the
third and fourth acts. Very courageous, for she looks
almost plain, but there is truth and reality.
Grace Palotta’s singing lessons in Vienna and Paris, of
which she wrote so much to fond friends on this side,
have evidently spoilt even the tootle tricks she employed
successfully out here. A ’Frisco paper remarks :
“ Grace Palotta, who seems to be regarded as the biggest
fish among the new-comers, is a good-looking young
woman, with a vivacious expression and a tendency to
sing all out of tune. But she is rather dashing in boy’s
clothes, and is so blissfully unconscious of her deviations
from the correct pitch that people accept her as some-
thing to look at and not to listen to.”
Brough and Flemming are advertising their “ Walls of
Jericho ” comedy with a fac-simile letter from a cockney
vicar which is quite interesting, for its simple admission
that the giddiest people for the most part never go to
church to get moral lessons. “ Your play,” wrote the
shepherd, “ must either fail utterly, or it must make the
Smart Set writhe. Why we clergy are so keen on what
you are doing is, I fancy, this : We are for ever trying to
preach such truths —but we never have the audience.
The Mormons are not there. A West End theatre is a
place of worship they still attend, and they have got to
listen to what you say, and I think it must flash a sudden
light into many a long-drugged conscience.” The un-
conscious jealousy in the holy man’s reference to the
helpless condition of the theatre audiences is quite
pathetically expressed, “They have got to listen.” In
the church they avoid it by going to sleep. In the
theatre they are too much interested to sleep.
San Francisco Examiner publishes a hugely-illustrated
interview with Lydia Yeamans-Titus in which her hus-
band, the trim and voluble Titus, has most to say. Titus
talks of the wayback time when a gallery interruption of
his wife’s musical monologues lifted him from his seat on
the piano-stool and made him outwardly and inwardly
mad. “ I used to get up and raise the very devil with the
audience. I used to make gestures and holler at them.
But not now ; you won’t find me doing that now.” Lydia
remarks that Titus is “getting wise,” and his rational
estimate of the labor involved in earning their joint
salary suggests that he is at least wiser than his average
brother pro. Titus doesn’t claim compassion for his
clever little wife on account of her mental strain, and so
forth. He doesn’t talk of “the wear and tear of an
artist’s life ” like some of the music-hall toilers, who sing
three songs a night and change their precious ditties
twice a year. He says : “ We’ve been living together for
twenty-one years, and we don’t feel a day older than when
we started. In this business it’s the pace that ages you.
Tiiere isn’t enough work in vaudeville to kill a fly. The
pace is for those that care to run it. Our greatest dissi-
pation after the show is an apple and a biscuit.”
Bruce Smith, who gave it as one of the
reasons why he should be returned to the Federal
Parliament that “he had never sent any of his
children to a Roman Catholic school,” now raises
a sectarian issue in connection with his precious
Empire League. “ None but Protestant schools,”
he said, “ had joined in the Empire celebrations.”
The statement was not a fact—Smith had to back
down a moment later by saying that he referred
only to the metropolitan schools, and even that
qualified statement was probably not a fact. But
why should Smith, who declares he never sent any
of his children to a R.C. school, mention it, unless
to raise again the sectarian snakes ? The Reid-
Srnith anti-Socialism will be found, before the
elections come on, to resolve itself into sectarian-
ism.
London Daily Mail, on the word of its
alleged Sydney correspondent, lately set going an
amazing Stinking Fish yarn, which has been
grabbed by almost every other English paper that
happens along. The yarn was to the effect that
“ the White Australia law” is such an absurdity
that —
The Ormuz, the first Orient liner arriving here under
the new contract, reports that white labor is unsatisfac-
tory. The purser and another officer spent hours at
Adelaide Police Court, yesterday, in connection with
drunken firemen. It is stated that the engine-room was
often left in a disgraceful condition.
Then follow weird particulars, in an English
paper, of the utter worthlessness of the Englishman
when compared with the Lascar. It would be an
amazing thing for Englishmen to gloat over even
if it were true ; but, seeing that none of the
Ormuz firemen landed at Adelaide, and that the
whole yarn regarding the Adelaide Police Court is
a lie, it is more amazing than ever. The Daily
Mail ought to re-christen itself the Dibty Fowl.
“ Squidge,” who, for all we know, is a
reliable person, tells this :
The other day, in Melbourne Town Hall, the Vic. Free-
masons held a big fella corroboree and installed Dr.
Balls-Headley as Boss Grand Panjandrum. At every
door of the Town Hall two old persons of antic disposi-
tion stood in dress-clothes (it was the middle of the after-
noon) and sheepskin sporrans, with great bands of silk
round their necks, and little tin things dangling from
the bands, and with two little curved swords in
their hands, to keep off intruders. They demanded
entry cards from all who tried to pass them, and those
who were not provided with tickets had one of the idiotic
little swords presented at their hearts. About half-past
3 Yes-No Reid hove in sight. He was arrayed in full
evening get-up, and carried in his hand a black case con-
containing his ham frill, sporran, and tin danglements.
He hoisted his protuberant fulness up the steps, and
arrived panting in front of the sentries. They barred the
way with their toy swords. Georgie grinned.
“ Ticket, please,” said one, in a cringing bow-to-the-
ground tone of voice.
Georgie grinned more than ever, and did a bit of panto-
mimic business with his arms.
“Oh yes, we don’t doubt that,” the sentry replied to
the pantomime. “But you cannot go in without a
ticket.”
“But, you see,” said Yes-No, “I’ve forgotten it. I
really must get in. They are expecting me. I promised
to come, and I am here. Really, you know, this is too
absurd. Of course, you know who I am. I am George
Reid—the Prime Minister, you know—and I’m”
George reeled off the story of Masonic things that he was,
“ Yes, yes,” said the phantom, firmly. “We know who
you are, Mr. Reid, and we’re sorry; but you can’t go ii..”
“But I must,” persisted Georgie.
“ You can’t,” echoed the apparitions sternly, and pre-
pared to repel the boarders with the tin toothpicks.
Yes-No argued some more, but it was no use. The
sentries could not be blandished, and George, his mous-
tache bristling with rage and his eye-glass flashing like a
heliograph gone mad, was not foolish enough to risk a
puncture. He turned on his heel and stamped off down
the steps again, muttering some awful Masonlo remarks.

Adelaide Advertiser’s London correspondent:—
I have it on the very best authority that there is a very
strong opinion in influential naval circles that it would
make for England’s naval efficiency and national safety to
treat the whole navy as a homogeneous force, in which
there should be no “China,” or “Mediterranean,” or
“ Australian ” squadrons, except for the purposes of dis-
tinction and convenience of nomenclature; that
all these squadrons should really form one force,
and should be concentrated or distributed and
ordered about as parts of one whole, and without
regard to the sectional considerations affecting
British North America, or South Africa or Australia.
I am speaking by the book when I say that the Austra-
lian precedent of an ear-marked squadron is condemned
at the Admiralty ; that it was accepted (largely through
Mr. Chamberlain’s influence when Colonial Secretary) for
“Imperial” reasons; that there are those at the Ad-
miralty who think that the policy to be put into
operation should be one navy for the whole Empire,
and pro rata contributions for its maintenance from
each and every part of the Empire; and that
failing the adoption of this policy the Australian contri-
bution should be permitted to lapse at the expiry of the
present agreement, and the Australian squadron re-incor-
porated with the rest of the navy when and wherever
required. I have not met one naval expert in England
whose opinions do not run more or less on these lines, hut
for State reasons they do not find publicity. Still it is as
well that you should know that this is the trend of
Admiralty opinion—that, curiously enough, they favor
the “Australian party s” policy of two or three years ago,
that Australia should keep its contribution and prom Ste
its own navy for local defence, and that the British navy
should be maintained for the defence of the Empire in
whole or in part.

f5 ° UCn Australian Trade . Home lYianu-
factures Holding Their Own,” the Freetrade
papers scare-head a recent report of the S. Aus.
Y. y, . . . .
Collector of Customs in their backstairs cam-
paign against higher duties. And then, in
type which isn’t of the scare-head variety, it is
remarked that in agricultural-implement making,
which was the State’s most important line, the
foreigner is knocking the Australian manufacturer
out under the tariff which G-. Reid whittled away;
that the whisky-distilling business has been simi-
larly knocked out; that the foreign importations
0 f hats and caps have increased ; that the foreign
flannels, flannel shoddy and woollens jumped from
£154,000 to £179,000; that foreign boots and shoes
are coming in at a greater rate than before; and
that local cigar manufacturers “ complain that it is
impossible to compete with the imported article
under the present tariff. ” Still, according to the
lying scare-head, “home manufactures are holding
their own”! But even if they were, what a
miserable business it would be—just to stagnate,
to petrify, to remain where we were, to be
stationary, in short, to “hold our own!” The
Yankee doesn’t want to hold his own ; he wants
to hold the earth.

WHEN doctors differ to whom should the siok man turn? Not to his friends or
relatives. They are good enough for sympathy. But sympathy won t oure
disease. It won’t even palliate it. It may only tend to produce and maintain
melancholia, and melancholia feeds disease.
The sick man should turn to the Specialist who is a dootor in Men’s Diseases. All
doctors are not Specialists, anymore than a rifle is a gun. They belong to the same class,
but are essentially different. A man suffering from Rupture should consult a Specialist in
that disease, as a man with diseased eyes should consult an eye doctor.
Talking about Specialists, one is at once reminded of the great skill of the Medical
Experts of

The little Australian who can’t notice a
wedding-ring on his mother’s finger has nearly
three chances of dying within his first year to the
ether little Australian’s one. Last year, in
N.S.W., 74 of every 1000 “ legitimates” died ; of
; ae other sort, 192 per 1000. And yet the little
Australian is the most desirable immigrant Aus-
• ra lia can have; and the chances are that the
little Australian who doesn’t pass through the
wedding-ring would make just as good and
vigorous an Australian as the other. But still
Australia lets him die, what time it listens to
Bruce Smith pleading for the admission of Yet
Sing and Chudder Bhong.
Curious how Britain lags behind in some
things. Our old friend Hopetoun, now Secretary
for Scotland, lately received a deputation from the
Scotch Society of Agriculture, which protested
against the new Bill for requiring vehicles to carry
lights at night. Hopetoun assured the bucolics
that they needn’t worry—the Bill couldn’t possibly
become law. And apparently it won’t, so carts
and waggons will continue to crawl in thick dark-
ness all over Scotland, and smash up any lighter
traffic that comes along. A few of the English
counties have accepted the reform, but only as a
result of independent action by their county
counoils.

A letter from Timaru (Maoriland) :
I know a man who always takes his washing to Ohow
laundrymen, and who is always boasting how superior
they are to the white wash-lady. The other day he was
called upon by a policeman, who asked him if he had ever
had any white shirts stolen. He said he hadn’t. “ Well,"
said the man in blue, “we ran in some Chows on Sunday
night, and one had a shirt on with your name on it."
Then the patron of the Chow launder hurried to the
police station and rescued his property. He found a
number of well-known citizens similarly employed.
Violent inquiry revealed that when a yellow person wants
to do the dude, he never buys the boiled garment of
civilisation. He simply goes to a Chow washhouse and
borrows a white man’s shirt for a small gratuity.
“ It is notorious that men grow more
rapidly tired of aotresses and the professionally
light-hearted—whether they follow them or marry
them—than they ever do of women who lead
quiet lives.”—John Oliveb Hobbes.
“ M.G.” : Brown, next door to me, owned
a “dorg”—a mongrel of an Irish terrier. The
derned animal yelped all day and wouldn’t even
leave the neighboring cats in peace at night. I
did most of my writing at home, and with the
noise of my kids and the dog next door I could
scarcely hear myself compose my literary master-
pieces. I could see no way of getting rid of the
youngsters, but was determined to “do away”
with Brown’s yelping animal. Asked Brown how
much he would take for it. He said “ two quid.”
It was scarcely worth registration fee, but I bought
it at his price. That night the dog and I took a
trip across the harbor in the ferry, and when
about half-way across there was one dog less, and
Brown’s animal was no more. A couple of days
later I came home at dusk and heard a noise like
the howl of a hungry dingo, or the wail of a book-
maker’s ghost. “Struth!” I said—“what’s
that?” “That’s Brown’s new dog!” my wife
answered.

Proserpine (N.Q.) Guardian appeared, the other Satur-
day printed on heavy, dark-brown wrapping paper-
tftfee shades a darker brown than Proserpine s own
Tommy Tanna ; and this was the leading article :
“ The fates have been treating us to more than our share
of bad luck this week. First our editor, who is also
comp., etc., gets the dengue and has to retire to bed.
Then we find our supply of paper has been left in Mackay ,
therefore this issue is only being produced with the kind
assistance of friends. What will happen with the next
issue, time alone will tell. But we hope fate will be more
kind by then.”
And it would be a scurvy fate if it wasn’t. A battler who
nuts his editor and staff to bed, and then finds his paper-
store empty, and still brings out the weekly issue,
deserves three columns of new advts. and a rush of fresh
subscribers.

The Inky Way.
John Norton issues his Maoriland paper at Welling-
ton. As the violent little gentleman With the bald
head hinted lately that, being in Maoriland, he supposed
he must do as Maorilanders do, it is inferred that the
paper will boil like the local geyser and blfcw up like the
local volcano.
* • • *
Mr. J. 0. L. Fitzpatrick, who succeeds Mr. R. J. Baker
on the Molong Argos, has a definite idea of what a
country newspaper should be. “ Leave the city papers
to create national feeling ; limit yourself to looking after
local feeling.” That was the history of his career on the
Hawkesbury, and the result was financially good.
From the journalist’s standpoint, Vandemonia is the
Slough of Despond. Salaries are generally most pitiful.
The leading paper expects to get first-class reporters for
£3 a week, and pays some members of its staff much
less. Another daily pays its editor £3 aweek, and works
him hard ; and the offices are notorious for their meanness
in the matter of expenses.
* * * *
Vic. Labor caucus recently spent some hours in discuss-
ing the formation of a Labor daily, towards which they
have secured £7OOO. Much more than that sum is re-
quired before any serious start should be made ; and, in
the undertaking, the Laborites should remember that,
most of all, they have to fear themselves. The public
asks first for a paper which gives it news, and news served
up brightly, and with a due sense of proportion. The
sentiments professed in the leading columns are only a
secondary consideration with most readers, and, though
a citizen be a most sincere believer in every plank of the
Labor platform, he will cease subscribing to the paper if
he finds that the Argus or Age ‘ ‘ scoops ” his favorite organ
several times a week. Good news means good men, and these
are not to be obtained without money. The ranks of
Labor are full of persons with the journalistic itch, who
imagine that because, with plenty of time and plenty of
paper, they can turn out an eloquent diatribe against
the Tory, they are qualified to take a place on a news-
paper. If the Labor paper is formed, these men will
rush for the billets and feel they have been treated most
treacherously if they do not get them. If the manage-
ment has any sense they won’t get them. It will pay the
Labor paper to buy up the best men from the administra-
tive staffs of existing papers, together with their best
reporters ; and that means giving them better pay than
Toryism now gives them. One of the worst points about
all labor organisations is their meanness in the matter of
wao-es. It will be a very difficult matter to convince the
directorate of the new paper that its star reporters
should get quite as much as a member of the Australian
Parliament, but if they don’t acquire that conviction they
will get no star reporters. If the expected happens, and
ex-members of the Trades Hall Council are put on to do
the reporting, the paper will be bor.i dead. There is no-
thing that the journalist—Democratic or Tory
resents more than an attempt to bring down
wages. The reporters of the two existing papers
will take good care that the cheap new reporter gets left,
and everybody familiar with press work knows that a
combination of two against one in news-getting means
the defeat of the minority almost every time. The
directorate also needs to take aback seat in the adminis-
tration of the paper. A good man should be secured as
editor, with a five years’ engagement. He should be given
the platform of the paper, and, provided he keeps within
its lines, he should be given an absolutely free hand as to
his methods and management. There are great possi-
bilities before a well-managed Labor paper. It would
pay some unprejudiced, smart capitalist with business
instincts to start one for his own profit, and denounce
himself and all his friends, and his ways, and his friends’
ways, in its columns ; but the outlook of any paper with
a host of managers is not bright, and when those
managers are leading members of a political party, it
almost become! gloomy-
“Viking”: For the information of the illiterate
(including editors and others), I would like to say
that it is a very improper thing to take an adjective
derived from a proper noun and to attempt, by adding
“ er ” to such adjective, to make another proper noun
having precisely the same meaning as the first. It is also
a waste of ink. It would indeed be a sad sight for a
Briton to see a Britisher helping a Japaneser to smash a
Russianer because the latter had hurt a Chineser s feel-
ings by aiding a Germaner to take a Portuguese-sneezer s
man-o’-war from a Turkisher. Such a proceeding would
rouse a Mexican Greaser to fury.
• * » *
In the Contemporary Review Alfred Kinnear arrives at
the conclusion that the verbatim Parliamentary report in
the daily papers is a thing of the past. British Parlia-
mentary debates are no longer interesting enough to con-
stitute the best feature of a newspaper, or to justify, as
they onee did, an ornamental expenditure. John Bull s
taste has changed ; the long, close-set speech has ceased
to pay. Even the Parliamentary “ front-rankers have
to be cut ruthlessly nowadays. Five years ago, Rosebery
was worth a full report; he is now saleable to the Press
as a whole at from half to three-quarter column. Cham-
berlain, worth at the outset of his fiscal propaganda two
columns out of every three delivered, has depreciated
greatly. Balfour, once worth a full report, now “goes
at a Times “ turn-over,” say a column and a little bit.
Asquith and Campbell-Bannerman are saleable best on the
half-column scale. Within the last two years four first-
class London morning dailies have discharged their Par-
liamentary reporters and handed the business over to the
Press Agencies, and now the only City dailies which have
special Gallery staffs are the Times, Daily Telegraph and
Morning Post. Provincial dailies represented in the
Gallery are the Scotsman, Glasgow Herald, Freeman’s
Journal and Manchester Guardian.
BALLAD OF THE EVENING TOAD.
POLITICAL POINTS
’Twas the Evening Toad newspaper, and its editor-in-
charge
Was a milingtary person whose views were very large.
The office called him “ kernel ” —and ’twas said that
sudden sack
Would descend upon the party who guffawed behind his
back 1
’Twas a milingtary p.’.per—it was extra-fond of blood,
And its “leaders” smote the Russians with a deadly
evening thud.
But the war had slumped to zero—aye, the war was
baggy-kneed,
And the editor was troubled at the dreadful dearth of
screed.
So he placed a hostile sentry with a cannon at his door,
And deliberated fiercely for an hour or two or more I
In the silence of the sanctum he evolved a mighty
scheme,
And its glories rose before him like the glories of a
dream.
Twas the Evening Toad newspaper, and it went to press
that eve
And told the sodden city news that made the city grieve.
But the most depressing feature fulminated by its pens
Was a mighty Referendum on the Ten Best Citizens 1
But the kernel stuck his chest out, and informed his
aide-de-cong
That the paper’s circulation would be multiplied ere
long.
And the aide-de-cong assented; then went home and told
his Pa,
That the Toad was even sillier than most newspapers
are !
But his Pa’s opinions differed —as they generally do—
For he basked in church each Sunday next a knighted
person’s pew.
So the ballot first unfolded by the milingtary gent
Emanated from that father... and it mentioned Thomas
Bent;
’Twas the Evening Toad newspaper, and it sowed the
plague broad-cast —
Aye, the voting choked the office, and the postmen stood
aghast 1
Dear Suburbia’s sacred smugness, dear defaulting
gentlemen—
Every bankrupt straightway labored with a damp andl
reverent pen!
Every Bumble fell instanter—on his waistcoat lay
a-sprawl—
Yea, the Nicest folk joined issue in a simultaneous crawl.
Lo, the holy rushed with coppers to obtain their Evening
Toads;
Whilst the folks who led the voting bulged with pride in
their abodes.
One could read it in their faces as they paced along the
way;
And the House of Madden simpered, and the House of
Bent was gay 1
But the proudest man among them was the Man Behind
the Pen —
Aye, the milingtary Person in his milingtary den.
’Twas the Evening Toad newspaper, and it told in tram
and train
The Best Citizen was leading, and all rivalry was vain.
But somehow it failed to tell us—and the reason no man
kens —
How one vulgar ballot-paper named the Ten Worst
Citizens 1
Yea, the milingtary kernel read that list With secret woe,
And the tears of indignation must have t rickled down his
“mo.”
For the names upon that ballot were identical, in part,
With the bigwigs who were riding in the Referendum
Cart.
Give their names?—the law of libel firmly blocks the
blessed road,
Or I’d print the list omitted by the dismal Evening Toad 1

A Cure for fliddle Age
(By “Old Boy.”)
It always comes as a shock to a man or woman to hear that they are bein'? classed as middle-aged
people. Youth merges so gradually into age that one is apt to consider oneself young for years after
those who are really young have placed us upon the shelf of age. In all but a small minority of cases,
however, when a person gets to be over forty, a subtle diminution of energy makes itself felt.
Perhaps, if we are lucky, we have nothing special in the way of pain to growl about, but, even in
such cases, there is an indefinable something which insistently brings to our knowledge the fact that
youth has fled. In a larger proportion of instances the advance of middle age is heralded in a more
emphatic manner. The joints become stiff, twinges of gout are felt, rheumatic and neuralgic pains
be,dn to trouble, there is a sharp pain in the back when rising from a stooping posture, a good meal is
eaten with a miserable consciousness that presently we shall regret the eating, and numerous
weaknesses and aches all tend to advise us that we must begin to take more care of ourselves.
Although there is, unfortunately, no means of preventing the advance of age, it is possible to
ward off its effects for an indefinite period if adequate care is taken to see that the eliminating organs,
the kidneys and the liver, are in good working order, because the encroachments of age are ever
gradual and painless when the kidneys and liver are properly performing their functions.
The kidneys of the average person filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine
every day. In this quantity of urine should be dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains
in weight of uric acid, and other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to
nearly an ounce. If the kidneys are working freely and healthily, all this solid matter leaves the body
dissolved in the urine, but if, through weakness or disease, the kidneys are unable to do their work-
properly, a quantity of these urinary substances remains in the blood and flows through the veins,.
contaminating the whole system. Then we suffer from some form of uric poisoning such as;
Rheumatism, Gout, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Persistent Headache, Neuralgia, _ Gravel, Stone,,
and Bladder Troubles. A simple test to make as to whether the kidneys are healthy is to place some:
urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and let it stand until next morning.
If it is then cloudy, shows a sediment like brickdust, is of an unnatural colour, or has particles floating
about in it, the kidneys are weak or diseased, and steps must immediately be taken to restore their
vigour, or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some of the many maifestations of uric poisoning will result.
The Liver is an automatic chemical laboratory. In the liver various substances are actually made
from the blood. Two or three pounds of bile are thus made by the liver every day. The liver takes
sugar from the blood, converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it
Ito the blood, as the latter may require enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble,
into urea, which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with the blood corpuscles which have
ived their life and are useful no longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer from some
form of biliary poisoning such as Indigestion, Biliousness, Anaemia, Jaundice, Sick Headache, General
Debility, and Blood Disorders.
So intimate is the relation between the work done by the kidneys, and that done by the liver,
that where there is any failure on the part of the kidneys the liver becomes affected in sympathy and
vice versa. It was the realisation of the importance of this close union of the labor of those vital
organs which resulted in the discovery of the medicine now known throughout the vvorld as Warner’s
Safe Cure. Certain medical men, knowing what a boon it would be to humanity if some medicine
could be found which would act specifically on both the kidneys and liver, devoted themselves to an
exhaustive search for such a medium, and their devotion was eventually rewarded by their success in
compounding a medicine which possesses the required quality in the fullest degree. Warner’s Safe
Cure exhibits a marvellous healing action in all cases of functional or chronic disease of the kidneys
and liver, and restoring them, as it is able to do, to health and activity, it, of necessity, cures all com-
plaints due to the retention in the system of urinary and biliary poisons. A vigorous action of the
kidneys and liver naturally eliminates the poisons, and troubles due to the presence of the poisons
cease. Cures effected by Warner’s Safe Cure are permanent simply because they are natural. [ll].

Brogan’s Flat.
POLITICAL POINTS
IT wasn’t quite the spot an average person
would select
To study classic architecture in,
For every man in Brogan’s Flat was his own
architect,
And materialised his plans in slabs and tin.
The sun-baked street was crooked, and the houses
wide apart,
With a patch of bush between them now and
then;
But the township didn’t pride itself on literature
or art —
All its pride was concentrated on its men.
As I don’t hail from Brogan’s Flat, it don’t apply
to me;
So you can take my word this tale is true.
The people there possessed in a superlative degree
The faculty to go for all they knew.
If a Brogan’s Flat inhabitant played cards, he
played to win ;
And he always held the joker and the bower ;
But if he didn’t play at cards, then cards were
deadly sin
And a shocking waste of energy and power.
The good men there were extra good, the bad ones
very bad,
The tall men always grew extremely tall;
And every man was jolly or superlatively sad,
And they always smoked or never smoked at all.
The pious men at Brogan’s Flat spent all their
time in prayer,
The gossips spent the whole of theirs in chat;
And the teamsters on the Namoi never quite knew
how to swear
Till they’d had a final course at Brogan’s Flat.
Bill Peters kept the smithy at the west end of the
town,
And he couldn’t fit a shoe or weld a' link,
Fix a bolt for Jimmy Thompson or a nut for
Johnny Brown,
But his shibboleth was “ Comanavadrink.”
Then sounds of noisy revelry would float from
Murphy’s bar,
And be followed by a simple old refrain,
The melodies were various, but as constant as a
star
Was the chorus —it was “ Fillemupagain.”
Beyond the mill, where Brogan’s Flat extended to
the east,
Stood the forge of Jock McTavish, who would
swear
That the man who touched strong drink was more
degraded than a beast,
And that wine was a delusion and a snare.
While McTavish made a horse-shoe ’twas his
custom to inveigh
Against publicans and whisky, rum and gin,
And his preaching was as powerful while rivetting
a stay
As the blows with which he drove the rivets in.
While Peters sang a roaring song in praise of rosy
wine,
And filled himself with whisky from the still,
McTavish used to preach in praise of abstinence
divine
And sing of sparkling water from the rill.
If a stranger came to Brogan’s Flat, and entered
from the west,
The place, seemed full of drunkenness and mirth.
While the stranger from the eastward always
thought the town was blessed,
And imagined it the soberest spot on earth.
Now, although the temperance. question was
decided east and west,
In the middle Brogan’s Fiat was still in doubt;
And the middle folks consulted and decided it
was best
Just to meet and thrash the troubled problem out
So they called a public meeting and arranged for a
debate,
And the subject for discussion it was this:
“Does the cup what’s said to cheer us, and what
don’t inebriate,
Or does whisky, give the greatest share of bliss ?”
Jock McTavish championed temperance with elo-
quence and skill,
And his reasons were convincing and profound ;
While Peters fought for alcohol and whisky from
the still,
And his arguments were logical and sound.
And every point McTavish made was driven home
with force
Like his mighty sledge, his blows were hard and
true;
While the arguments that Peters used, he clinched
without remorse,
As he clinched the nails when nailing on a shoe.
When the chairman put the question, he an-
nounced, ’mid great applause,
That the voting had resulted in a tie ;
And he couldn’t give a casting vote on either side,
because
He was at a loss to give a reason why.
He had listened to the various points the speakers
dwelt upon,
And he felt convinced the meeting would agree
That the intellectual arguments delivered pro and
con.
Were just about as equal as could be.
When Peters rose to move a vote of thanks unto
the chair,
He said, “ I’d like to make this one remark—
The arguments of friend McTavish lead me to
declare
That I’ve always been a-gropin’ in the dark.
I’ve been like one benighted, and I’m certain
as can be
That I’ve always been mistaken heretofore ;
From to-night I’ll stick to w T ater, varied now and
then with tea,
And I’ll never touch the whisky any more.”
Then McTavish rose to second it, and briefly he
announced,
“ Mr. Peters has converted me outright;
And, although my love for water has been ardent
and pronounced,
I will never touch cold water from to-night.
I feel to Mr. William Peters gratitude intense,
Words fail me to express the thoughts I think,
But I can’t resist his eloquence, his wit, and
common sense —
In short, my brethren, comanavadrink.”
McTavish now sings roaring songs in praise of
rosy wine,
And fills himself with whisky from the still;
While Peters preaches temperance and abstinence
divine,
And sings of sparkling water from the rill.
Now, the stranger entering Brogan’s Flat, and
coming from the west,
Never hears the sounds of revelry and mirth ;
And the stranger from the eastward never marks
the place as blest,
For he thinks he’s struck the drunkest spot on
earth.
N.S.W.
THOS. E. SPENCER.
The Snake.
POLITICAL POINTS
MOTHER was making cakes. She
prided herself on her cakes, and Ted and
I encouraged her.
This morning she had insisted upon having a
big pile of wood built up in a corner of the kitchen,
which was large and roomy, though Ted had
strongly advised her against it, saying that he had
heard of lots of snakes having been caught in
town.
But she was obstinate, and had her way.
And now she was busy baking. Ted was
digging a gutter near the back door, “in case it
might rain any time within the next six months,”
he said, stopping now and again to rest on his
spade and address me where I sat in the shade
near him, sewing. Then we were suddenly and
rudely interrupted.
Poor old mother, in turning to place a shovel-
ful of red-hot coals under the colonial oven, had
seen, coiled up in the middle of the brick floor, and
between her and the closed door, a long reddish-
brown snake ; and then she had impulsively placed
the shovelful of glowing coals right in the centre
of the coils.
For a moment nothing happened,, and then the
snake broke into an ecstasy of calisthenics. He
was drunk with excitement and hot coal. Up
and down! up and down! between ceiling and
floor, like lightning went that snake. Mother
screamed and sprang on to the table behind her.
The snake landed beside her—whipping, whirling,
twisting. Mother shrieked and made a terrific
spring for the dresser, emitting a series of terrified
yells as she leaped backwards and forwards, for
the reptile was going strong, always appearing to
be in mid-air.
It was at this stage that Ted reached the door,
spade in hand, and opened it, only to close it
again promptly for a moment.
“ What’s the matter ?” I cried, breathless.
“ Matter ! matter ! ” he gasped ; “ the kitchen’s
full o’ dam whip snakes ! ” and then he rushed
in.
I stood at the door crying helplessly, and wring-
ing my hands, and listening to mother, who had
stopped screaming, and was repeating aloud, with
great fervor, something that sounded like the
litany for the dead.
Ted was making hits, mostly futile, at the flying
snake, but at last he managed to cut it in
two on the window-sill, and half of it dashed at
the wood heap, while the other half danced in
mid-air. I ran in. “Oh, mother! mother! are
you hurt! ” I said.
“She’s bit all over,” Ted moaned, with the
certainty of despair, smashing the snake’s head
viciously with the spade.
But she was not bitten at all, only terribly
frightened; and after what Ted called a good
stiff “pull” at his flask (he was a handyman to
have about the house in ease of emergency) she
recovered sufficiently to be able to tell us all about
it.
“When I sprang from the corner of the table
to this spot ”
“Y’ never jumped from that corner to here /”
ejaculated Ted, interrupting.
“I did, indeed,” she assured him.
“ Fetch the tape measure,” Ted cried, excitedly.
“It’s eight-foot-six if it’s an inch; an’ fer a
woman o’ fifty, an’ in skirts, it’s a world’s record ! ”
He eyed her doubtfully.
“I s’pose y’ couldn’t do it again, ’ithout th’
snake, an’ for a wager ? ” he said, regretfully.
She replied indignantly that indeed she could
not ! And I giggled. And then mother said
that she had always lived honestly, and tried to
do her duty, and she didn’t know why she had
been afflicted with children who were idiots.
PAUL PEARSON.
Pirates.
POLITICAL POINTS
MICKO (he might have been Johno—
but Johnos usually wear wide-brimmed
straw hats and smoke cigarettes by in-
stalments ; my friend wore a “ monty ” and
smoked to a finish) sat beside me on the rear of
the tram and opened the conversation. “Them
coves is the greatest pirates on the road.”
I looked up in time to get the full force of his
exhalation of cigarette smoke. I cleared the
atmosphere with a few waves of my newspaper
and fixed him with an inquiring gaze. Micko
repeated the observation. Three girls, and the
driver of the tram immediately behind ours, were
laughing and chatting together.
“ Uniform ?” I suggested.
“No, it ain’t altogether the uniform that does
it; but, yer see, girls ’as a better chance o’ gettin’
on a conversation with them tram-drivers than
with or’n’ary blokes like you ’n’ me. Oh, I tell
yer they’re somefin’ orful; I nearly lorst the best
little girl I ever ’ad over one o’ them pirates. We
wuz goin’ to a picnic down the ’arbor, an’ sat on
the front o’ the tram. After a minute or two my
little lot starts cliippin’ to the driver, ’n’ ’im to
’er —never noticin' Pilgarlic ’ere. Well, just as
they wuz gettin’ dead sweet an’ arrangin’ for a
meet, ’e runs ’is date-box over a dawg, ’n’ in the
confusion I ’ops me clinah off, ’n’ we finishes the
journey to the Quay on foot. My oath ! A Act
oughter be passed to stop girls from ridin’ on the
front of trams.
“ Never ’ave a girl wot lives in a suburb where
the tram runs ter,” continued Micko. “Now I
know that girl wots doin’ a tale to that driver, ’n’
I know she spends more in traveilin’ on trams than
she does in clothes, ’n’ that’s sayin’ a ’ell ov a lot.
But ’e’s on’y foolin’ ’er. You see ’im when ’e gets
out ter the suburb where ’e’s goin’ ter. Ye’ll see
a publican’s daughter standin’ at ther door waitin’
ter see ’im pass. On towards the Quay y’ll see ’im
tip ’is cardboard to two dressmakers wot ’ll be
’angin’ outer a winder, also waitin’ t’ see ’im pass.
Blessed if I know.”
Micko shook his head ruefully, and was silent.
After a while: “I reckon we’ll soon be a nation
o’ tram guards ” —this with some conviction. “ I
suppose ye’ve read in them novelettes where the
rich girl clears out with ’er old man’s coachman ?
Well, they don’t do that ’ere, coz there ain’t many
coachies—but there’s tram guards.” And Micko
rattled the laugh of a man with a chest complaint.
“ I knew of a girl—’er old man ’ad stuff—wot
cleared out with a tram driver.”
“And they were married, and are now living
happily ? ”
Micko sneered at my innocerlce. “ Nao ! She’s
workin’ in a laundry now—’n’ Vs in gaol.”
I asked him if it occurred to him that he was
slandering a respectable body of men. “ What! ”
he shouted. “ I suppose you're one o’ the cows,
off dooty.” I told him I was merely the solicitor
for the Train Employees’ Union (or something
equally untrue) and he replied that he didn’t care
if I was dead.
Then he rose, looked down on me contemptu-
ously, and moved towards the side of the car.
Shortly afterwards there was a prolonged grating
sound, a yelling and shouting, a clanging of bells.
Micko dropped lightly off the tram and stood
gazing bade at the rapidly increasing crowd. The
tram following mine had struck a loaded meat
waggon and the roadway looked like a dynamited
abattoirs. As my car moved off again Micko
waved his hat triumphantly to me, and jeeringly
pointed to the pale and trembling driver, who was
doing his best to explain to a policeman that it
wasn’t his fault.
ROBERT HAP.
