Waiting and Watching.
POLITICAL POINTS
THE sun’s behind the mountain, ’tween two
trees,
Which, joining arms, lean inwards where
the pass is,
And over-arch the gateway. Lo ! the breeze
Bears gently through, a bright’ning cloud whose
brass is
Transmuted to red gold, ’mid crimson lees
Of sunset-wine, that sinks in gorgeous masses
Behind the chasm ; beyond those sombre ranges
Merged in the after-glow of serial changes.
Against this patch of glory slowly fading,
A dark’ning speck, a blur —I know not what,
That seems to grow, advancing and invading.
The mists of eventide that shroud the spot
As all the changeful colors in their shading
Turn fainter, paler . . . dreamier is the blot.
Yet still ’tis there, as you’ll best understand
Who’ve sought a phantom-sail or “No Man’s
Land.”
You who have sickened in the shimmering heat,
Mid-ocean in some lost and leaky boat,
No sail in sight, no humans left to eat.
And you alone, of all the world, afloat;
Your shadeless canvas still; all slack the sheet,
With not a mark upon the void to note,
Till Fancy, flying to the soul’s relief,
Points to some spectral note and chases Grief.
A sail! A sail! You hug Hope closer yet.
'Tis distant. . . What of that ? You know ’tis
there.
In yon horizon’s ring your gem is set;
Long leagues beyond your reach, howe’er you
dare.
Yet still you hope. She smiles; you cease to fret
Till Night shrouds Man and Ocean in despair,
And the pale stars all mock your silent sorrow.
No lantern cheers your night, no sail your morrow.
And you, lost wanderer in those far-bush mazes,
Where every tree and track shows out the same,
A brazen sky o’erhead; a glare that dazes;
Where fallen timber’s strown all smirched with
flame
And weird, impenetrably-tangled spaces
With vines at Death-grips in a Dantesque dream, —
Yet still you hope, as ’tween the twisted branches
The setting sun his fire-shafts towards you
launches.
“Dear Life’s” you’re one concern on land, on
ocean,
Though there be kindred lives that centre there.
’Tis selfish mostly, yet the strained emotion
Which racks your Soul, let other lips declare,
Although you’ve got a hazy sort of notion
That were you lost, some lives, indeed, were bare.
Ah yes ! one never knows until ’tis tested,
The fulcrum firm wherein Hope’s lever rested.
You, who have watched and waited, sister mine,
The coming of a lover, husband, son,
With all the love affection makes divine—
Love that on earth but barely is begun ;
Yet glad as sunbeams, strong as Chian wine,
More excellent than gold, where fairly won;
You know the tricks an o’er-wrought fancy plays,
Which leads one roundabout, not out the maze.
Where trees are beasts Hyrcanian; columned rocks
Take shapes of man; and, fleeting clouds out-
sailing
On airs of saffron borne, are sheep in flocks
That Jason might have shorn, ere daylight failing
Turns gold to grey, and frenzied fancy mocks
With sprawl of dragons on a field that’s paling;
* Or glimpse of ghostly navies, that before
, Ploughed seas of sapphire toward an unknown
shore.
; Shore that one knows so well, yet never reaches,
With terraced heights and labyrinths and domes,
"Long promontories, lakes, and golden beaches,
< O’er which a flood of molten topaz foams,
Whose dreamy loveliness too sweet for speech is.
In ever-changing beauty as one roams,
Too oft, alas ! the back-ground that our sorrows
Relieve against in form that glory borrows.
: Some shape illusive, there, against the gold,
: Stands out, arresting sight and other senses,
.And, ah 1 how longs the gazer to enfold
The form as yet past all those trees and fences.
The joy once more that faithful heart to fold
Close o’er its throbbing mate, Love’s recom-
pense is—
A dream-act Love’s much given to rehearsing,
TTill frowning Fate stalks in, the plan reversing.
vio.
G. G. McO.
Down the Range.
POLITICAL POINTS
THE “bike” was a German one, and
thoroughly brittle and unreliable. The
chain had snapped twice when climbing
!the long hill, and the truth of what old Billson,
my trainer, had said, was revealed to me. Said
Billson: “ Climb the Horse Range, an’ if ther’s
anything wrang wi’ yer heart or yer machine,
’t’ner Range ’ll find it out! ” The heart seemed
all right, but by the time I reached the top of the
Range my temper was broken right up and the
machine revealed as a fraud and a delusion. I
got off half-way through the defile, and after drink-
ing the little rock fountain dry, sat for a while to
get breath and objurgate the man who first
imported made-in-Germany bicycles. Then I
once more mounted and started down the hill.
It was a wild night. Great masses of scurrying
clouds made fitful darkness, and a nor’wester
howled between the rocks and sighed through the
long golden tussock grass on the hillside. The
Range had a bad reputation; the last day the
coach had run, loaded with notables, to see the
opening of the railway, the brake had given way
about half-way down, just where the sharp right-
angle turn is, and eight horses and 23 human
beings had gone over the precipice. There had
been other minor accidents, involving only two or
three broken limbs at a time, and, consequently,
not considered of much importance. But I
thought of them all, as, with my feet on the front
rests to keep the fore-wheel steady, I came down
the mountain side, with the wind bowling behind
and the moon making fitful light that blurred that
from the lamp.
Suddenly, the machine sprang downwards like
a stone from a sling, and the pressure on the
brake-lever ceased. With a chill feeling down
the spine I clawed for the whirling pedals with
my feet, for the brake had given way at the most
critical part of the descent.
At last I caught one and back-pedalled vigor-
ously, and breathed a sigh of relief as the machine,
for the fraction of a second, slackened speed.
Then—“twang, ping, clang’’—and I heard the
broken chain coil up round the back stanchion as
the machine threw itself promiscuously forward
towards the Coachman’s Leap.
There is nothing in this world gives such a vivid
impression of irresponsible force as a runaway bike
with a broken chain and a powerless brake. The
moon rode out through an archipelago of opalescent
clouds, and her light streamed down on the
waving tussocks on the hillside, the dark, yawn-
ing chasm on the right, the yellow road in
front, and a figure that waddled uncertainly from
left to right, picking his way among the stones
and ruts. For the moment the thought of the
Coachman’s Leap was pushed out of my mind by
this more imminent danger. The track was a
narrow one, and the figure moved from side to
side in an erratic way that made a collision almost
certain. I rang my bell, shouted and howled at
him, and next instant he was right in front of me.
I swung to pass between him and the cliff, but
the left handle hit him in the side. As he shot
behind me I recovered my balance and clung
frantically to the machine, which was now going
furiously down a road as straight as a billiard cue.
Streaming moonlight all around me, howling
wind behind me, and beneath a machine that stag-
gered and trembled with its own incredible speed.
Somewhere thereabouts was the chance of a
plunge of 300 feet, smash, on to black basaltic rock,
but the exultation of wild, bird-like flight robbed it
of all its terrors. The wind cut the eyes like a
knife, breathing was difficult, and the moonlight
went streaming past in a golden flood.
* * * *
I found myself on a level road. The speed had
slackened and the landscape had broken through
the moonlight that had ceased to stream, and I
fell splash into a little pool of water that filled a
depression.
I gathered my mislaid identity after a while.
My head was sore and my limbs were shaking,
and I felt a weak, fluttery feeling half way down
my chest that made it hard to breathe. I remem-
bered what had happened, and looked up the ex-
panse of waving tussocks shimmering goldenly in
the soft Maoriland moonlight. Somewhere up
that hill the traveller was lying injured or
dead, and I set my teeth and dragged my
self up to look for him. It took me an hour to
reach the spot where the figure had been struck.
I recognised it by the narrowness of the road, due
to the falling away of the hillside. But there was
no sign of the traveller. I shouted and cooeed, but
the only answer was the roar of the wind through
the rocky defile above, and the sigh and whisper
of the tussocks. Then I knew I had hurled him
over the precipice. I got down on my hands and
knees, and foolishly tried to peep into the black-
ness below. I saw where the steel rim had
scratched the hard clay, just one irieh from the
edge. Then I choked. For my handle, striking
the poor traveller, had swung me round the sharp,
almost right-angled turn of the Coachman’s Leap.
If it had not struck him I would never have turned
that corner.
I walked down the hill to where I had left the
machine, fixed the chain with my last splicing
link, and rode the crippled implement into the
township. Arrived there I told the police sergeant
what had happened, and went on to the hotel to
stay myself with stimulants.
They were playing cards, and remarked that
they did not envy the sergeant his search up the
range. In between whiles they tried to cheer me
by pointing out that I was in no way responsible
for the accident, and flippantly observing that if a
man hadn’t the decency to get out of the way of
another man on a runaway bicycle, what happened
served him right.
Just as I was beginning to see it the parlor door
opened feebly, and a white-faced rustic entered.
He dropped on the sofa with a heavy groan and
gasped for whisky. The landlord ran for the
bottle, and, when the white-faced man had taken
a long drink, someone remarked: “ You look as
though you’d seen a ghost, Sandy !”
Said the rustic scornfully: “Mon, I’m aston-
ished at ye. D’ye think a mere ghost wad upset
a mon tae this extant.” (Raising his voice) “I
ha’ seen auld Horney himsel’ this verra nicht.
An’ more, I hae such a flick in the side frae his
tail that I’ll hae the mark thereof till I dee.”
Mr. Crump, the town clerk, looked up, placed
his English terrier on the table, adjusted his
glasses, shifted his pipe to the other side of his
face, and spoke severely :
“ Do you seriously mean to asseverate, Mr.
Macpherson, that you have seen a demon to-
night ? ”
“I doo,” said Macpherson, otherwise known
as Sandy ; “an’ I tell ye I hae such a flick frae
his tail in ma side that tuk the breath clean out o’
me.”
“Describe the apparition,” said Mr. Crump,
loftily.
“I was cornin’ doon the Horse Range, and ma
horse bolted when I got off at the top tae hae
a drink frae the little fountain, and I was footin’
it hame. Ye ken that place whaur the coach
lep-ped ower is haunted. Sure enough, just when
I gets there, I hears the most awfu’ howlin’ and
shriekin’ that ever mortal ears heard. While I
was wonderin’ what tae dae, he shot pass me wi’
an awfu’ screech, an’, as he went, he flicked me
wi’ his tail and sent me bang against the cliff. I
climbed oop tha side, and came doon by a goat-
track ; but when I look-ed oop he was just disap-
pearing doon the road.”
“ Disappearing down the road ! And what was
his appearance—how did he look?” asked Mr.
Crump.
Macpherson drank some more whisky, and
cleared his throat. “He was like a mon frae the
top o’ his head tae tha tail o’ his coat, but he’d
neither arms nor legs—just a heed an’ a body, an’
he was sliding doon a moonbeam in a most ter-
reefic manner, without effort—just sliding doon
it as a callant might slide doon a stair banister,
but at a terreefic speed. Mon, it was an awesome
sight.”
“ But his countenance —his face; what was his
face like ? ” asked Mr. Crump eagerly.
“It was an awfu’face —a wicked face, and his
eyes were like twa red lamps, an’ he smelt some-
thin’ awfu’ o’ sulphur.”
“ Had he any horns ?” asked Crump, cheerfully.
“He had twa. He was an awfu’ villain tae look
at an’ he was screamin’ like a thousan’ lost souls.”
I couldn’t stand the description any longer, so
I put my hat on top of the alleged horns and left.
The sergeant returned at 4 a.m., cold, worn-out,
bruised, and angry. The things he said about me
couldn’t have been more disrespectful if my head
had been decked with sixteen horns.
C. A. JEFFBIES.
